The Red Page
The Rymbel
When the rymbel was introduced to Aus-
tralasia she was an awkward and stuttering
idiot: this page crudely attempted to teach
her manners, and then left her to the tender
mercies of the bards. Each had his own idea
or the education she required; and back she
comes to tnis page decked out with improve-
ments and clad in fanciful costume. But
some of the garments into which she has
Deen put are grotesque, and some of her
company manners are hardly nice. So, from
the errors in ta-ste discernible in her educa-
tion, this page has formulated its own rules
qt conduct and dress, and by these rules the
An-trahan rymbel will be judged.
at- i irS J?’ t * le l 3Un hiust be a complete one;
lviick Sweeney ends one stanza with compos-
ing, and begins the next with compos mentis.
Next, it seems an error in taste not to keep
to the same length of pun. Thus, if you
u Bll \ a one-syllable keyword, you
should keep to the one-syllable miscue all
thiough. And if you prefer a three-syllable
pun you should not relax into puns of lesser
length. Thus Roy R. 11-ede begins with
na Allows, cleverly, with idolatry—
1, Doll, a tree, and horizon with Ho! rise
on; and T. V. Tierney, beginning with
nanayr hand, progresses to circumvent — -Sir,
come vent. But these are counsels of per-
fection, and in the final judgment they have
had to go by the board. Incidentally, it
may be mentioned that the best metre for
the rymbel is the trochaic—a fact discovered
a minority of the competitors. The
chief fault of the entries, however, was the
frequent excessively wide jump in thought
between the end of one stanza and the begin-
ning of the next, suggesting the idea that
i? e JP un keen dragged in at the expense
of the orderly progression of the rymbeller’s
intent. A good sample of this faking is to be
found in A. E. B. Wood’s rymbel:—
Miss Olga, fair of face and slim,
Is Puritanic purely,
But Yunga, plain I must confess,
Has not her sister’s narrowness,
Nor does site smile demurely.
De Muir Leigh is their pater. He
(Through Olga’s intervention)
Most luckily perchanced to see
Our greeting osculatory,
And asked me my intention.
(In “ ’tention” there’s a warning sound
To anyone who’s martial.)
I deemed it meet to be discreet,
And told him to his daughter sweet
Miss Yur.ga I was partial.
“Pa shall not sanction it,” exclaimed
A voice I knew, maternal,
“I have in view a Christian true,
And will not let her marry you.
Your cheek is most infernal.”
“In fern a lady could be dressed.
’Tis cheap, and maybe healthy.
But as for me,” sneered Olga Leigh,
“I much prefer costumery.”
(In satire she is wealthy.)
“Well, Theo, Yunga’s yours,” said Pa,
“So hustle. Find a domice.
She’ll get a wee annuity
If from this awful wowsery
You’ll save her.” Did I promise t
And John Patrick spoils a natty rymbel
by suddenly introducing George I. Moffit in
response to the damsel’s remark, “ George,
I’m off it!"
Obviously, however, there must be some
slight titillation of the reader’s attention by
the keyword of each stanza : the problem of
rymbelling is so to adjust the continuity of
the narrative with the cleverness of the pun
that the reader’s mind is first brought up
short and then finds its suspense neatly re-
solved without a break in the meaning. In
miniature, it is the old principle of dramatic
suspense; you wonder, worry, and, then,
looking back, suddenly see.
Another fault of immature rymbellers is
their lack of climax. The thing should end
with a snap: the last word should not be
too clearly foreseen : it should be used in a
different, and unexpected connotation to that
anticipated. The perfect rymbel begins quite
unobtrusively, and ends with an unsuspected
emphasis upon the modest keyword or
words. The perfect rymbel has not yet been
written. Many excellent rymbles, among
them entries from Syd. C., Rudolph Aug-
stein, “Last Hope” (a particularly neat, but
too slight, effort), and Leslie Lee, fail in
cleverness by employing easy, single-syllable
puns. The last entrant, too, drops from
poetry to prose in his weak ending, as does
Duncan Browne, in a quotable rymbel of
serious intent;—•
SUNRISE—AN OTWAY MORNING.
Prone, without form, beneath the pregnant night,
The earth like some great huddled sleeper lies
Outstretched towards the trembling edge of light,
Where newborn day has peeped with blue-grey
eyes
To see the twinkling heavens on him fawn.
Faun and satyr in this place must be
Lurking amid the bushland’s deeper shade,
While loud-voiced Triton from the restless sea
Makes more profound the silence of the glade,
As winds his awful roaring to it bear.
Bare trees against the sun’s light first throw out
Their fire-scorched limbs as if to hold the night’s
Dark tresses to their nakedness; about
Our feet the virgin younger growth delights
The eye and hides full many a time-scarred butt.
But, with a choir of magpie minstrelsy
That pours in liquid n*tes the bushland’s charm,
The laggard sun low down against the sea
Bestains the earth with gold—in quick alarm
Are fled the faun and satyr from the scene.
Seen through the mist the Bay of Islands gleams
In curves of glistening white about the base
Of age-old cliffs ; and ever upward streams
The long diapasons of waves that chase
Each other through each rocky eddying strait.
Straight from the tents across the dew-hushed sod
To where night’s shadows in a pool lie hid,
Go men with drowsy greetings; and a nod
From one hight “Boss” warns me I must be rid
Of dreams to which all idle men are prone.
It will be seen that the rymbel can be
serious when she chooses. Here are two
of high merit:—
A RYMBEL OF FAIRYLAND.
•' Caro will not come, my little one,
Within this dim, enchanted glade;
Care does not mar the fairies’ fun,
Who dance their dance from sun to sun
In moonlight robes arrayed.
Rayed from, the centre fairy-man,
See how light they trip and twirl!
How ■ like some filmy, fluttering fan,
They flash and fade, now bright, now wan—*
Each foot a twinkling pearl.
Purling along, the brook a-near
Keeps time for their dance in minor key,
As it flows to its rest in the silent mere;
List to the fairy music, dear;
Sit here and watch with me.
Methinks they see thee, little one—
They know thy shining eyes and hair; .
They call thee now to join their fun,
And dance with them from sun to sun,
In this Land of Never-Oare.
L. SPARROW.
Rime not, for pity’s sake, for hereabout,
One distant day, the voice of Poesy
A dulcet incantation sang to me;
And song’s great spirit came dull care to rout,
And brought the promise of a guinea fee.
Feed not on promises thy Muse may bring;
It brought me this, my fancy’s painted doll,
The Rymbel, or a punster’s fun’ral knoll.
I felt like, as I cursed the endless thing,
A mariner marooned on some atoll.
A tolerant and peaceful attitude
The thing maintained throughout my hot tirade
Until I tried with my slow brain to trade,
And whisky brought; then it became quite rude;
And there against my lack of nous inveighed.
Invaded by the thing’s invective vile,
Around, about me reeled this den of mine,
Each book danced as a dancing columbine;
Then, as its frenzy passed, with quiet guile
It sought to me this mission to assign :
“As, signed by thee, a duplicate of me
Roams o’er the earth,” it said, “Where song is not,
All other poems brand as rhythmic rot,
Till I alone rule song’s Posterity;
This task, O dreamer, thee I now allot.”
A lot of Rymbels on. the shelf now lie;
Those harlequins of sweet song’s pantomime
Rejected were by editors each time
I sent them in. Sometimes I think they sigh
And see Parnassus ’neath the ages’ rime.
L. A. ROYLETT.
But the rymbel is happiest when she is
most irresponsible. Some excellent samples,
worth space—and space-rates :
STRIKE!
Strike me pink! But this is rather rough.
I really feel that I should make a row
About this rattly, rambling, Rymbel rot.
If it’s the latest fad, it’s rather hot—
It is, in fact, a perfect, blanky cow.
Cowards, of course, would take it lying down;
But I’m a poet, so I swears and storms,
And hereby hurls a crimson curse or two,
Because they’ve gone and started something new,
Just when I thought I’d mastered all the forms.
Forms may be mostly made to sit upon;
But I’ve a feeling that I’d rather like
To come down on this latest wilh a flop.
I think I’ll shut up my poetic shop,
And, as a poet, go right out on strike!
MOANA.
BULLETINITIS.
Sally waits for me I know,
The right I’ll not dispute her.
I want to read the new Red Page,
I cannot help my Sally’s rage,
Such treatment ne’er will suit her.
Souter’s dainty semi-nudes.
Each week they grow the sweeter.
Eardley Turner’s subtle wit,
“Kodak’s” sporty little bit,
And Hervey’s fervid metre.
Meet her now I know I should,
Waits she at the G.P.O.
The “Snake” has me within his toils,
At “Bellerive’s” tale my., mind recoils,
Oh, that blood-stained stiletto!
Still let, oh, let me tarry,
And with “Vandorian” dally.
’Tis joy enough for me to read,
To muse upon her witty screed,
And con each brilliant sally.
L. W.N.
I am sending on a rymbel, it’s a rymbel. writ at
random,
And I thought the same was worthless till I read it
to my mate,
Then Bill the bullock-driver
Said it’s worth a blanky fiver,
Being a South Australian rymbel manufactured in
the State.
Statements often differ, so I’m doubting Bill’s
opinion.
He’s a good enough Australian, but his intellect is
weak,
So it goes for your inspection,
Your approval or rejection
You’ll decide if it’s a rymbel or a literary freak.
Frequently I’ve noticed, in your correspondence
columns,
Some awful verses written for the Sydney Bulletin,
And it won’t be unexpected,
If this rymbel is rejected,
And you tell me that my effort was a literary sin.
Sincerely I might mention, in conclusion, I’m a
smithy,
And hammer glowing iron for a living—by the bye
My muse was going splendid,
Till my rhyming mood was ended,
By a glowing cinder landing in the comer of my
eye.
BELTANA.
NYMPHS IN PASSING.
Rosie, you’re a little thorn,
That to me has rife sent;
’Tis my heart that you have torn,
’Tis my love you make forlorn
With your charm translucent.
Loo sent love beams with her eyes—
Yours past hers are rated ;
Why are yours twin mysteries,
Well’d in depth that chill implies?
Be love-animated.
Annie mated long ago,—■
Fat for her was fated;
Now she queens it in a show,
And she Still does fatter grow—•
To flesh dedicated.
Dead-eye Kate hid from the trade,
Bars no more may hold her;
Oft, full, oft that defty maid
-Pull’d me foam that winked and played
At the tales I’ve told her.
Toll’d her bell! Rose ne’er pulls,
Beer that makes me prosy ;
, Rosie's charm nor Time annuls.
She can ne’er be with the culis—
Time with her is rosy !
L. T. V.
Tlie last rymbel is delightful, but the puns
are a little forced.

The guinea is divided between Nella
Campbell, 6t> Corlette-street, Newcastle,
N.S.Avh, and Ethel Pawfiey, “Ocean View,”
Ocean Beach, Manly, both rymbels making
exceptions in the suggested rule of keeping
the puns to the same number of syllables,
but both compensating for this defect by the
cleverness with which the continuity in de-
velopment is kept by the rambling rebellious
pun-linlcs, and the cleverness with which the
innocent links are disguised. ' In the
present state of development of the
hardly-acclimatised rymbel these two com-
petitors score simply by their treat-
ment of the subject matter. Again,
the technically correct examples sent in are
in themselves not sufficiently interesting to
win the prize. Finally, the perfect rymbel
is not yet written. A further opportunity
may yet be given by means of another com-
petition.
RYMBEL.
BY NELLA CAMPBELL.
A handy cap adorned her head —
Her head was very fair —
And Oh! She was demurely meek!
But when I kissed her blushing cheek,
She begged me to forbear.
“For. hear in mind,”" she coyly said,
“If George comes out, of course
There'll be a fearful row, for he,
In matters oscillatory,
Your conduct can’t indorse!’’
Indoors she went, and left me there,
Abashed at this mishap.
I deemed her single, fancy-free—
Of course her husband’s sure to be —
The brute! —a handicap.
RYMBEL.
BY ETHEL PAWLEY.
The poets write that silver streams
Go down to kiss the sea.
That fairy ships a-load ivith dreams —
With elfin spoil each shallop teems —
With Ocean haste to he.
Two beastly, sluggish creeks I know;
/ But ne'er a silver rill.
Those very creeks a hard would show
To he brave streams that swiftly flow
With waters clear a-fill.
A Philistine I e’en must he,
With dull and holder eyes,
Which in a creek no magic see
While hards in words of ecstasy
Do it immortalise.
Immortal eyes do hards possess
That they see visions bright?
To never see the streams grow less,
Nor forest lose her vernal dress,
Are these the poet’s right?
Fiction
“There are still spacious compounds on
this planet where perfectly natural sexual
relations are permitted to be mentioned with-
out unnatural blushes” ; and apparently Bur-
mah is one of them. For The Repentance of
Destiny, a romance of Anglo-Burman life, by
Shway Dinga (Duckworth, per George Rob-
ertson), is astonishingly frank in these mat-
ters. The author of Wholly Without Morals
evidently knows the Burman and his British
ruler at first hand ; but this story of the con-
tact of East and West suffers because the
author takes for granted the reader’s know-
ledge of Burmah under British rule. It is a
brutal story of the apparently usual concu-
binage practised by the bright young men
who come from Oxford to rule Burmah; and
the Burmese “marriage” of a beautiful young
Burman woman with a British official is des-
cribed with much interesting detail. But
this Englishman possesses a sort of con-
science, and desires to give up his “wife” in
the interests of his career and of the Impe-
rialism he professes. The consequence is
tragedy, in which is involved a Burmese
whose ambition draws him from a monas-
tery to enter the Government service. This
admirably-drawn character affords us a valu-
able insight into the mind of the Burman.
Bocal color, however, is splashed o* so
thickly that the story behind it becomes occa-
sionally indistinct. The impression, how-
ever, emerges that, if not now, at least re-
cently, the behavior of the ruling race in
Burmah does not make for the respect of the
conquered Burman. “The key to our Impe-
perial greatness,” the hero says, “seems to lie
in the fact that we keep a conquered race at
arm’s length.... But I cannot and will not
believe that our Imperial greatness lies in
cutting the Burman by day, "whilst it’s per-
missible to consort with his daughter by
night.” As always, a conqueror becomes the
slave of the race he has conquered.
£
James Stephens had better be careful.
The author of The Charwoman's Daughter
and of The Crock of Gold is one of our few
humorists; and in Here are Ladies . (Mac-
millan) he is beginning to cheapen and dilute
his fantastic wit. Yet Stephens is still
Irish, and. apparently intends to remain so.
In his latest book, a collection of short
stories and sketches, there is invention, fan-
tasy and line humor—in streaks, but the
streakiuess is compensated for by the pre-
sence of anew element —his brilliant powers
of observation. His “Three Heavy Hus-
bands,” his “Three Women who Wept,” and
his “Three Young Wives” are delightful
studies in modern realism lit by freakish
fantasy. The wife of one of the heavy hus-
bands was resentful, for though “sometimes
he petted her, and always he was polite, he
had not told her that her eyes were the jnost
wonderful and inspiring orbs into which a
tired man could look. He never said that
there would not be much to choose between
good and evil if lie lost her. He never said
that one touch of her lips would electrify a
paralytic into an acrobat. He never swore
that he would commit suicide and dive to
deep perdition if she threw him over—none
of these things. It is possible that she did
not wish him to say or do such extrava-
gances, but he had not played the game, and,
knowing that something was badly wrong,
she nursed a grievance, that horrid foster-
ling.” And one feels a certain sympathy
with the heavy husbahd who was confronted
with the problem: "After one has married a
lady, what does one say to her?” And one
is left pleasantly wondering at the dilemma
of. the third heavy husband, who refused any
longer to wait up' for his irresponsible wife—
she had gone out at night alone against his
express commands.
“In his bedroom he did not trouble to get
a light. He undressed in a bitterly savage
mood; and rolled into bed, only to jumn out
again in sudden terror, for there was some-
one in it. It was his wife. He lay down
with a hazy, half-mad mind. Had he
wronged her? Was she more amenable than
he had fancied? She had not gone out at
all—or, had she gone out, sneaked in again
by the back door and crept noiselessly to
b‘>d? He fell asleep on the tattered fringe
of a debate—Had he wronged her? or had she
diddled him again?”
For James Stephens in his new mood, let
us consider this passage from “A Glass of
Beer” : “There were faces also to be looked
at; round, chubby faces wherefrom the eyes
of oxen stared in slow, involved rumination.
Long faces that were keener than hatchets
and as cruel. Faces that pretended to be
scornful, and were only piteous. .. .Raddled
faces with heavy eyes and rouged lips.
Ragged lips that had been chewed by every
mad dog in the world. What lips there were
everywhere! Bright scarlet splashes in
dead-white face?. Thin red gashes that
suggested rat-traps instead of kisses. Bul-
bous. flabby lips that would wobble and
shiver if attention failed them. Lips of a
horrid fascination that one looked at and
hated and ran tq.”
His garrulous old man who discourses on
all things in “There is a ■ Tavern in the
Town” shows us Stephens pumping up his
humor with difficulty. His old man is fool-
ish. forced and dull. But everything can be
forgiven the author for his tremendous story
of the adventures of a threepenny-bit in
Hell. Brien O’Brien, into whose dead hand
the cash had been placed, dropped that three-
penny-bit on his way to Hell, and there
discovered his loss. Despite the
and ingenious efforts of the Chief Tormen-
tor, O’Brien kept shouting his complaint:
“I have been robbed in Heaven !” All Hell
took up his demand for justice, and business
was suspended. The trouble extended to
Heaven, and Rhadamanthus was compelled
to sit up and take notice. As it happened, a
young seraph had found the money, and re-
fused to give it up on the ground that find-
ings were keepings. The perplexed Rhada-
mon thus flung the seraph into space, and
O’Brien after him, and ultimately they both
fell to earth, where apparently their adven-
tures are only about to begin. This story is
a masterpiece in fantastic invention.

still. We call them “Disease Germs,” and they also

intestines, fever with its hallucinations, or biliousness with
its aches and pains, is the result.

School Teachers Who Suffer from
Insomnia and Nervous Breakdown.
The following is a testimony to the nerve-restoring powers of
Clements Tonic. One of the most brain-racking of duties is that of
public-school teaching—correctly training “the young idea how to
shoot,” making pupils perfect by examination time —these great de-
partmental worries make nervous breakdown and insomnia common
ailments amongst the rank and file of public-school teachers. This
letter is from a lady who has been a brilliant example of her profes-
sion, having held the following high appointments in the Educational
Department of New South Wales :
First Assistant Fort Street Model School for Girls.
Mistress, East St. Leonards School. _
Assistant Mistress, Cleveland Street Public School.
Mistress, West Redfern Public School.
This testimony must appeal with commanding force to each mem-
ber of the Educational Department, showing what a nerve-soothing,
insomnia-killing, and health-restoring medicine they have in Clements
Tonic. All they need do is to try and persevere with it. The rest
with health are certain. Miss CARDWELL writes from her residence,
“Coree,” 45 Booth Street, Annandale, 27/8/’l3, thus
CLEMENTS TONIC LTD.
“I have great pleasure in giving my testimony to the benefits derived
from Clements Tonic as a builder up when in a run-down condition.
“When teaching in the State Schools of New South Wales, I many
times resorted to its use, taking one to three bottles in course.
“The ever-constant strain on the nerves of a Public School Teacher
soon brings on a low state of the whole system. In my case it was over-
strain in teaching, and mental worry through family bereavements. I had
been taking in*escribed medicines, but seeing Clements Tonic advertised, I
tried one small bottle, and feeling so much benefit from its use, got the
larger size. It was such a restorative that, when I felt the least run-down,
lat once sent for Clements Tonic, and it never failed me. No househol
should he without it, or fail to use it in any case of over-strain, for 1 fee
sure it will have the desired effect.”
I am, Sirs, gratefully yours,
A. M. CARDWELL.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Cafe De Fairfax.
It nestles ’neath the lofty walls
Of Mister James’ rag,
A refuge that at midnight calls
Late pilgrims of the jag;
Though others to its canvas halls
Belated hunger drag.
When ev’ry pub looks dark and dead,
And weirdly rings a laugh,
ThroiTgh empty streets are laggards led
To where its aproned staff
Serve out stewed steak, with scones or bread,
Tongues, smalls, or half-and-half.
Let others seek their costly grill,
But give me, flush or broke,
The place that lacks all pomp and frill,
Where waiting is a joke;
The servitors are Jack and Bill;
Each customer’s a bloke.
The oil-light limns a motley crew,
Drunk, toiler, broke and swell,
Assembled with a will to chew —
And this is safe to tell:
Regarding what they come to do
They do it very well.
Yet there is comradeship innate
Amongst them all. They mix,
And strangers know as “Jack” or “Mate,”
And points of law they fix,
While loudly blends the clattered plate
With sport or politics.
Above the Hekald’s dingy wall
Frowns sternly, many-eyed ;
I fancy Mister James and all
Must view with angry pride
That little democratic stall
That nestles by their side.
And at the stall I eat my pies,
And gulp my coffee down,
And wink towards the gloomy eyes
That from that building frown,
Where lives, so stodgy, dull and wise,
The staidest rag in town.
Let others oyst in gilded room,
Or in a hansom hie,
Where menus, bands and blossoms bloom ;
For me a humble pie
Beneath the disapproving gloom
Of Mister James’s eye!
N.S.W.
HAMER.
Riches.
He had not wherewithal to buy
The little things the many need ;
And yet his seeming was a lie,
For he, I swear, was rich indeed.
His clothes were worn and soiled and old,
And held by many a patch and stitch;
But, tell me, is it only gold
That makes a mortal truly rich?
He knew no banquet-board, and still
(I vow by all the Muses nine!)
He fared with them who feast their fill
On better things than’cakes and wine.
When, plumed and painted overmuch,
Gay dames and damsels drew aside,
Lest he should taint them with a touch,
He saw a Vision starry-eyed.
That Vision nought on earth could soil —
No tress of gold make grey, I vow,
Nor Master Time with wrinkles spoil
The marble smoothness of its brow.
His deeds were few; yet this I know —
He dreamt of high and noble things ;
And dreaming thus, with eyes aglow,
Took rank above the rank of kings.
Beside him, night and noon, there stood
Two shapes that he alone could see,
And each was fair, and both were good,
And one was known as Memory.
And one was that fair Queen of Dreams
Who lifts the soul on spirit-wings,
And lights the sordid world with gleams
That men have called imaginings.
Though he had not the gold to buy
The little things the many need,
Was not his seeming all a lie?
And was he aught but rich indeed?
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
In a Garden.
All blossoms flung their scent with wanton
hands
Into the tresses of the summer night,
And, as the breezes blew the perfumed
strands
Across her face, she swooned for sheer de-
light.
The moonlight lay as softly on the flowers
As tender kisses dropped on sleeping eyes,
And cast a spell upon the fleeting hours,
Turning our laughter ere we knew to sighs.
A cactus flaming from its foil of green
With hidden daggers pierced the warm dusk
through;
And slender aloes thrust their spines be-
tween
The fronds the jacaranda crowned with blue.
And all the while the unseen crickets sent
Their little passionate chirpings from the
grass,
We fancied that the hooded night had bent
To whisper to us e’er she let us pass.
So we who did not know her mystic power,
Nor dreamed yet of the simple web she wove,
Made foolish vows we could not keep an
hour,
Trembled and laughed and kissed—and
called it love !
N.S.W.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to Tin: Bulletin ivould oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
verttsemenfs, etc.) should in no case be addnssed to
the hdilor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. "The Editor, Tim Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for "copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

THURSDAY, FEBRUARY 12, 1914.
NO LAND AVAILABLE.
On a certain recent day a man connected
with The Bulletin was afflicted by the
possession of a relative —a thing which is
ftot uncommon and may happen to anybody,
therefore no blame can be ascribed to either
party. The relative had heard vaguely of
the Northern Territory and was seized with
a desire to settle there, so he wrote to his
connection in this office asking that infor-
mation about land and other matters might
be dug up. His thirst for knowledge was
sent on to the External Affairs Department
—it is characteristic of the great lack of
official interest in the Territory that it isn’t
treated as part of Australia but is lumped
in with External concerns, as if it were
Chili or Timbwctoo. The Department
nroved to be courteous and gentlemanly, but
low-spirited. It replied as follows' (the
capitals are furnished in this office) :
There is at present NO agricultural or pastoral
LAND AVAILABLE for selection in the Territory.
Before such land can be thrown ooen for selection
it has to be classified by the Land Board at Darwin.
As soon as this is done, particulars will be pub-
lished in the Commonwealth Gazette specifying
the areas available and the conditions attaching to
their occupancy.
Some grazing and mixed farming blocks are about
to be made available for selection in the Territory
and a copy of the pamphlet containing full informa-
tion respecting water supply, area and locality of
the blocks, class of soil, etc., will be sent to vou as
soon as available.
This paper doesn’t propose to throw any
special clod at the Cook Government, which
hasn’t been long in office and is in such a
dreadful minority when the two Houses are
added up that it can do nothing except on
sufferance. Besides, the Cook Government
has intellectual limitations which must be
allowed for. Nor does it throw the whole
clod at any one Government in particular—
not even the one, whichever it was, that
pushed the Territory into the outer darkness
of External Affairs —for they seem to have
all inherited the same state of mind, and
the same indifference to the larger concerns
of Australia. Between the politicians who
didn’t want the Territory taken over because
it had no immediate votes of any importance,
and those who, for the same reason, don’t
really care a hang what becomes of it now
that it is taken over, there is little to
choose. Between Ministers who did badly
and Ministers who did nothing there is also
little to choose. This paper wouldn’t even
unduly rush the Laud Board at Darwin lest
that body should get up a perspiration. Nor
would it treat that institution, or any other
institution, with contumely except on good
evidence. Besides, in the absence of any
definite instructions as to the terms on which
land is to be let or sold, the Land Board at
Darwin can’t do much, But attention is
hereby drawn to the fact that in all
the great Federal Possession—the place
which is constantly being blamed because
it doesn’t attract settlors—there is NO .
LAND AVAILABLE. Yet the Common-
wealth’s own expert agent, after inspecting
the new Canaan from Dan to Beersheba,
reported that though Queensland is the
best of the old States, the Territory excels
even Queensland in the splendor of its pos-
sibilities.
The Fisher Government attempted to do
two things of imoortance for its big pro-
perty—to provide it with a land policy and
a railwav policy. Both were bad failures,
because both were devised with very little
view to the interests of the Possession, and
with very much view to the possibilities of
retaining votes in the older States. The
land scheme was an advertisement of the
tremendous resolve of the Ministry to adhere
to its policy of public ownership and its
desire to secure the unearned increment for
the nation, but it was calculated to make the
average land-seeker gather up his carpet-bag
and flee from the premises for bis life. Land
was to be Government leasehold only, which
was reasonable enough. The rent was to be
subject to periodical re-appraisements, which
was also reasonable enough provided the
re-appraisements weren’t so frequent as to
destroy all fixity of tenure. But, in addi-
tion, the. rent was to be liable to be raised
at odd times—apparently any day or every
week or twelve times a year—if new
Government works had been constructed,
which, in the opinion of the landlord (the
Government) enhanced the value of the
nropertv. There was, of course, to be some
form of anpeal—possibly an expensive and
long-winded one —to some Court appointed
by the landlord, against the landlord’s
opinion on the subject of rent. But appeals
are uncertain things, and if there was no
real limit to the possible number of rent
increases there would also be no real limit
to the possible number of appeals, and in a
very big country the Court is liable to be a
long way off and costly to reach. In tiny
case, the new settler wants to be nlougliing
and fencing— not appealing; and nobody
is likelv to invest money in improvements
on land which is held on something very
like a day-to-day tenure. Even the rich
landlords, whose exactions are impoverish-
ing Western Victoria, aren’t quite so ram-
pant in their terms, because they don’t want
to terrify to death the fowl that lays the
golden egg. In the desire to carry the policy
of public land-ownership to the last ex-
tremity it was proposed to give the tenant
no solid rights whatsoever, and possibly a
great many wrongs. It was sought to com-
pletely abolish the pleasant gamble—even
the smallest semblance of a gamble—in the
unearned increment, and seeing that the
Labor party is as fond as anybody else of
backing a horse, or taking a ticket in Tatt.’S,
or running an Art Union, the attempt was
excessive. No State Government exacts
such terms as regards its public lands, nor
is Daceyville or the resumed Rocks property
in Sydney managed on these principles.
Unfortunately the Territory, by reason of
its lack of voting power, is the place that
doesn’t matter. It served as an advertise-
ment of the Government’s devotion to the
principle of public ownership of land, and
when the scheme fell through because it
became too obviously impossible, no other
scheme was promptly substituted. The mat-
ter was hung up, and the Land Board was
left in futile perspiration at Darwin, and
there is NO LAND AVAILABLE. Yet, on
second thoughts, something has been done.
A small, lonesome farming settlement on the
Daly River is occasionally heard of. It is
said to be in process of abandonment, be-
cause it lies far out from anywhere, and the
same old curse of isolation is thick upon it.
Then it was decided by the Fisher
Government —and agreed by the Cook
Government —to extend the scrap of railway
a scrap further southward to the Katherine
River. Construction hasn’t really begun
yet, and nobody shows much enthusiasm
about the beginning of it. The prospect ex-
cites such skimpy hopes in the bosom of
External Affairs that nobody has been moved
to cut up great areas for settlement on the
strength of it. The work won’t connect any
section of the Territory with any other State
or with any centre which has population to
spare. It won’t reduce the isolation one
iota or. bring one of the existing big markets
one mile nearer. And thg scheme which
commences the railway at the north instead
of the south is a horrible advertisement for
the Territory in another sense. It concen-
trates all the new nopulation. the new
workers and the new Government activities
on the hot, muggv coastal strip. It helps
to fix the silly old delusion that the hot.
musrv.v* coastal strip and not the great, cool
tableland is the Territory, and thus it plays
into the hands of the nigger-labor push and
the anti-white Australia liars. Materials
for construction and supplies for the con-
structors are dear because they shamble to
the scene of action by a vast and costly
circuit. The employees are alleged to be
limp through being shot down suddenly in
the one part of the Territory where the
unacclimatised toiler is liable to feel limp
for three or four months of the year. These
people want extra pay for their limpness,
and labor troubles ensue. There is a great
increase in the volume of correspondence
setting forth that the grand old Territory
is no good, for the strangers fall easily into
the habit of assuming that the one hot corner
they have seen is the whole blessed place.
As usual, this splendid Federal asset —the
grandest property owned by any Government
on earth—having no voting power of its
own, is used as a mere pawn in the wretched
political game. It is the place that Cook
never heard of and the wilderness in which
Johnson sat on the alligator. The Federal
Government is pledged to finish the* line
from Port- Augusta to Darwin, but it is
strongly urged, by interested parties- to break
its pledge and make the connection with
Sydney and Brisbane instead. If it were to
start building in real earnest at the right
end. it must say whether it~is going to take
the line of honor or the track of political
expediency. In other words, it must commit
itself. Along one road it must risk losing
the support of Doodle, M.11.R. (S.A.), and
along the other it must take the chance of
making an implacable enemy of Boodle,
M.11.R. (N.S.W.), and Noodle, M.11.R.
(Queensland). The fragment of railway
which it is proposed to build is non-com-
mittal. It tends to show that the Territory
isn’t quite forgotten, but as it points no-
where in particular the work doesn’t
imply a declaration of war against Doodle,
who can’t be spared, nor is it a direct annoy-
ance to Boodle, who is essential, or Noodle,
who is liable to raise Sheol if things don’t go
his way. Even the seemingly-mild Poodle
down in Victoria, whose opinions are
divided as regards the route, and who may
really be a slumbering volcano, isn’t aroused.
* * * *
Meanwhile, as already mentioned, there
is NO LAND AVAILABLE. So far as is
known, there is no land policy available either,
As those in authority seem to have little br no
faith in their pretences and appear to regard
their schemes, not as devices for waking up
the Territory but rather as precautions
against waking up dangerous politicians, it
is possibly not worth while making land
available. For many years an isolated set-
tlement has been attempted in the one really
bad part of the Territory, and it failed be-
cause, while it was possible to get land that
was in direct connection with big centres of
population and big markets, the lonesome
place offered few attractions. The present
attempt is the same old attempt over again
on a slightlv more pretentious scale, but
with less heart and soul in the business.
There are more officials, but there seems to
be not so much sincerity. Still hope isu’t
wholly lost.. Out of the 523,620 square miles
—much of it splendid land —some areas may
be made available in course of time. The
Land Board is still struggling with the vast
orb of its fate at Darwin.
THE DOCTRINE OF NON-RESIST-
ANCE.
A certain, or uncertain, or hazy Quaker
named Brown has come to Australia. Ho
has mildly denounced this country’s very
humble Defence policy, and has risen, in
moderation and without blasphemy, to
preach the doctrine of humility and of tak-
ing: it lying down. The Sydney Telegraph,
which interviews all the passing cranks, gave
him some ispace, and the following is part of
the report:—
The London Quaker deputationist, Mr. Alfred 11.
Brown, believes that the Commonwealth has no need
to resort to arms to defend itself.
In the course of an interview yesterday, shortly
after his arrival in Sydney, he gave the following
illustration of the Quaker policy of defence: —
“You sometimes see a boy putting up his fists to
hit another boy. But the other boy makes no move-
ment. He only smiles. There is no fight. The
bellicose boy becomes ashamed of himself.”
In a word, the Quakers act on the proverb, “It
takes two to make a fight.” That the Quaker policy
of not being “one of the two” will prote effective in
all cases to prevent bloodshed may be doubted by
some people. The Quakers, however, have strong
testimony to support such a belief. First of all,
there was William Penn. He and his little colony
lived in the midst of the wild Red Indians for 60 or
70 years. The Indians were constantly at war with
other Indian tribes, but not a single Quaker ever
suffered injury at their hands. The reason was that
the Quakers lived with their doors wide open, and
they never carried arms. As soon as the Quaker
administration ceased in Pennsylvania, and men armed
themselves, the Indians began to slay the white man.
In the same fashion and for the same reasons the
Quakers passed through the Indian Mutiny and the
Irish Rebellion without a single Quaker being killed.
Recalling this testimony, Mr. Brown said: “The
fact that a man is known as a peaceable man consti-
tutes his surest defence. In the midst of the Indian
massacres the Quakers slept with their doors open,
and none of them were injured.”
Discussing the existing compulsory enlistment Act,
Mr. Brown strongly condemned it, not only because
it was directly opposed to his principles "of peace,
but also because compulsion would tend to destroy
the patriotism of the lads. He declared that this
compulsion to carry arms was causing about a dozen
Quaker families to leave Australia, and would have
the effect of checking immigration. He believes there
are about 1000 Quakers “on the books” in Australia,
and many others who emigrated from England and
have been “lost.”
* * * *
It is a pleasant theory on top, but rather
anti-Christian underneath. According to
the Quaker view that no person is
injured provided he maintains a peaceful
attitude, it is implied that the Saviour
wouldn’t have been crucified unless he had
hit Pilate in the eye, or been unduly aggres-
sive to the Scribes and Pharisees and the
priests. And by the Quaker doctrine, all
the saints and martyrs met with their woes
because they didn’t assume the proper non-
aggressive position. For, according to the
mental asnect of the Society, this is a good,
kind world in which no bad man smites any
good man unless the good man smites him
first. And as the good man never smites
the bad man either first or second, therefore
all good people are immune from wrong and
injustice, and only bad people are damaged
- —by other bad neople. In consequence
of this state of affairs soldiers are needless,
and police and magistrates and judges are
superfluous, and bolts and bars and locks
and safes and strong boxes are not required,
except to protect wicked characters who
hardly deserve protection. Ours is the best
of all possible globes, and its records of in-
sult and injury and torture and fraud and
rape and mutilation done on kindly, unoffend-
ing Early Christians and such-like are mere
inventions.
The Quakers’, own records are not pleasant
reading. In their early days, despite the
pleasant smile they gave to the persecutor,
they were lavishly hogged and imprisoned,
and had their goods confiscated, and their
ears cut off, and their tongues bored with hot
irons. When the pretty Quaker girl came
hack from seeing the Amalekite in office she
wasn’t good to look upon, and both her back
and her face were in disrepair. These things
might convince the average Quaker that the
doctrine of non-resistance was a success, but
people of sturdier build would prefer to be
rough and hostile to the fiogger and the
gaoler. Yet with all liis troubles the Quaker
had one streak of luck ; he usually hung out
his non-resistant banner in some land where
there were plenty of sturdy and probably
un-Christian fighting men to defend him
from foreign aggression. Only once in all
its long career did the broad-brimmed sect
pretend to found a Quaker State and to try
its doctrine of non-resistance and Christian
forbearance on a hostile race, and Pennsyl-
vania, despite all the bogus advertisement
that has been spent upon it, was something of
a joke. The early Swedish and Dutch settlers
and the non-Quaker English helped a lot to
save the pioneering Friends from destruc-
tion in the early days. But even William
Penn, the boss Quaker and the shining light
of the new Paradise, was driven, a very few
years after its establishment, to organise
some kind of a force to defend the borders.
In 1699, when the State had hardly cut its
first tooth, the leader was struggling with
the pirates who raided his kindly people and
took their girls and their goods. Not long
after, the Indians came along in dead earnest
and made ashes of a part of the
Quaker land, and Pennsylvania had to raise
a militia and build forts and vote money for
defence, and in other ways plug along god-
less paths of violence. And in 1764 a force,
partly Quaker and partly German, garrisoned
Philadelphia with the gun of wrath and the
pike that leadeth unto destruction, it being
a time of serious trouble when Susan and
Rachel and Ruth were threatened by
the Philistines, and verv angry and drunken
Philistines at that. Then the Scoto-Irish
settlers drove the Quakers from the control
of affairs, on the ground that their non-re-
sistance was a failure, while their resistance
was inefficient. They were accused of being
half-and-half, or shandygaff, or dog’s-nose, or
some other mixed drink. The slaughter of
white men was going strong in Pennsylvania,
and the Quaker G.overnment didn’t wholly
lie down and be humble, or wholly stand up
with arms in its hands and be arrogant.
It is quite possible that no Quakers were
killed in the Indian Mutiny, but then it is
also quite probable that there wasn’t a
single Quaker in the comparatively small
area affected by the Mutiny. And it is
equally possible that no Quakers were killed
in the Irish rebellion —presumably that of
1798 is meant. There were hardly any
Quakers in Ireland then, and the patriots of
’9B weren’t addicted to the slaying of non-
combatants. No more kindly crowd of
“rebels” ever infested the earth. The
Friends of to-day are a scanty folk.
They are said to number about 122,000,
so if they all failed to come to Aus-
tralia they would hardly be missed. About
100,000 are in the United States, and some-
where about 17,000 in Great Britain. In
Ireland, where a Quaker might get trodden
on by accident in a discussion between a
well-meaning Nationalist and a kindly
Orangemen, there are only about 2000. In
countries, however prosperous and well-
governed and well-defended, where there is
a chance for the Quaker to be a martyr by
refusing to do his term of military service,
there are very, very few of him. In coun-
tries where the Government is weak and
corrupt, and strong men fend for them-
selves, and the Quaker, unless he departs
from his creed altogether, has to quell the
brigand or the savage by kindness and the
present of a sample of American
tinned goods and a Testament, there
are practically none of him. He locks his
door at night like any other citizen, and
sticks close to the protection of an ungodly
police and a swashbuckler soldiery. He
isn’t by any means a pioneer, his one futile
attempt in that line having been enough for
him, and more than enough, and a great deal
too much. If the Quaker would go to the
front and prove his doctrines by actual ex-
periment upon rude, coarse tribes, the world
might take him seriously, but his clamorous
adherence to the peaceful back row of things
prevents his aosDel having a fair chance.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Hulumn and his Tory Government.
One of the happy jests of the Political
Labor League Conference recently held at
Sydney was the presentation by Minister
Trifling or Trivial or Trefle of the report
of the Agricultural Committee. Clause IV.
read in this fashion: —
The opening of new ports and the connecting of
border railway* to give producers the easiest access to
markets.
This is affecting, and it would be impressive
if the gang of Tory backsliders and dodgers
and city and suburban landlords which calls
itself a Labor Government was a little less
glaring in its political hypocrisy, and _ a
little less subservient to the congestion
policy of the Sydney conservative dailies,
and to the interests of the Sydney property-
owners. But its record is against it. It
once professed to have a great scheme on
hand for connecting Western Iliverina with
its nearest port, Melbourne; hut when the
scheme was rejected it hardly made a. decent
pretence of being annoyed. It is taking the
North Coast railway round in a great, ex-
pensive circuit to avoid touching Port
Stephens, and giving that splendid harbor a
chance to do some business. And as the
railway could hardly be extended to Bom-
bala without an agitation arising for a fur-
ther extension to Twofold Bay, the promised
Bombala railway did a sort of perish at


Nimmitabel, and has bee^
tes'ToS shuffling Two 'things that take the
Se are the Holman Government s pre-
tendedcraving for decentralisation and its
alleged hunger for'State ownership of land.
The° idea of creating State-owned cities on
places like Port Stephens or Jervis Bay or
Twofold Bay, and thus competing against
the private landlords of Sydney, makes it so
very cold about the feet that it won t even
mention the dreadful possibility.
Deadlock.
Tasmania’s little isle, having acquired an
Assembly in which the Government follow-
ing numbers 15 and the Opposition the same,
Party Government is “up against it.” The
Government has to find a Speaker, and it
the parties sit tight, and no Oppositionist
can be corrupted, it must find a Speaker out
of its own ranks. In a House where the
numbers are equally divided and the party
system is strictly adhered to, this act would
put it in a minority of one, and being
promptly licked and shot out in consequence
of its minority, the other lot would naturally
come in. And unless the other lot could buy
over an opponent, it would also come in
simply to go out again. In fact, under the
party system, if honestly conducted, the only
crowd that could possibly be in power would
be the crowd that was out of office, and the
crowd that was out of office couldn’t get into
office without losing office. It is a silly sort
of business, by which a presumably intelli-
gent community ties itself into a hard knot,
and becomes incapable, by its own conduct,
of conducting its own business. If the busi-
ness consisted simply of passing laws and
doing acts of administration for the good of
the people, and everybody could vote on each
Bill or clause or proposal solely in what he
believed to be the interests of the people,
nrobably things would go smoothly enough.
There would generally be a definite majority
for or avainst. and the Speaker’s casting
vote would settle the matter if it couldn’t be
settled otherwise. But, in the great majority
of cases, and no matter how members
labelled themselves, the Speaker’s casting
vote wouldn’t be required. Unfortunately,
under the system which makes a Minister—
unlike a scavenger or cook or clerk or civil
servant —liable to be sacked without notice
and on general principles, the case doesn’t
turn on the merits of the measures put before
the House. It depends on the Vote of
Censure, and the Motion of Censure, when
reduced to plain language, reads thus :
That the Honorable Members, now in possession
of the Loaves and Fishes, should be torn from those
Lucrative Provisions, and the Honorable Members
who are now deprived of the Aforesaid Provender
should be endowed with the Nutritious Substances
hereinbefore specified.
That is a proposition on which every member
can vote according to straight Party prin-
ciples, without violating his conscience.
Therefore, unless some member is bought
over, or takes to drink and doesn’t know
which way he is going, or becomes a habitual
absentee, the very ordinary accident of one
half the House being labelled one thing and
one half the other thing brings the country’s
business to a dead stop. And neither prefer-
ential voting nor second ballots, nor the
Referendum (valuable as it is), nor the
Initiative, nor the Recall can materially
alter the situation. A si mule-looking remedy
would be to have the Ministry elected by
the new Parliament as soon as it meets, and
fixed for the space of three years. In the
rare cases when Parliament was so eoually
divided and so rigidly adhesive to Party
lines that no one could be elected, the matter
might be referred to the peonle, voting as
one constituency. Then the Motion of Cen-
sure and the struggle for the Boaves and
Fishes would cease to disturb the Legisla-
ture. and no party would fear td provide a
Speaker, and no member need refuse the
Sneakership if the pleasant billet came his
way. and even' member could vote on every
measure according to his conscience without
considering how his note would influence bis
nartv’s immediate chance of securing the
dignities and the remuneration and the lien
on the official motor-car. None of these
nuestions cmld arise again unt'l aUer next
election. The change might deprive poli-
ticians of some amusement, but it ought to
heP business along, which is the thing that
really counts.

The Unclean Pub.
“Ugh”: What’s the explanation of this?
Regularly I read in the police-court reports
that some seller of small goods in a lost or
strayed suburb has been fined for uncleanli-
ness in his kitchen or fowlhouse, or that an
ice-cream prince doing business between
siestas in the Domain has been bounced be-
fore the beak by the Health Department
and charged with possessing contaminated
cups or spoons. Good enough. I’m always
glad to hear it. but why this exclusive atten-
tion to the trader who, compared to the dirty
publican, is a mere cipher in the germ-
developing and distributing business? Does
anybody know of one solitary, lonely in-
stance where the Health Department or any
other department has taken one of Sydney’s
many disgusting booze traders before a
court? I’ll bet. no. The disturbing or sus-
picious thing about it is that there is no
difficulty in getting a case. In fact, even in
mid-city it is the. exception to find a system
of cleanly liquor-serving, while in the sub-
urbs the methods are frequently putrid. I
was in the bar of a Western suburban so-
called good hotel the other evening where
glasses were being slopped in and out of a
tub of liquid characteristic of that common
to a pig’s trough. That is plain fact. I
ventured to remonstrate with the fat, sullen
party (he was the boss) who served me, and
I was thrown a snarl that he was running
the business, not me. I believed him. The
only difference between that dirty and dan-
gerous vendor to the public—he endangers
the health of hundreds for every one that
the saveloy-distributor and ice-cream poten-
tate can operate on —is that he kept his pig
trough where it could be seen, whereas the
average trough is located under the counter,
and, consequently, out of sight. But the
evil is so prevalent that even the Health
Department’s officers should be able to dis-
cover it (provided there is any anxiety in
that direction). In fact, a blind man could
track it with his nose. That would lead
him to the frequently stinking drying towels
which, as likely as not, spread on the glasses
cancer, syphilis and other horrors, if their
germs happen to be sluiced off in the trough.
Syphilis as a Punishment.
From a doctor who lias specialised in
infectious diseases: —
A favorite Wowser argument against any organ-
ised effort to deal effectively with venereal disease
is that syphilis is a GoD-sent punishment for incon-
tinence. The God of the Wowser must be an eccen-
tric sort of deity, judging from the evidence
recently given by Dr. Sequira, of the London Hos-
pital. Under his care was a girl of 12 infected
with syphilis by her own mother’s kiss. A woman
patient came into the hospital with secondary
syphilis, carrying a baby suffering from the same
disease in an inherited form. She also had with
her two little children, both of whom had been
infected with syphilis in the mouth from kissing
the baby. The same authority related the case
of a glassblower’s boy who had contracted syphilis
on the lip from using a blowpipe which had been
handed to him by an infected glassblower for finish-
ing off a bottle. At an evening party, some time
before Dr. Sequira gave his evidence, some kissing
game was played, and at this party one person with
a syphilitic spre on the mouth infected nearly every
girl present. Another case was that of a cook on
a railway dining-car who came straight from his
work in such a condition as to need immediate ad-
mission to hospital. He was described by Sequira
as “one of the worst cases of syphilis I have seen.”
How many people he may have infected by means
of table utensils and articles of food is a matter for
interesting speculation. Add to these and other
innumerable well-attested innocent infections —the
frequent accidental infections of doctors, nurses and
students, from performing a post-mortem examina-
tion, attending a confinement, being accidentally
bitten or scratched by a patent, making an exam-
ination, or dressing a wound —and the God of the
Wowsers appears as a very eccentric deitv indeed.
In fact, He is scarcely to be trusted by the Wowser
himself. If the evening party described by Sequira
had been a Sunday-school picnic at which kiss-in-
the-ring occupied its usual prominent position
amongst the sports, the holiest of Wowsers might
have developed some new views on the subject of
divine punishment for moral offences. The Out
Patient Committee of the London Hospital has
recentlv recorded its opinion as follows: “for the
good of the race, and for the good of the children
vet to be, an organised war on the spirochete of
svphilis should begin. Dr. Bulloch states that if
the weapons at our hand were wisely used syphilis
would be as rare as is small-pox in the course of a
generation.” “If the weapons in our hands were
wisely used” —can any fiercer indictment of the
social machinery of modern civilisation be con-
ceived ?
Randolph Bedford defends his position: —
Various Bulletin contributors object to my
“idealisation” of Tyson (as they call it). I treated
Tyson as a man of his time and an inevitable pro-
duct of it. He “did nothing for art or literature,
certainly ; and probably he did that nothing because
-vhpn made his habits there wasn’t any art or
literature in Australia to help. He certainly did not have
any graces of manner, and he may have been “uncouth
to the mind of any person who was quite opposite to
Tyson. But Australian internal development was
not made by duchesses or people with pretty bedside
manners or enthusiasts in art or literature, necessary
as all such people are in the making of a country.
Australia was made by the large and often coarse and
uncouth men of one idea, each working his own idea
to the logical conclusion. But for men who were
mostly as innocent of the affectedly cultured manners
of i.nrrl Chester vie no (who was in his proper job
as Groom to the Bedchamber) as they were ignorant
of the rotten dishonesties of mind and his general
treacherousness of character, the goldfields which
made Australia —the goldfields which made a market
which made Tyson —might never have been discov-
ered.
I “idealised” Tyson, then, only as a man I once
met, and as the man whom an excellent judge of
character, such as big Jim Wilkinson, knew inti-
mately ; as a man who had done good for himself
and liis country at the same time; who had made
millions without hurting anybody. And that appre-
ciation of him I repeat. My critics apparently fail
to see this man because his money hides him —though
there was nothing disgraceful about his money, except
the way it was spent. They object to him being, as
they allege, quick off the mark with foreclosures, and
then make a great success as mind-readers, by dis-
covering that he rarely lent on mortgage except with
an eye to foreclosure. Why not? It’s done every
day, and it’s quite legitimate. What do they want —
advances up to 90 per cent, of the valuation, so that
it wouldn’t pay to foreclose if the interest got into
arrears? Tyson committed the crime of having three
or four millions more than I have, and for that
reason these people want me to find nothing admirable
in him. Is there any man in Australia who can put
his finger on a mean or treacherous or dishonest action
of Tyson’s? If so, let us hear of him.
What arrant nonsense to call the man’s way of
dressing anyhow—the ragged bootlaces and the old
dark grey coat, and all the rest of the carelessness
of habit—affectation and playing to the gallery!
Had he dressed like a German Jew financier who is
about to be introduced to an English king, he would
have been an empty fop. With his work filling his
life, his methods of dressing were as careless as thev
could become. While conserving decency, they call
his rags part of a scheme of playing to the gallery !
Tyson was not conceited in the little senses ; he. was
self-centred. Where did I “idealise” him? I claimed
for him the bushman’s virtues—courage, endurance,
enterprise, self-denial. Who shall say he did not have
them ?
The interminable business of importing
ships has at last reached a climax. Last
week the dear old Ma State imported a
wreck. It wasn’t a nuw inter-State liner
that went to smash, but a small, peddling
thing called the Cambewarra, intended for
the South Coast blue metal trade, and speci-
ally fitted for that purpose with a huge, top-
heavy crane like a Carthaginian’s nose. A
local shipmaster who knows the dreaded
“southerly bustes” that periodically, sweeps
the N.S.W. coast was firmly of opinion that
the curious little import, handicapped as it
was by its heavy gear and its lifeless cargo,
would' be lost in the first wind it met. But
the import was fated never to reach its
feckless purchasers or the place where the
“buster” rages. It bumped the Westralian
coast one’ calm morning last week, and
very soon the product of a, Dutch shipyard
had disapueared from the world of visible
assets. The position may now be summed
up this way :
(1) The unspeakable N.S.W. roads will be worse
than usual for months, because the imported craft
bought with borrowed money has been lost, and is,
th-ref ore, unavailable for metal carrying.
(2) Some of Cohen’s capital, on which interest
has to be paid, is submerged on a coast where there
are too many obstructions already.
(3) The wages which would have kept some Aus-
tralian shipwrights in work have gone to Dutchmen.
(4) Some more is going just as soon as they can
start on another import.
(5) Some valuable shipbuilding experience has
been lost to the people of this continent, and a
second lot will be lost as soon as possible.
(6) The Australian has been further advertised
abroad as an incapable islander who would not be
able to travel by sea but for the superior skill of
intelligent foreigners.
It is enough to make local patriots writhe
to see Jap-built liners like the Tango Maru
dwarfing the Circular Quay warehouses,
while Australian State Governments inflate
the foreigner with orders for pottering
dredges and little stone carriers. For the
sake of our national self-respect, let us have
a duty on imported ships—and imported
wrecks.
“Granite’s” few reflections :
Minister for Works Griffith has been spared some
trouble by the wrecking of the s.s. Cambewarra, off
Fremantle (W.A.). This costly craft was built in
Holland to the order of the N. S. Wales Government
to run in the Kiama blue metal trade, and was fitted
with a patent contraption, or hypodermic syringe, in
the bows by means of which the loading and unload-
ing of the stone was to be facilitated and cheapened.
The patent device was welcomed by the customers of
the Government, but the stone-lumpers weren’t enthu-
siastic. Indeed, they were , blastiferous. The stone-
dumping union is a close trade corporation. The
wages are governed by overtime, which is so large
that the men are the best-paid manual workers in
the State. At present, the little s.s. Kiama is
engaged tearing up and down the coast in a breath-
less effort to drag up enough output from the Kiama
quarries to satisfy all the metropolitan municipali-
ties. So, as soon as it arrives at either end, there
is a scurry to load or unload, irrespective of the
time of day or night. Hence the big wages earned.
These workers didn’t view with delight the news
that Griffith was importing a machine to do four
times as much work as they could do at a fraction
of the cost, and they were credited with the inten-
tion of manufacturing trouble if the Cambewarra on
arrival turned out to be a success. The disturbance
was all the more likely in view of the understanding
that if the anticipated satisfactory results eventu-
ated, another vessel of the same class was to be
procured. This meant practically wiping the stone-
jugglers off the map. The wreck postpones the
clash until another Cambewarra is built, and deliv-
erv made, which, with the approval of Providence,
will be as soon as possible.

Squatter Hay, of Coolangatta, lias not
much opinion of the N.S.W. South Coast
farmers. He declares that the Illawarra is
not the district it used to be 25 or 30 years
ago, and unless some change is made for the
better it will cease to be known as “the
garden of the State.” His suggested remedy
is a holy gem. He proposes that the Govern-
ment should establish an experimental farm,
which would be subsidised out of the Berry
bequest to the extent of £ISOO to £IBOO per
annum. There should be 50 students, im-
ported youths, who, on completion of their
training, could be distributed among the
farmers to assist them with their expert
knowledge. Evidently Mr. Hay does not
regard the local farmer’s son as a fit subject
for scientific training with a view to advis-
ing his old man. The short and simple
annals -of a great part, at all events, of the
Illawarra district are that., in the struggle
to pay the rent, the farmer has had to drag
the immortal soul out of the land till it has
become impoverished. And when the land-
lord who got all this rent dies, he isn’t even
buried on his miserable tenant’s property to
enrich the soil. The amount of nitrogen in
a landlord is quite remarkable, and yet he is
put away in the family vault, so that it is
all wasted.
“Shipping Clerk” writes :
Sydney Harbor authorities haven’t said any-
thing out loud about an incident which occurred on
February 5, when a big' tramp steamer, drawing 29ft.
odd, touched bottom, while leaving Port Jackson.
The vessel was in mid-channel with the marks in
line when those on board distinctly felt it bump.
Whether it hit the bottom or a floating anchor or
other obstruction has not been made public, but
that there was mighty little water underneath the
steamer is quite certain. The perjured case for the
port largely rests on the assertion that the Port
Jackson main channels can without dredging carry
water up to 40ft., whereas the truth is that any-
thing like this depth can be reckoned on only
within narrow limits. The day isn’t far off when the
neglected deep-water ports will come into their own.
Meanwhile, if Sydney is to compete with a place like
Hobart, where dredges are unknown, and accommo-
date the biggest modern vessels, costly deepen-
ing operations will be necessary in the near future.
“Vic.”: A Parliamentary Commission has
handed in a scathing report on the Closer
Settlement vagaries of Premier Bent, whose
lands minister was Yawn Murray. The
management is slaughtereu unmercifully. It
seems the amount spent on the purchase of
estates was £3,103,731, of which vacant lots
represent £341,704. Of these vacant lots
£75.228 worth have been abandoned by the
settlers. They were a progressive lot, these
lessees, and they progressed into the Credit
Foncier strong-room to the extent of
£160,127, of which no less than £125,000 is
in arrears. Fifteen of the large estates
purchased have proved to be quite unsuit-
able, and the transaction has a distinct odor
of unburied rat. The Secretary of the Com-
mission believes that the Government has
been let in very considerably by fraud
through applicants being allowed to select
before the land selected was purchased from
the squatters. Many of these pre-purchase
selectors have defaulted, and the most .of
such transactions are suspected of being
what is known in the vulgar tongue as “put
up jobs.” It is a vast and arrogant swindle.
Yet it might have been a vast and arrogant
success. Private speculators have made a
profitable business of buying estates to sub-
divide them —but then P.E> puts on the
screw all the time and gets its money’s worth,
whereas the Government of Victoria just
butted in like a good old milch cow, and its
butting didn’t pay.
“Miles,” more in sorrow than in anger:
I am with “Battler” (B. 8/1/T4), in his dis-
paraging remarks about Australia’s citizen forces. I
have been connected with them for many years now,
and can assert there never was a time within my
memory when they were so untrained and undis-
ciplined. Only those who are doing the work know
what the officers are up against these days. On
paper our Defence scheme is a great. success, iff
practice, it is making in the direction of rank
failure. The fault lies, not with the officers (Lord
knows they do their best!), but with those who
framed the foolish regulations. The infantry soldier,
for instance, has to do 16 days’ training per year*
eight days of which must be spent in camp, ihis
means that, for the rest of the year, he has to put
in the eouivalent of only eight days to be marked
efficient. You can’t teach a man even elementary
drill in the time; and the result is that four out of
the eight days in camp, which should be devoted to
higher training, are wasted teaching squad, section,
company and battalion drill. As two days are s P<-' rl t
travelling to and from the camp, there remains but
two days for manoeuvres, from which the man in tne
ranks learns nothing. In a great many • cases the
trainees are eager enough to learn, and take an
interest in the work; but there is another section,
and not a small one, which attends drill only because
it has to, and while there harasses the instructors as
much as possible. This type of youth will go a long
way towards wrecking the scheme, unless he is taken
in hand at once, and instructors are given more
opuortunities to drill a little manliness into him.
All officers regret that night drills have been practi-
cally cut out, in deference to persons who wowse;
for' it was at those drills that officers got to know
their companies, and were able to instil discipline
into them. Some fond parents, however, set up a
wail that their Willie would have to be out at
night and would get into bad ways, so Willie is
now free to stand at the corner of the street and spit
at the kerbstone. But he won’t stop a Jap. soldier
by spitting at him, however well he can do it; and,
for his own morals and the country’s safety, his
nights were better spent learning to defend the home
which will surely call for defence some day.
Apparently the easy casualness with which
Australia’s champion airman, Harry
Hawker, steps into his machine and goes
for a flight as one would take a run in a
motor, has stirred or shamed the Defence
authorities into doing something with the
four Bristol biplanes which ex-Defence Min-
ister Pearce ordered away back in July of
the year before last. The machines, together
with four mechanics and two certificated air-
men, duly arrived and have remained stored
up or bushed or lost ever since. First a
“ground” was necessary—though Hawker
merely runs his weapon out on to the street
and soars up forthwith —so a huge paddock
at Altona Bay was engaged. Then a hangar
must be erected — Hawker uses an ordinary
motor garage—so a contract was let for one
at £4lO0 —the four ’planes only cost £3460.
The hangar (shed) isn’t finished yet; in fact,
it is hardly begun yet, but the Department
has got a move on; it has bought a motor
lorry to hump the ’planes from place to place
—as if they couldn’t fly from place to place
—and has engaged motor-cars for the use of
the mechanics and attendants.. The whole
thing would constitute a shrieking joke if it
wasn’t that there is tragedy behind it. If
the Government would—rand could—engage
Hawker, and give.him full authority and
instructions to go ahead, it could probably
have an efficient flying: corps in a year or
two. But then Haw.ker is an Australian,
and it is always necessary to have .an im-
ported expert to make a fool of a job like
that.

There’s a wet sleet falling far away in
Loandon Town,
And the lamps illumine pavements of a
dreary, hopeless brown,
Where the shuffling, unkempt hawkers tramp
the gutters in despair.
And the outcasts sit and shiver
On the seats above the river,
Where the oozy ripples quiver in the damp
and biting air.
All day long the traffic’s booming through
the streets of Loandon Town ;
All the year long, rain or sunshine, go the
people up and down ;
Now ’tis February weather —reeking fog, and
mud, and cold —
And the Capital stupendous.
Where they make the cash they lend us,
Wears a pali of smoke tremendous as
whelmed Pompeii of old.
Banks and offices are verv busy now in
Loandon Town,
And the clerks are growing dizzy with the
figures they put down ;
For the Money Mills are working to the
limit of their span :
Tn the streets stalk Want and Sorrow,
But the boom may burst to-morrow. _
And the whole world’s out to borrow —with
Australia in the van.
See ! The same old Mat of Welcome lies out-
side the Same Old Door,
And the Same Old Sign is hanging where it
always,' hung of yore ;
For it’s DhoTb’s Day in Loandon, and the
Loan-Drunks’ reg-i-ment.
With their paner bugles blowin’
Are parading before Gotten,
Madly bent on further owin’ while their
names are worth a cent!
’Tis a proud day for Australia, for her
leaders bear the brunt
In the game of Pawn-the-Future — they are
easily in front!
Long ago they pledged the Present in the
Loan-Cup of the .Tew,
But those sums could not content them,
So, on finding they had spent them,
To the Cup once more they bent them —and
they’re raising it anew !
Hocus-pocus, and Hey presto! Lo, the
vision fades away ;
And, behold, our own Australia on a blazing
summer day:
Hot and brilliant is the sunshine, and the
dust is all about —
Fverytliing is dry and splendid.
For the rain has not descended,
Though the Loan Jag has not ended, we are
getting near a drought.
But, who cares? A fig for croakers! Though
the fend is dry and brown —
Though our imports are gigantic and our
revenues are down —
Though we’ve nearly reached the Limit of
our credit and renown. —
Our prosperity’s surprising—
The expenditure is rising,
While Government advertising is a credit to
the Crown!
We’re all right, thanks be to Uncle up in
Loandon, cold and drear!
Never mind bis shivering outcasts. We re-
quire the money here !
Our amazing wealth and sunshine, our re-
sources. our grand Debt,
And the Boa ring Times we’re having
Show the folly of mere saving;
So, though Drought’s brown flag is waving,
- we will drain the Loan Cup yet!
Then, three rousing cheers for Uncle and
for Loandon grim and grev!
Where our Lean-Drunks are parading in the
good; old-fashioned way.
While we skite about Our Future —which
belongs to GoD-Knows-Who !
Till we set about returning
All the money we are burning—
Oh, there’ll be a snaU-like yearning to repay
it —when we do.

The Yarn of the Dusty Capital.
Sydney Telegraph lias broken out again re
the dry-hash Federal capital site of Can-
berra, which has been in grievous trouble of
late, and badly requires a trifle of advertise-
ment and apology and some whitewash on
its sepulchre. This is the best part of the
Telegraph’s narrative: —
As Mr. Cook is still in control of the Home Affairs
Department, it is up to him as a man who has
fought many stirring battles for the rights of New
South Wales under the capital section of the Con-
stitution, to see that his official staff is kept. con-
tinuously on the move till Canberra becomes a
reality. Mr. Cook was far-sighted enough to bring
from Chicago Mr. Griffin, the winner of the first
prize in the planning competition. The absence of
Mr. Griffin in the United States has been respon-
sible for some of the recent delays, but when he
returns there ought to be no further hesitation in
pushing right on until Parliament is ready to occupy
its new quarters. It is regrettably the case that
some Victorian influences are still active on the side
of frustrating the work by protests against the ex-
penditure. But the majority in the two Federal
Houses is definitely committed to completing Can-
berra, and New South- Wales will look to its mem-
bers, no matter which party they belong to, to see
that the compact is kept.
It is a curious business —that “compact.”
It was agreed that the capital was to be
in N.S.W., and the capital was fixed in
N.S.W., at Dalgety, on the swift and clean
and ever-flowing Snowy River—the one river
in N.S.W. which didn’t fail or stagnate even
in the lamentable 1902 drought. The place
had a good port handy, and an abundance
of cheap water-power for lighting and heat-
ing and manufacturing. But there was
among the parish-minded and congestion
folk a demand for a capital in some poor
region where it couldn’t become more than
“a large country town,” as the Sydney Tele-
graph frankly described the future Can-
berra, and couldn’t possibly be a rival to
Sydney ; also where its little scrap of trade
might be conveniently annexed by Sydney. The
Herald and Telegraph, the two unlettered
rags which used to tell their readers at great
length how Captain Cook was the original
discoverer of Australia, clamored for Can-
berra, which, seeing that their interests
mostly lie in Sydney, didn’t look so well as
it might have done. In fact, it looked like
using national interests for purposes of pri-
vate profit. Cook and Conroy and J. C.
Watson and Hughes and the rest of the
old Sydney push clamored also. The other
States, as they couldn’t have the capital
themselves, took little interest in the matter,
so it was mostly left to the N.S.W. members,
and they were mostly dominated by the igno-
rant but rich and vociferous papers which
thought that Cook discovered Australia. The
Sydney end of the Federal Labor party
threw dirt at the Referendum principle when
it was proposed to apply it to the capital
question. No vote was taken of the people
of Australia or even of the people of
N.S.W. The matter was just scrambled
through. And to-day even the advocates of
Canberra don’t know quite what to do about
their glorious site. The regular dry season
has come. The alleged rivers which were
to make the Federal capital another Venice
are to a great extent off duty. The dust
storms are bad. The labor troubles are bad
also, and they are mostly based on the diffi-
culty of getting something to drink which
hasn’t a green scum on top and a
dead rabbit below. Nobody seems to have
much heart to push on with the business.
Now the Telegraph, by way of side-stepping
the issue, sets the sluggishness down to the
temporary absence of Griffin, the Yankee
expert, and considers in its inmost stomach
that all will be well when he comes back.
But Griffin is only a cheap young man at
best. If he had been anything else he would
never have come all the wav from Chicago
for a mere £IOOO a year. A first-class expert
would have cost several times the money.
Anyhow, the people here have Griffin’s
plans, specifications and ideas. They can’t
be lielnlessly dependent on Griffin, seeing
that all the existing Australian cities were
built without his aid. Also this country
probably knows a vast amount more about
water with scum on top and dead rabbits
underneath than Griffin ever heard of.
The real trouble isn’t too little Griffin, but
too much Canberra.
Apparently there is a belated chance that
N.S.W. may get the totalisator in place of
the bookmaker, though it is mentioned that
the State Ministry is divided on the subject,
while the Opposition is certainly divided,
and the pious section of the press is so badly
divided that about nine-tenths of it is on the
side of Isaac Shortodds. It is a pity that
there should be so much division ; the thing
arouses suspicion. The advocates of the
tote can’t be other than honest, for the
gambling implement divides all its takings
and hqts no fund for purposes of bribery.
Besides, it is an inanimate machine, which
can’t go round buying support with money
in one hand, and a whisky breath and the
offer of an introduction to a ballet girl in
the other. Of course the opponents of the
tote may be honest, but on the other hand
they may have been “interviewed” on a
business basis by Isaac Shortodds. He has
the means for corruption, and he has also a
mighty strong reason to do all the corruption
he can. No opponent of the tote is above
suspicion so long as Isaac Shortodds is alive.
