Once more the weekly paper starts in hope-
less pursuit of the one-week show. It is like
giving away a 300-yards start in a quarter-
mile dash; but still it has to be done in the
interests of Art and Literature and (pos-
sibly) Science. This time the flying show is
the last Lewis Waller production of “A Itoyal
Rival,” which jumped off at Sydney Royal
last Saturday night, and passes the post to-
morrow (Friday) evening, whereupon old
age overtakes it and it falls into the grave.
Ihe story of the “Kival” is the old familiar
one of the “Maritana” opera, and author
Gerald du Maurier has built his version
neatly round the brave, roystering, reckless,
tattered Don Caesar de Lazan, which con-
genial and attractive part is played gaily by
Waller. The English star’s light touch is
delightful in these so-called “romantic”
studies, where the dull bailiff Unreality is
waiting always at the wings to take posses-
sion at the first sign of solemnity or heavy-
footedness. Madge Marita Titheradge, the
winsome and ambitious gipsy singing girl of
the play, has something like a triumph in the
first scene,_ where she performs an exhilarat-
ing Spanish dance as few professional
dancers and no musical comedy prancer
could ever do it. It sets fire to the theatrical
premises, and the audience takes a long time
to cool off and cease demanding more. Re-
membering that fiery and stirring perform-
ance, one regrets Marita’s elevation to the
formal life of the Court and a sort of sombre
second-row seat in the plot. She is highly
decorative all the time, and plays cleverly
in a minor key until the last embrace. Frank
Woolfe, as Don Jose de Santarem, the schem-
ing villain of the piece, as usual alienates
the sympathy of all hands. This last villain
study is one of the best in his comprehensive
gallery. Charles Quartermaine provides an
intense _ and interesting study of a puppet
stage king obsessed by the usual lady; and
May Santon makes a pretty appeal for sym-
pathy as a very shapely and winsome Spam
ish lad. Alec F, Thompson and Marianne
Caldwell, as two attempted aristocrats, are
excellent in their grotesquely absurd parts.
The co. moves on to South Africa shortly.
“Come Over Here” still perspires to good
business at Her Majesty’s, Sydney. If it had
been better than it is, the people at the doors
might have had their feelings harrowed by
the sight of hundreds clamoring hopelessly
for admission, so perhaps it’s just as well it
is just what it is.
, * * *
There are no signs yet that the most
barefaced “fake” in theatriealism ever offered
to Sydney folk is beginning to be found out,
since “Officer 660” has been on duty for
seven weeks at the Criterion.
At Sydney’s Little Theatre Pinero’s neat
comedy, “His House in Order,” is admirably
presented by a company whose chief excel-
lence is its all-round thoroughness. Every
woman who has married a widower is ad-
vised to take her husband.
* * *
Mr. Bruce Robertson, an Australian
artist, whose work has found representation
in the National (N.S.W.) Gallery, has
prepared a very interesting exhibition in
the form of an etching of the arrival of the
Commonwealth fleet in Sydney harbor last
year. The Australia appears in. the fore-
ground swinging slowly, with, as it seems,
a personal sense of dignified pride, to its
anchorage. To the many depictions of that
historical home-coming Mr. Robertson’s
etching is a conspicuous contribution. Fifty
copies are to be offered subscribers at £2 2s.
each, and precipitous application is neces-
sary.
The Aladdin panto, will come to the dead-
end to-morrow night: and on Saturday the
Sidney Adelphi will “come back” with “The
Pride of the Prairie,” a Mexican drama full
of gunpowder and cold spines.
At Sydney Tivoli this week, the Tom Davis
Trio of motor-cyclists provide a daredevilish
turn that, since it is liable to produce a crop
of amateur imitators, brings joy to the hearts
of local undertakers. Vivien Talleur and
Margaret Klos open up a stock of dainty
dances, ancient and modern; and Talleur
Andrews has transferred his voice from the
J.C.W. Co. to the Tiv., with a large amount
of success. Waterbury Bros, and Tenney
are responsible for a rather good musical
item, with some delightful incidental comedy ;
and the Two Bobs (another first-class rag-
time combination), Clemons and Dean
(songandance people), and J. Shields (a
clever imitator) complete the list of new-
comers. Selma Braatz, the lady juggler,
still causes the. world to wonder why she
doesn’t keep still; and various ragtimers,
who inflict their goods on a more or less
unwilling house, are recommended to mercy
on account of their youth.
The Fuller-Brennan programme at the
National Amphitheatre (Sydney) is becom-
ing more ambitious each week, and new and
important turns follow each other with
commendable rapidity. This is possible only
with an extensive circuit, and the manage-
ment is making the most of it. Even Syd-
ney Morning Herald has taken to serving
up boom notices of the Amphitheatre, for
the sole reason that the show deserves them.

Everybody in Sydney having now seen
“The Grafters,’’ at the Palace Theatre,
William Anderson, with that forethought for
the people’s entertainment which is one of
his most notable characteristics, announces
that on Saturday “The Speculators” will
be staged. This is another musical burlesque
from Alabama, or thereabouts, and Bert Le
Blanc is its prophet.
The White City still blooms its thousand
lights upon the voluptuous night in Rush-
cutter’s Bay, beside Sydney harbor, and nice,
white Australian girls and lovers frisk and
flirt and joy-ride upon the flying switchback
and glittering carossel where once a Chinese
market garden waved its turnip-top at the
stars and the lonely Pong wasted his un-
freshness upon the desert air. Calvert, “the
drunk on the high wire,” continues to be the
chief free entertainment, and his popularity
is likely to continue until he falls from his
altitude of 60ft. upon a 45-year-old girleen
belonging to any popular musical comedy in
town. After that the rush of press agent
on behalf of the more or less musical and
the somewhat comedy will obliterate every-
thing else on the sky-line.
Of the 305 days in the year there is one
in which the old unreliable maxim, “Charity
begins at home,” may be overlooked by the
closest-pursed citizen. That is on the day
when the actor seeks a helping hand for his
unfortunate brother. Morning, noon and
night the showman and the player are first
off the mark when “’ssistance is needed for
hospital or kindred relief. Once onlv during
the year the mummer mentions the fact that
his sick or needy brother is in existence, and
if the citizen turns a blind ear to his remark
The Bulletin has misjudged the said citi-
zen. On Monday, at the White City (Syd-
ney), there will be a carnival in aid of the
benevolent and pension funds of the Actors’
Association of Australia, and £sofWis the
least that should be expected. The big show
will commence at 2 p.m. and will close at
midnight.

ventor (moving-picture variety), wondering why his

Has absolutely no connection with the Victorian

Final results on application.

Adelaide Tivoli, which remains in the
open on the Oval, using the big new theatre
as well on Saturdays, is very strong on the
athletic side. The Sckmettans, the Clem-
endo§, the jumping Rose and Ellis, and the
Graus on their rod, pole or perch, form the
backbone of a good bill. The King’s is for
the moment strongest on the musical side,
with Bailey’s posing dogs as an extra. Fer-
nandez is a real musician, though mostly
content to fool with weird home-made in-
struments, and Ray Lawrence is about the
most ladylike and convincing of the recent
crop of singing female-impersonators. The
Dandies, Branscombe’s co., with Roland
Henry as chief humorist, have a big and
faithful following. The picture-houses—
West’s, Wondergraph, Pav., Empire, Star
and others —continue just as before ; and so
does the Royal, which is to say it remains
closed.
The only show that came new to Melbourne
last Saturday was Hawker’s Flight at Caul-
field racecourse. Hawker had previously
smashed all Australian records that this
writer can remember, save two —those of
Hart and another (he di'sremembers the
name of the other) —by travelling on his bi-
plane from Elsternwick to the lawn at
Government House and back again. Also
his airmanship had been noted on one or two
other occasions. On the strength of the
assurance that Hawker would rise to the oc-
casion in any ordinary sort of wind and
weather, a crowd of 25,000 expectant citi-
zens paid their money to see him nerform.
He put up a neat performance. Starting
from about the place where a Caulfield Cup
field is lined up, Hawker’s conveyance ran
on wheels 40yds. a-down the course ere it
rose from the ground ~n d amazed the multi-
tude. The effect of a flier in action is very
variable. As a rule one’s biassed view of
Hawker’s vehicle gives it the appearance
of a fish, or torpedo, with its chest resting
on one plane and its rudder end inclined
towards the upper plane. On Saturday, the
beautifully balanced ending to Hawker’s first
flight was the best sensation of all. The
machine circled downwards until it came
skimming over the same stretch of ground
from which it had started. At last it landed
on its wheels and ran itself to a standstill
in the centre of the course. Hawker, as he got
out and walked, was an apparently unflus-
tered aviator, wearing an engaging smile, a
cloth cap and a blue sac suit, cut baggy. To
anyone who saw the first productions of
“Milestones” the personality of the young
aviator is easily described. With another
couple of inches to his height he would be
the “dead ring” of Arthur Preece. the enthu-
siastic inventor, as impersonated by Herbert
Ford. liawker is a boy of 22. Ford, in his
character of the eager, ingenuous Preece,
looked very little older.
THE PANTO., AT HER MAJESTY'S,
MELBOURNE (Monkey Ballet).
Hawker made three flights last Saturday.
The second was a high soar, in which he
went over the course many times at a great
pace. This time the final whiz up the
straight ended unluckily. The machine landed
on its wheels, but swerved in towards the
rails, and bumped violently against a post
without hurting itself. Some of the crowd
that should not have been standing there
might have suffered contusions or sudden
deaths. However, all of the misolaced spec-
tators fell back out of harm’s way, except
one unfortunate who caught a whack on
the nose from one of the aeroplane’s tractor
blades. The third ascent was a homeward
.fly. Hawker took a charming lady passenger
into the airy structure, and together they
Hew in Elefernwick after fD’io- the rounds

MR. PAGE, late of Paris, New York and David
Jones, Sydney, in conjunction with MADAM PAGE
(nee Miss Davis), have opened as LADIES’ TAILORS
•and COSTUMIERS at Ocean House, Moore Street,
■Sydney.
Mr. Page, having held high Continental positions
:as the actual designer of ladies’ costumes, there will
•ensure a distinctiveness in every way equal to the
unost favored Continental styles.
Every costume will be a special study, which will
•quickly establish Mr. Page's name as a household
•word in ladies’ tailoring.
The fact of Mr. and Madam Page operating per-
sonally in the creation and construction of all details
■will present an economic advantage not possible of
■attainment by other fashionable houses, where the
•cost of a highly paid staff is charged always to
rthe customer.
This substantial saving should come as a welcome
•opportunity to all, particularly as every garment is
•exclusive in style and material, and bears the touch
•of a master hand. Note carefully the address:—
M. PAGE, OCEAN HOUSE, MOORE STREET,
SYDNEY.
The bloke who celebrates having seen a
train or got married or born for the first
time should do so on the royal brand.
Exhilaration combined with wise sobriety
is best obtained from King George IV. top-

“The Lion and the Mouse," wherein
Julius Knight and co. fa rewelled Melbourne
last night (Wednesday). is a bad play
written years ago with a good American
purpose. Ti e purpose has lost its topical
interest, but the essential solemn foolishness
of the story was more glaringly apparent
last Saturday than when it came fresh to
this country. The fact that most of the
principals were uncertain in their lines

didn’t tend to make the performance con-
vincing. Even Julius Knight, as the allegedly
iron-liearted millionaire who acts like a vain
old ass with an autobiography in view, had
seemingly forgotten some of his cues, and
lost the run of his previous conception of
the part. Nevertheless the audience took
kindly to the first three acts of “The Lien
and the Mouse.” Audiences consist largely
of girl these times, and this entertainment
consists mainly of a girl defeating the com-
bined forces of an unscrupulous plutocracy
and a corrupt legislature. The heroine, orig-
inally played here by Miss Katherine Grey,
is an easy winner all the way. Miss Browne
has the grateful task of reducing the bloated
capitalist to limpness in this revival. She
performs it vigorously and picturesquely.
Whereas Miss Grey of yore always wore a
feeble coat and skirt, and kept her hands in
her side pockets, Miss Browne is a dream
of drapery and an otherwise impressive young
person. Therefore when she tells the multi-
millionaire what kind of a person he is and
how she intends to play h —l at Washington
rather than see her father further sat upon,
the audience cheers her as she never was
cheered before, and the deplorable weakness
of the last act doesn’t matter much.

Scenery, pageantry and all the other things

Also the Temptation scene is featured in the

lication would say that a red-hot lemptation
scene is b“ t the thing to attract the godly
and the ungodly alike.
At Melbourne King's there will be no more
“Land of Nod” after Friday, the 20 th. The

“J. Disliragge”: The long wash-up of a
Maryannless home threatened to come to an
end last week, when a yellow-haired siren
from a registry office drifted along to see if
we would suit her as employers. She took
the best chair calmly and without invitation
and began to cross-examine Mrs. Dishragge.
When she had been informed how many we
were in family, how many minutes the
assisting sister-in-law would linger on the
premises after she took it over, what time I
came home and left, how old the little fellow
was, and about a dozen other things, she
cleared her two yards of powdered throat
and moaned, “Oh, I’m afraid the place
wouldn’t suit me at all! I can’t hear chil-
dren !” Then uprose the proud and furious
mother and, not meaning it that way at all,
floored the disdainful invader with: “No!
you don’t look the sort of girl that could!”
I am informed that three imitation pearl
buttons flew off the hysterical siren’s back
as she fell from the premises. No matter how
yellow a siren may be. she hates to think
she couldn’t if she wanted to.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Milford Haven (that was his professional name;
Ije was William E. Weaver in private life) died in
Sydney ■ Hospital on the 2nd. He was the only son
df Captain Weaver, of London, and started his pro-
fessional career in Australia a quarter of a century
back with the, Williamson Opera Company. After-
wards he spent seven years on Harry Rickards’ cir-
cuits. His remain^ 1 were’put to rest at Waverley.
“Moana”: After all, the whoop made by the
Noo Yark Anti-White Slave Society, and the general
fuss, and the yards of cablecrams about the affair,
and the alleged lengthy conference of the alleged Im-
migration Officers, and the miles of interviews in the
(N.Y. papers, and the general hue and cry, it tran-
spires that the spectacular arrest of Marie Lloyd
and her best jockey boy, Bernard Dillon, some time
back in Uncle Samland was nothing more serious
than a cleverly planned and excellently staged adver-
tisement for Marie, engineered by her American press
agent! It takes its place in theatrical history along-
side the Big Hat Prosecution a year or two back in
Fogtown, and next door to the famous story circu-
lated about a celebrated actress some time ago
describing how she caught a whale in the Gulf of
Mexico with a rod and line!
THE PANTO., AT HER MAJESTY'S,
MELBOURNE (Barry Lupino and another).
The new British Copyright Act seems to be loaded
with trouble for theatrical managers. Hitherto man-
agers have stood on velvet when dealing with
authors who submitted manuscript plays. Now the
miserable author has some rights of his or her
own. In London Miss Rosemary Rees, a Maoriland
girl, recently sued Walter and Frederick Melville, of
the New Prince’s Theatre on a charge of having lifted
portion of a play she submitted them under the
title of “The Beggar Bride.” The Melvilles rejected
Miss Rees’s play, but shortly afterwards produced a
new creation which they called “A Beggar Girl’s Wed-
ding.” Miss Rees declared that it was mostly her play
under another name, and started legal proceedings.
Two well-known playwrights swore that the plot of “A
Beggar Girl’s Wedding” was identical with that of
“The Beggar Bride.” Verdict went for de-
fendants. A Sydney case, based on similar
allegations, hasn’t got into full blast yet, but the
foundation-stone was laid last week, and the com-
pleted structure is expected to blossom forth and be
steaming at high pressure very shortly—in a manner
of speaking.
If the second or third re-arrival of Allan Doone,
with a well-worn budget of feeble broguey dramas, is
“Glorious News for the Irish,” the forthcoming first
visit of Harry Lauder might be expected to count as
“Bonnie Tidings for the Scawtch.” Lauder is the
chap who introduced anew and genial form of Sandy
to the music-hall stage—a merry, bonnie Sandy in
place of the dour, bony ditto of yore. But as busi-
ness propositions for Australia the difference between
Lauder and Doone is the difference between perfervid
Irish belief in things Hibernian and sober Scottish
faith in things Caledonian. One is reminded of the
proverb, “Scotchmen drink Scotch whisky because it
is the best; Irishmen drink Irish whisky because it
is Irish.” Even the McTavishes will judge Lauder
on his merits in a great measure. The Mikes have
swallowed Doone as a shark swallows a bit of sail
cloth.
“Have you heard of any possible engagements ?
I am so anxious to get on. I hope you will put in
a word for me whenever you can.” So Nellie Arm-
strong wrote to Wilhelm Ganz, an influential London
conductor, composer and pianist, in 1886; and
Nellie Melba will probably thank him very pro-
fusely for including her early appeal in his recently
published autobiography. She had taken a letter of
introduction from someone in Melbourne to Ganz.
At the first hearing he recognised that she had an
uncommonly good voice, and he promptly communi-
cated the fact to his friend Carl Rosa. Rosa said
he would make an appointment to hear her, but he
forgot all about it—and that was how Nellie Arm-
strong came to drop Ganz the quoted reminder.
Albert Cohen (“Call Boy”), of the Dunedin (M.L.)
Star, is giving up his theatrical work on the paper
after a solid 20 years at it. He has been one of the
most popular show critics in a country where there
are practically no critics. Asa matter of hard fact
it was his personality that made his popularity,
although, as far as really and truly theatrical news
(not press agent’s news), went Cohen was well out of
the ruck, just as he was, for so many years, in re-
gard to political news before he took over the sub-
editorial chair of the Star. When he left the M.L.
Parliamentary press gallery, the Ministers (it was in
Richard Seddon’s time) handed him a service of
silver plate in sheer thankfulness. They reckoned
that, with Cohen off the track, they could secrete a
piece of useful political information until the time
was ripe.
The old hands are beginning to shake their heads
and remark sadly that the Stage is not what it used
to be. There was a time when it had its own
peculiar style of garb, largely red and yellow, for
street wear, allowed its hair to grow long, and had
its own code of morals and ethics. Also, the stage-
manager was expected to have a pungent wit and
a fine flow of descriptive language. The Stage has
g'one to the Devil. Not one actor in a hundred now
dresses differently from the other man in the street;
not one in 200 wears his hair long; and his morals
arc no worse than, and no better than, and no
different from those of the vulgar mob. Also, the
glory of the stage-manager’s eloquence has departed;
and he is no longer allowed to tear the atmosphere
asunder while addressing Miss Snevellici, and Miss
Bravura, and Mrs. Gruddei), and the Infant Phenom-
enon. One recent young lady who thought that a
Melbourne stage-manager had not been quite polite
served blue paper on the staggered king, haling him
before the City Court op a charge of using “insult-
ing words.” But when the time came the lady did
not appear, and the affair blew over. Possibly Vin-
cent Crummies had abased himself and eaten his
hat at the lady’s feet by way of reparation.
The following is alleged to be Ethel Warwick’s re-
ply to the question, “Are you delighted to be back in
Australia?”: “You can bet your life I am. All of
us are glad to get back to Australia. All over Lon-
don you meet artists who are longing to return, and
who Warmly congratulate one and wring one’s hands
when they hear that you are. going back to the land
of sunshine and hospitality. Why, you cannot get
away from the fact that we all have a glorious time
here. In London, you know, there are so many people,
and so many actors and actresses,, that one is com-
paratively a mere detail in that huge whirlpool. In
Australia the limelight of publicity on the people of
the profession is a million candle-power, and we are
kept in view all the time. We are made a fuss of
and feted and talked about, while the warming spirit
of hospitality is all the time being poured on us.
Personally, I love it. To me it is the wine of life,
and I make no secret ,of the fact that I love, atten-
tion, adulation and appreciation. Yes, indeed, Aus-
tralia is the artists’ paradise.” The moral of the
lady’s frank confession would make the rabid mum-
mer-worshippers of this country feel uncommonly
small if they were capable of taking it to heart.
“Circle”: Death stilled one of the loudest voices
in Vaudeville, when it dropped the chocolate-colored
Irving Sayles, at Christchurch (M.L.) last' week.
Unlike the bogus and gloomy white' minstrels who
were common in (he business when he was at his best,
poor Sayles always enjoyed his own uproar, and to
many work-wearied people his childish delight in
mere noise was infectious. He was a neat-iooking
colored pusson, particularly about the head, which
never seemed to grow any hair and apparently was
merely there to finish him off and contain that
smooth, gleaming battery of teeth. When Sayles
opened his neat head it was like lifting the lid of a
brown pill-box; and when the preliminary roars and
howls streamed out from between his lips, the listen-
ing patron was apt to imagine himself at the mouth
of a cave in which a lion and a hyena were having a
wild duet. In his later days Sayles got his simple
living by. his uproar, his teeth-exhibitions, shuffling,
cackles and neat double-somersaults—the last reluc-
tantly performed only after much persistent stamping
and whistling by the gallery folk. He only really
failed to hold the house when he allowed his humor-
ous brown countenance to become drawn and sad
and tried to sing seriously and huskily of his “baby”
or his “honey” with the remnants of the voice he had
cheerfully ruined. He hailed from somewhere in the
Southern United States, and commenced here as a
youth way back in the ’Bo’s with the Hicks and
Sawyer minstrels, to which troupe his partner, Charlie
Pope, also belonged. Most of the subsequent years
were spent at the top of his voice on the Harry Rickards
paysheet, but he finished up in M.L. as a Fuller
artist. The quiet of Eternity will be seriously threat-
ened if he has been allowed to take his howl across
the Jordan.
Walter Melville, the prosperous bellow-
dramatist, is refreshingly frank on the
subject of his “art” and his practice
thereof. Giving evidence re the charge
of plagiarism brought by Miss Rees, of
Maoriland, he stated that his “original
melodrama” which he was said to have
borrowed from the lady’s manuscript
“contained stock incidents and conven-
tional. characters.” He was not aware of
the existence of Miss Rees’ play when he
was writing it. He dictated it in less
than a week. Playwriting came very easy
to him. His only difficulty was to find
euphonious names for the characters.”
No intelligent person who has considered
Melville’s bellowdramas in all their naked
foolishness can doubt that he finds play-
writing easy. If they were printed for public cir-
culation they would testify to the truth of Douglas
Jerrold’s dictum, that “easy writing is d —d hard
reading.’’
A Poverty-pointer, talking of plagiarism in the
vaudeville business, said: “Bert Levy, for example,
began drawing to gags and music, on a contrivance
which reflected his work in a magnified form on a
screen. There must be dozens of shameless stealers
of his idea operating in different parts of the world
at the present time.” But as regards the contrivance
aforesaid, The Bulletin’s old friend George Brew-
ster, the Melbourne photographer, writes: “Bert
Levy, as we all know, got the idea from Alex. Laing,
who used it here when he introduced the Pansy Mon-
tague ‘Milo’ turn at the Opera House. Laing had
adopted it from Jocksie Sutherland, the inventor of
the submarine merry-go-round, the target cinema-
tographer, etc., etc. Twenty-five years ago I sold
the same idea to Pain’s Fireworks, for the production
of fire portraits. Pain’s did not use it much, owing
to the chap they put to work it not being quick
enough to get good effect. Where I got the dodge
you can’t expect me to remember after all these
years.”
“Jean S.”: Actor Jimmy Atholwood is the possessor
of an old circus-trick dog which is of such patient
bearing that it listens to Jimmy’s views on Hamlet
without ever answering back. Lately he took his
pet to Her Majesty’s, where a couple of miscreants,
mistaken for comedians, inveigled the beast into
the paint shop and gave it a coat of divers colors.
When Atholwood subsequently discovered that the
post-impressionist tiger he met was his own Fido in
disguise, it seemed as if nothing but the instant
shedding of blood would appease his wounded feel-
ings. A few days later Fido, washed and restored
to his former self, got into trouble on his own
account. The faithful hound accompanied his master
on a visit to Gregan McMahon, and while the
tragedian was talking with' Mac, the dog started
browsing on some plays that were lying around.
When torn from his repast Fido had practically con-
sumed a masterpiece, and Atholwood was terribly
distressed. “My Heavens, Mac,” he said, “the dog
has swallowed your play!” Mac, however, held that
the brute was more to be admired than blamed,
having succeeded in doing what no audience had
accomplished up to now.
Something new in the way of a record was recently
achieved in Berlin by Emmy Destinn, the prima
donna, when she collected £2500 from a flickergraph-
film company for singing a single aria from “Mig-
non” in the cage of a hungry lion, what time anxious
operators made a flick record of the show and frantic
individuals armed with coal-scuttles and other artil-
lery hovered about oh the outskirts ready to slay the
lion on the slightest provocation. The act lasted
five minutes, so Emmy was rewarded to the tune of
£SOO per minute, if rumor isn’t the usual old liar.
In addition to this, the film co. had to insure the
plump singer for £25,000 against death or injury.
As the patrons of the world’s flicker shows, who are
to be thrilled by this film, won’t hear Destinn’? voice,
it seems difficult to understand just why the co. parted
with so much good cash. Surely it could have hired
any female lion-tamer for about 11s. 4d., and rushed
her into the part? She could have sung any old
thing, and it would have been much cheaper and
much more effective, especially if the lion had become
infuriated at the lack of tune and had eaten her.
From London: The Daily Telegraph, reviewing
the drama in 1913, says that most of the English
leaders of the craft have “observed, an . obstinate and
disheartening silence,” whilst the few who have
spoken in plays have said nothing of any account.
The year’s laurels are awarded to Arnold Bennett,
whose “Great Adventure” has passed its 300th night
at the Kingsway, and is still going strong. .T. M.
Barrie has lost ground considerably. His “Adored
One” is dismissed as a “farrago of inconceivable in-
cidents,” while his “Half an Hour” “yielded hardly
any better results on close analysis.” He did a little
less badly in “The Will,” but the idea was transpar-
ently borrowed or stolen from “Milestones.” Bernard
Shaw is also on the down grade, his “Androcles and
the Lion” and “Great Catherine” proving “positively
tedious.” Beerbohm Tree’s Biblical “Joseph and His
Brethren” is summed up as a signal and well-
deserved success—“strongly melodramatic and splen-
didly spectacular, but with little religious signifi-
cance.”

“Y.Z.” : Not all school teachers have sense,
but here and there a wisehead appears among
them. I have sincere respect for the astute-
ness of the young man who sent this letter to
our wash lady: “Dear Madam. I cannot hope
to teach your boy anything while you persist
in dressing him in sawed-off old pairs of his
father’s pants. llow can you expect a boy
to learn arithmetic, grammar and geography
if he has always a pair of trousers on his
mind?”
Every now and then some large, vague
assertion that has about as little foundation
as a balloon floats into notice. The Aus-
tralian Review of Reviews, which seems to
be handicapped mentally by the peaceful
HIGH TREASON.
BOTH (in a hoarse whisper): “ Socialist!!”
opinions of the late W. T. Stead, sent up
this globule of gas in its issue of 12/1/’l4: —
Nowadays every sane person admits' that we are
in no danger whatever from a Japanese invasion, but
that bogey was used so effectively a few years ago
that we are to-day compelling all our lads to train
in arms, and are spending millions where formerly we
were content to spend thousands on military prepara-
tion.
Well, suppose for the sake of argument that
the Review of Reviews (it really should be
be the Retreat of Retreats) is right in its;
tremendous assertion, and that Australia is
in no danger of a Japanese invasion. Is that
any reason for disarming? England used to
be afraid of France, but it didn’t scrap its
navy and its collective gun when it became
good friends with the people across the Chan-
nel ; it was old enough to know that there
were plenty of other potential enemies
around. Later on, it was very bad friends
with Russia ; but when it shook hands with
Russia, it still kept its gun, because it had
found a new enemy in Germany. Guns and
armies and navies are generally accumulated
and kept handy on account of one particular
foe; but thev are intended for any old
enemy that happens along out of the shifting
riot of history. No rich man or nation ever
lacked enemies, and anyhow it is a manly
thing to have a gun in the houfte. We may
all lay down our arms when We get to
Heaven; but there is no certainty about it.
The way the self-elected saints fage together
on earth seems to show that, the real peace-
lover will have to wear an Automatic gud in
his hip-pocket if he wants to spend a tran-
quil eternity.
A DISOBEDIENT CHILD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Miss ’s grandfather was a Church of England
clergyman, and her father was also a man of very
strict ideas. Both her parents were strongly opposed
to her entering the theatrical profession; but Miss
, as an amateur, had felt the lure of the foot-
lights, and considered herself justified in disregarding
her parents’ wishes in the matter. .. .It was several
years before Miss ’s father and mother could
be induced to see their daughter perform, but when
they did they were delighted, and are now very proud
of her. —Melbourne weekly.
Her poor mamma wept bitter tears,
Her dad went white with rage
That night when Birdie said that she
Would like, above all things, to be
A “star” upon the stage.
Her parents’ will the stage-struck miss
Defied. A-thirst for fame,
As chorus-girl she made her bow,
And, aided by large teeth, has now
Achieved a paying name.
Of Birdie are her parents proud.
But, Watching, unbeguiled,
Her acting and brr smiling phiz,
I sadly muse: “How tragic is
A disobedient child!”
VICTORIA.

SOCIETY
An Adelaide man has pot six months -for
being in love. It affected him vocally—Like
a cat. He insisted on standing outside the
Beloved One’s house at night, and making
loud noises, amorous and otherwise; and she
and the neighbors disliked it ,about equally,
aad signified the same in the usual manner.
A cat’s language when cold water is thrown
on it can’t be understood, fortunately, but
his could ; also the stones which he bounced
on their roofs as a practical retort were un-
mistakable. The lady tried to discourage
him by once getting him sent up for a tort-
night. But it was no good so now he has
<;he longer term, during which one or two
doctors are to have a look at him.
“Cebes”: Re “Nestor’s” remarks on the
“Toll of the Surf” (B. 29/1 /’l4). “Nestor”
wants a surf-boat on every beach to save life
and keep people inside the breaker line.
Now, I, who am in the surf every day of the
week, consider that I know something about
if, and declare that “Nestor” is talking
through his hat. For the following reasons:
(1) On the metropolitan beaches there are people
bathing from 4.30 in the morning till 7.30 at night.
On Saturdays and Sundays you have to walk over
the mob inshore to get into deep water; conse-
quently at least two boats would be required to
“patrol” each of the bigger beaches. In all, that
would mean about a dozen boats, with crews. Now,
who’s going to pay?
(2) The breaker line at Bondi after a good south-
erly is out near the point somewhere, and the
breakers are as high as a house. Notwithstanding
that, there’s always somebody bathing, and a man
could get drowned 20 times in as many seconds if he
were 50yds. from shore, where the surf boat couldn’t
float, but a man with a belt and a line can. It has
been done.
(3) “Nestor” forgets that for every life lost, there
are probably at least 50 saved by the surf clubs,
which also stop bathers from taking foolish risks.
Further, where would a surf boat have been in the
recent fatality at Cronulla, or the two-year-old sen-
sation at Coogee? Both those occurred close to the
shore, and certainly not beyond the breaker line. It
is on such occasions that surf clubs are particularly
useful.
(4) “Nestor” has evidently been to the Con-
tinong” : but, from what 1 have heard, and photo-
graphs I have seen, there is no comparison to be
made between Australian surfing and Continental
bathing. The Continental waves are ripples, which
appear to break right on the beach.
Wherefore I adjure “Nestor” to really find
out something about surfing; and when he
has done it, he will pat the surf clubs on the
back, instead of damning them with faint
praise.
“Demos” hands in his regrets
Success has had its usual influence on the Labor
movement, and the effect is displayed in the degen-
eracy of the N. S. Wales conference. There is not
half the 1 real earnesttiess and genuine work shown as
in the poor days of Labor when the mostly unpaid
delegates could not afford to let conference last more
than three or four days, and were remarkably brief
in stating their convictions. Nowadays, a great
deal of the time of conference is occupied in talking
to the gallery (the gallery being the Government) by
aspiring l billet-hunters. When the real, dyed-in-the-
wool Laborite gets up' to ask why the Government
has destroyed plank after plank of the platform,
Bloggs and Biffkins, who are candidates for M.L.C.-
ships, Boshter, who is after a job as an Industrial
inspedtof, and Blank, who has another axe to grind,
leap eagerly to their feet in a desire to defend the
Government. They are prepared to abrogate any
Labor principle, in order to show their prospective
employers their fitness for the job they are after;
and the woods are full of them. So, as a matter of
fact, are the straight-wire Laborites, but they can’t
get rid of them. It is the presence of these time-
servers which has spoilt the honesty and plain direct-
ness of the old-time movement, weakened its solid-
ity, and given Holman and his pals courage to take
liberties- with the platform which formerly would
have unhitched an earthquake.

Canberra’s first organ of public opinion -
i.c., the pioneer issue of the Journal of
the Royal Military College of Aus-
tralia —is a reminder that the Military
Defence system is springing up more rapidly
than the Federal Capital. The temporary
College at Duntroon, which is a boundary
mark of where Canberra city is going to be,
was opened in a half-finished state some 30
months ago, and the
students were under
fire a month later,
when the roof of one
of the wings of Dun-
troon House was
ablaze ' for a while.
There are 112 cadets
in residence at pre-
sent (the full comple-
ment of 135 will be
arrived at in March),
and seven officahs and
eight instructors make
up the existing human
equipment. It looks
like a sound testimony
.o the .superiority of
the Vic. State Educa-
tion that a large pro-
portion of the Aus-
tralian cadets are
Tliusfar
the boys have not
suffered in their stu-
dent life from the dis-
tractions of outside
civilisation. The rail-
way to Queanbeyan —
seven miles off—-is un-
completed. Canberra
consists only of a site,
strewn with building
materials in places.
There are no girls to
be fed with choco-
lates. The four years’
course of a military

dustry and compul-
sory thrift—as much
thrift as he can culti-
vate on an income of
5s. 6d. a day due at

In quite an incidental way attention has
been called anew to the rapacity of that
chartered old cormorant, the Melb. Met.
Tramway Cos., and the culpability of the
Tory Government of old which granted the
charter. The tramcar letter-box system is
about to make its first appearance in laggard
Melbourne on a municipal electric line. The
Malvern Tramways Trust has. graciously
consented to carry letter-boxes in
running between Deepdene and St. Kilda.
Even this had to be “arranged” by P.M.G.
Agar Wynne, though the Government should
obviously have been in a position to put let-
ter-boxes in the cars as a matter of course,
for the convenience of the public. And now
that Melbourne is about to get, in one sub-
urban district only, the benefit of a system
that is not new to Australia, the Postal
Department has explained why it was not
introduced long since on the metropolitan
cable lines. Why .? Because the precious Tram-
way Cos. directors demanded unreasonable
terms for the “concession.” Private Enternrise
stated its terms and told the Government
that it could take ’em or leave ’em. Private
Enterprise, having obtained a monopoly of
the right to supply public tramway service
wherever it chose to operate, would
see public convenience d —d before it
would do aught to oblige the Government.
“Melb”: Wednesday (4th) was a fear-
fully hot day, with terrific north wind. Yet
no fruit was allowed to be sold, by Parlia-
mentary edict, in consequence of the fruit-
erers’ picnic. Now the grapes, pears, plums,
peaches and tomatoes refused to stop rotting
by Act of Parliament. About 25.000 cases
come into Melbourne’s Western Market
every Wednesday. Mildura grapes had to
be sacrificed at 2s. the case. Tomatoes worth
45.. 6d. went for 2s. 6d. Pears were down
2s. a case. It was cruelty both for growers
and eaters, who cannot stuff, two days’ tucker
into one. Heaps of frifit went bad, and
heaps of people ate corned beef when some-
thing else would have done them a lot more
good. Let us crowd all these picnics into a
series and be done with it. Monday, fruit-
erers’ picnic (no fruit or vegetables) ; Tues-
day, beef and fish picnic (no beef or fish) ;
Wednesday, milk and grocery picnic (no
milk, butter, sugar, tea, etc.) ; Thursday,
gas, water and electric light picnic (total
darkness and beer in the evening) ; Friday,
picnic of policemen, tram and railway men,
night watchmen, burglars, etc.; Saturday,
picnic of all other tradespeople, including
publicans and all office employees (no wages
paid, the cashier being at a dance up the
Yarra, and no cheques cashed) ; Sunday,
preachers’ picnic, including archbishops.
MISTER BROWN; A MIXED ODE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The London Quaker deputationist, Mr. Alfred 11.
Brown, believes that the Commonwealth has no need
to resort to arms to defend itself.
In the course of an interview shortly after his
arrival in Sydney, he gave the following illustration
of the Quaker policy of defence: —
“You sometimes see a boy putting up his fists to
hit another boy. But the other boy makes no move-
ment. ITe only smiles. There is no fight. The
bellicose bov becomes ashamed of himself.”
In a word, the Quakers act on the proverb, “It
takes two to make a fight.”
As a result of his investigations in the Northern
Territory, Mr. Brown has come to the conclusion
that it can never be permanently settled by a white
race on account of the climate. — Sydney Telegraph.
Oh, have you heard of Mister Brown,
Who’s lately come to Sydney Town?
His views have all been taken down —
They’re very interesting!
He is a mighty Man of Peace,
Who prophesies that War must cease:
Though men will murder, rob and fleece,
Brown says they’re only jesting!
Brown says (see D.T. interview)
That just to make a fight takes two —
Which means, “Hit me. , and I’ll hit you!”
Such conduct Brown chides strongly ;
But, how about a fight of Three,
Say, you and Robinson —and me?
If Brown thinks fights like that can’t be,
I guess he’s guessing wrongly !
And how about a fight of Four?
(For details, see last Balkans War)
Or five, or six, or even more,
When thrones are badly shaken ?. ...
Good Mister Brown is far ahead
Of times like these ; he’s angel-bred !
But let’s be merciful instead,
And say that he’s mistaken.
Yet, hark ! an unfamiliar noise—
A sweet, mild, bleating voice that cloys
’Tis Mister Brown describing boys—-
The meek, good boys he dotes on ;
The Little Willie of “East Lynne,”
Paul Dombev, Eric and their kin—
All “interesting,” pale and thin —- .
. The kind C. Garbage bloats on !
So good is Mister Brown’s Good Boy
He will not yell, swear or annoy,
Or break his infant sister’s toy
By hammering, or filing;
While as for fighting others —No!
He will not give, or guard, a blow,
But in a gentle Quaker glow
He smiles—and keeps on smiling!
And Mister Brown declares that smiles
Are safer far, by miles and miles,
Than weapons, and vain warlike wiles,
And methods of effront’ry
To keep all foemen far away ;
While boys who can both smile and pray
Are sure to save Australi-a
Or any other country !
But Mister Brown, alas! deplores
The climate of our Northern shores,
Because the hoys that he adores
On them can never settle ;
lie’s never lived there, true; but knows
Intuitively, I suppose—
The only breed of Boy it grows
Is black as any kettle!
Hence, Mister Brown instructs us all
That poor Australia will fall.
Like Jericho’s thrice-famous wall
Before the Hebrew shakers,
Unless —despite our past expense —
AVe do away with all' defence,
And fill the land, say, ten years hence, .
AVith smiling, yellow Quakers! '.
(Three cheers for Brown.)
N.S.W.

From an Australian in London: —
Every tune 1 see a cadging Australian appeal in
England it hurts me, and I feel that it misrepresents
and belittles Australia. The latest to send round
Ih'’ hat is the Bush Book Club, of Phillip-street, Syd-
ney, whose cry goes up in the London papers. Accord-
ing to the two philanthropic ladies whose names are to
it, the club provides books for the bookless in the
backblocks; and here is the list of people who are
to benefit from the circulation of the hat in Eng-
land : “Settlers, schoolmasters, storekeepers, shearers,
sleeper-cutters and station-hands, with their wives
and families.” With the ladies’ fondness for the
letter “s,” why did they leave out swaggies and
sundowners? But do our backblock settlers, school-
masters, storekeepers and all the other s’s really
•desire to get their literature through English charity?
It would be a libel on their independence to say so.
There may be individual cases in which gift books
would be welcome, b.ut the ladies don’t say that —
ikey generalise in wholesale fashion. The classes
they mention are well able to get their literature
through local booksellers, and to pay for it. With a
view to stimulating the flow of English coin into the
hat, the club descants on the woes of the bookless
in the backblocks. They know a young governess
who was unlucky enough to get a billet in a house
without a book in it. “After a year of mental
starvation, she gave up her situation and returned
to Sydney, defeated by the conditions obtaining in the
home of her employers.” And, presumably, she made
straight for the public library—or was it the Tivoli
or the Royal ? The British charitable public is
assured that “far away on the Lachlan River and
up north on the Queensland border the people greet
the advent of the coach which brings them books
with unbounded delight and satisfaction.” At Barrin-
gun, the “arrival .of the first consignment of books,
was made the cause of public rejoicing, and a letter
pathetic in its overflowing gratitude was sent to the
president.” Yet Barringun, though it has only 87
people, and is nearly 600 miles from Sydney, has
two mails per week, and the mail-coach comes from
the adjacent town of Bourke, where literature is
quite procurable. “Books,” they add, “are a distrac-
tion, a comfort, and a source of information and
education. They make people think and give them
something to talk about, and they keep men away
from the nearest store where drink is sold”—a nice
little bit of bait this for Exeter Hall, always the
most easily “worked” section of English society.
By the way, when did the outback Australian start
drinking at the “store?” Where has the good old
put got to?

The blessed Knights of Tythias organisa-
tion, which got f start in ' Melbourne the
other week (and is going to supply in the
other States the unfelt want which the
Masons, Oddfellows, Foresters, Druids and
sich have not dealt with), will celebrate its
50th birthday this month. The Knights are
not a benefit, or provident, or mutual
burying society, and are
not as financially
strong as the Masons,
of course. But they
claim to be numeri-
cally stronger than the
aproned brethren. A
Yankee bloke by the
name of Justice H.
Bathbone was the
founder in 1864. As a
school teacher he be-
came bitten with the
Damon and Pythias
legend, and when he
got a billet at Wash-
ington his idea was to
confine his brotherly
love cult to the U.S.
Education Depart-
ment. Then, as the
ritual caught on, and
outsiders were taken
into Bathbone’s secret
order of Knights, it
was decided to give
, Canada a chance.
After which the move-
ment was extended to
the Philippines, and
now it has reached
Australia. Knight
Brown seemingly is
charged with the job
of planting the gospel
of Pythias hereabouts.
The distinctive charm
of the Order, says
Brown, lies in the
fact that the form of
installation differs for
each novice. No two
Knights are cut on
exactly the same
pattern. But they all
have to ante up the
same installation fee C£3), and the yearly
subscription will be thirty bob in every
case.
The thrifty ways of the Education' De-
partment of Victoria would be amusing if
they weren’t so disastrous. Just at present
those citizens of Crib Point who possess
youngsters of school age are “on strike,” and
though the school and a teacher are present
there are no scholars. For some mysterious
reason the Department seems to have its
knife into Crib Point. Perhaps it dislikes the
name of the place. No permanent teacher
has yet been appointed, though there are 64
scholars. The last 12 months witnessed no
fewer than five different temporary appoint-
ments. During the recent Christmas holidays
the Department got the idea that the Crib
Point scholars were revelling in too much

luxury, so it removed the sanitary conveni-
ences to a new school at Shoreham, 10 miles
away, and left Crib Point without any.
This was the end of the long lane that had

Last week the Victorian Full Court con-
firmed the decision of the local Church of
England Synod to give women the right to
vote at the election of lay members of the
Synod aforesaid. The odd thing was that
any man should have appealed against this
decision. Women are the backbone of all re-
ligious denominations, but ’"ore especially of
the C. of E. sect. They supply about 85 per
cent, of the congregations. They constitute
about 95 per cent, of all the church societies,
including “mothers’ meetings,” the other 5
per cent, being composed of parsons and war-
dens. They do pretty well everything in the
way of visible church work (outside of the
pulpit) except passing round the plate. And
why the taking-up of collections in the taber-
nacle is not performed by dimpled girls,
rather than by wrinkled old women in
trousers, even Mark Antony forgot to men-
tion when he held forth over the dead body
of Caesar, and the great Greek philosopher
Dogsneeze, who lived in a tub, couldn’t have
explained. Of course women should have
votes for the election of lay members of the
blessed Synod. The hens should themselves
be the lay members thereof, not the roosters.
The air of Melbourne is thick with stories
of the man who hires a taxi from the garage
by telephone, in a false name, and ultimately
escapes without paying for the hire. When
he hasn’t worked the false name dodge (some-
times he humorously tells them to charge the
joy-ride to a well-known Wowser) or laid
his plans neatly, he is carried back to the
garage and is tied to a post when he con-
fesses his impecuniosity. After which he is
hosed by order of the taxi-autocrat until he is
nearly as damp as the proverbial drowned
rat.. Some day a captured joy-rider will die
under the hose treatment, and then there will
be an inquiry into the question why taxi-cab
frauds are so much more common than han-
som cab frauds of yore. The explanation, of
course, is in the scandalous maintenance of
the high cost of taxi riding by giving the gar-
ages a monopoly of the trade. If nn»for-cafi>
ranks were placed at the convenience of the
public by municipal order, and tb.e terms,
were cash on delivery of the fare at his des;-
tination, as with the old hansom and fouir-
wheelers, the would-be bilker would have to
scheme darned artfully to get away without
paying for carriage. Under the existing sys-
tem fraud is invited, and the bloated bosses
are charging what they like, and hosing with
impunity any bad debtors who get caught.

“Weary Sub-editor”: There is variety in
social reporting in China. Some of the
Yellow Agony were lately indulging in their
version of “a quiet but pretty wedding”
when pirates fell down on the function, cut
the throats of 22 guests and captured 20
more —probably the best-looking and most
stylishly-dressed girls. Then the wedding
was no longer quiet or pretty, but a real
vivid news item with some print val e. Our
social life needs an infusion of piracy to
brighten it up. My job would be a joy
again if only I could write, “A quiet but:
pretty murder yesterday occurred at Belle-
vue Hill. The bride-elect went away with a
pirate wearing an axe, while the head of
the bridegroom was thrown into the back
yard. The presents were numerous and
costly, including a cruet and two dead
bodies. The wedding breakfast, which was
upset and trodden on during the disturbance*
was supplied by our esteemed fellow-towns>
man, Mr. Jonah Ketch.”

Courtship is becoming a strenuous busi-
ness. At the inquiry into the death of
Elizabeth Johnson, whose throat was al-
legedly cut by her lover at Windsor, near
Brisbane, it transpired that Borneo carried
a revolver and razor to the rendezvous.
Juliet, it next leaked out, had merely a re-
volver concealed in her dress. Poor thought-
less girl! if she had only brought some
cannon and a bulldog she might be alive
and hanpy to-day.
A SONG OF MANNERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The outward manifestation of any great woman or
man is the manners of that woman or man. I would
like to see the cultivation of manners placed in the
forefront of the policy of the A.N.A.”
—Chief Justice Madden {Victoria).
Since Society began,
All the makings of a man —
Any free, enlightened citizen or gaoler—-
Have been credited by fools,
Who were taught it in the schools,
To the handicrafty efforts of the tailor ;
And the tailors, by-the-bye,
Caught on kindly to the lie;
They put it on their fashion-plates and banners.
But the one conclusive test
Of our Greatness at its best
Is the “form” we manifest in social manners.
Not the boots that guard the feet
Of a p'liceman on his beat
Are the slightest evidence of his charnfcter ;
No outward sign of grace
Is the beafd on Wowser’s face —
He had better be clean shaven, like an actor.
Many noble souls and wise
Wear the vilest things in ties,
And many speak of sixpences as “tanners,”
And are capable of slang
In political harangue,
But the Best are somewhat Madden-like in manners.
Though a parting you may find
In J. Madden’s hair-behind,
And he always wears a “frock” with his belltopper,
And when he starts to speak
He is wound up for a week,
Full of language quite supremely fit and proper ;
Not his clothing nor his tongue
Set examples to the young. ,
Lots of lesser lights are fluent spick-and-spanners,
And are equally ornate ;
But the Madden man is Great,
And the measure of his greatness is his manners.
Manners, manners marl; the man,
Our lives by common courtesies we gladden.
They have always marked the man
Since Society began;
But we can’t all have the manners of a Madden.
.VICTORIA.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
“Vic” : One Barker, of Brisbane, a Eureka
Stockader, is in Ballarat. (The list must
be getting pretty well towards the end.)
Barker was on sentry at the Stockade
throughout the Saturday night; bufr before
the morning broke, Lalor said to him, “Go
home, old man, and have a sleep.” So
Barker slept through the battle, which was
at dawn on the Sabbath. Roused by the
rumpus, he went to the Stockade, and saw
the dead lying about. The English com-
manding-officer passed with a file of pris-
oners. “Fall in,” he shouted to Barker.
“No,” says Barker; “I was not in it.” A
trooper then shot him, the bullet passing
right through his body. Even at that stage
the authorities had no idea about Lalor’s
share in the business, but concentred on
Black and Vern; and when the blood-money
was offered it was for one “Lawlor.” Which
recalls that when Robespierre first ap-
peared in the newspaper reports lie was
“Robertspier.”
NANSON, ex-Attorney-General of Westralia
and the largest politician in the continent.
Died at Port Darwin, Paul Foelsche, for
years and years Inspector of Police in the
Northern Territory under the S. Aus.
regime. After retiring from the service the
old man looked south ; but he found that the
climate of Adelaide, Melbourne and there-
abouts_ was quite unfit for a white man, so
he decided to seek old age in the Territory.
But it was a mistake —the North killed him
dead at a mere 83.
By the Oldest Inhabitant:—
The Earl Grey now visiting Australia is a descend-
ant of that Earl Grey who was a member of the Syd-
ney Legislature for Port Phillip in 1848. That is to
say, the Melbourne people elected him as a protest
against the other fellow and against the policy of the
Sydney Government. He beat the other fellow easily,
but when the astonished peer heard that he was a
member of the Legislature at Sydney he said the
matter required consideration. His ghost is still con-
sidering. Other candidates for Port Phillip who did
not know they were in the running were the Duke
of Wellington, Lord Palmerston, Lord Brougham,
Lord John Russell and Sir Robert Peel. Sydney held
an indignation meeting against Earl Grey and threw
decayed vegetables at his reputation; but these capers
helped to make the English people aware that Port
Phillip existed, and shoved the Separation movement
along.
Harry Hoyle, who has become Minister in
charge of N.S.Wales Railways, was the man
who, over 20 years ago, laised Sheol concern-
ing the Baldwin engines introduced from
America by Commissioner Eddy. The en-
suing Royal Commission cost a pot of money
without attaining any good object. But Hoyle
gets some of his own back by finding himself
where McGowen formerly sat in the Labor
Cabinet. In 1894 McGowen outed Hoyle
from Parliamentary life for 16 years by de-
feating him for the single electorate of Red-
fern (which Hoyle had represented as one
of the four Protectionist members for South
Sydney in the early ’9o’s). It was not until
1910 that brother Hoyle scrambled back.

Edward Tregear, ex-Secretary of Labor,
has resigned the presidency of the M.L.
Social Democratic party, which is the poli-
tical wing flapped by the Red Federation.
It has always been something of a mystery
why Tregear joined it, but probably mere
misplaced enthusiasm was at the root of the
mistake. In a pathetic note of resignation
he says:—
My fault was that I was an old man who did not,
or would not, know he was getting old....Then
came the waterside workers’ strike. I had never been
close up to a great industrial conflict before, and
the mental strain was too much for me....Also, I
have found in my experience of the last two or
three years that the workers are so divided among
themselves that to attach oneself or serve with any
particular section is to invite the most bitter hos-
tility from all others.
Yet, it is not very long ago that Tregear
attacked The Bulletin in Sydney Worker
for pointing out the cleavage in the ranks of
Maoriland Labor.
Iceberg Irvine’s first job in
Australia was as tutor to
Henry Ricketson’s children.
Irvine was just out from Ire-
land, and John George Doug-
harty, of Melbourne, took him
by the hand and got him the
Ricketson tutorship. The sal-
ary proposed was £300 a year,
but Dougharty said : “No fear!
Mr. Ricketson is a free-handed man and can
pay, and it will be £400,” and £400 it was.
Australia was good to Irvine from the
beginning.
Ronald Crawford Munro-Ferguson, who
is to succeed Lord Denman as G.-G., is for
the present a mere commoner, the son of a
colonel, in the British Army who became an
M.P. in his old age. The coming G.-G.
started out as a lieutenant in the Guards,
but threw up the Army for politics, and
entered the Commons in 1880 as member
for Ross and Cromarty. Since 1886 he has
sat for Leith. At the general election of
1910 he only managed to get back on a
minority vote. He is 54 years of age, and
was a Lord of the Treasury in 1894. He
owns 26,000 acres of land and has houses
at Kirkcaldy, Evanton and Cadogan Square,
which seems to be a guarantee that he will
now be made a peer. His wife is a daughter
of the Marquis of Dufferin and Ava, one-
time Governor-General of Canada, Viceroy
of India, and Ambassador at Rome. The
lady was born in 1865 and was married in
1889. She has in her veins the blood of
Richard Brinsley Sheridan and of the Irish
Ulster patriot, Archibald Hamilton Rowan
•—to which last it is to be hoped Thomas
Henley won’t object. Her grandmother,
Lady Dufferin, it was who wrote “The
Lament of the Irish Emigrant,” “Terence’s
Farewell,” and other ditties that have har-
rowed the Hibernian soul.
Among the metropolitan boys who won
State bursaries there is only one Eric, only
one Rupert, only one Cyril, and not a single
Percy. There is a truly encouraging number
of Johns and Jameses and Williams. The
complete absence of Percy is a cruel revela-
tion. Perhaps, after all, Percy merely ranks
with Fido.
Says “Nemo” :
Two of the best-known practitioners in the old
police court in Swanston-street, Melbourne, of which
so much has been lately written, on the occasion of
the opening of the big new place in Russell-street,
were Frank Stephen and Turner. Frank became the
father-in-law of John Madden, now Chief Justice of
Victoria. In private he was an agreeable, generous
sort of fellow, with side-whiskers, but in court, pro-
fessionally, he was “a terror.” I-lis style alone
cowed many a witness, and made him think that he
had committed murder and hidden the body when he
really only owed £2. Frank was attorney for many
burglars and footpads in his time. Poor Turner was
the very opposite of Frank Stephen. He was a tall,
quiet, conciliatory man, a barrister of one of the
Inns of Court in London. He had fallen out of his
position in the profession, and now figured as a police-
court attorney. He was always hard-up, and he took
any fee that came to hand —no matter about the size.
But Turner was always “the gentleman.” He knew
all the law that was going, and if he could have
sold his decayed but “genteel” suit and bought a
roar and an utter lack of courtesy with the money, he
would have grown rich.
John Dynon, of Lonsdale-street, Mel-
bourne, whom people used to talk about as
“the Chinaman,” made his will and codicil
sufficiently contradictory or doubtful to bring
it before Justice a’Beckett’s attention in the
Equity Court, Melbourne, a few days ago.
Dynon wasn’t a Chinaman; he was an Irish-
man, who sold china ware. Apparently the
proceeds of the business enabled him to accu-
mulate an estate of £52,000; but, as a matter
of fact, John had another source of
big income of which few were aware. He
was the owner of two valuable pubs, one in
Bourke-street and the other in Lonsdale-
street, which brought him a big revenue.
One was called “The Shamrock.” The pro-
prietor left £SOO a year to his daughter, and
the question raised in court last week was
whether the will and codicil clashed so
badly as to wreck the income. The Judge
reassured the lady.

The case of Richard Denis Meagher,
Speaker of the N. S. Wales Assembly, has
some points of resemblance to that of Thomas
Reibey, a former Speaker of the Tasmanian
Assembly. Meagher came to grief over the
Dean Case, but worked and plotted and
thus climbed to the Speakership on the lad-
der of his sublime impudence. Reibey, an
archdeacon in the Church of England at
Launceston, sued a well-known colonist for
libel and slander, and lost his cause. There
was a lady in the case. He resigned from
the Church, entered politics with some suc-
cess, for he was Premier (July, 1876, to
August, 1877) and “Colonial” Secretary
(December, 1878, to October, 1879). He
had nearly four years as Speaker (1887-
1891). Then he was an honorary Minister
(1894-1899) under Braddon, and even after
that the indomitable Methuselah tried to
form a new party and made a bid for the
premiership.
Died at Rand wick (N.S.W.) last week,
George Rest Kelly, aged 86. “Gee Bung'’
was probably the most ancient pub-keeper
m Australia. Half a century or more ago
he kept the Bathurst Hotel, on the corner of
Market and Pitt streets, where Farmer’s now
is, and at the same time his sign was over
the Royal Victoria Hotel, adjoining the
V ictoria Theatre, where now McCathie’s
nourishes.

The father of Broughton Barnabas
O’Conor, Chairman of Committees in the
N.S.W. Legislative Council, was a church of
England minister, who was prepared for
hi" job by Canon Tom Smith, of St. Bar-
nabas’ Church, George-street West. Hence
the present M.L.C.’s second name. The first
he got from the first Bishop of Australia.
Among a host of Matthewses and
Mathewses in Melbourne, John Mathew, of
the Presbyterian Church, Coburg, is the only
Mathew. He has other claims
to distinction, too. One of
these is the fact that, though
now he is a gentle, black-coated,
greying shepherd, there was a
time when, in quite different
clobber, he herded cattle at the
Back o’ Beyond, and made
strange noises designed to push
unwilling stock on their way to
market. The reverend Mathew
is an M.A. of Melbourne Uni-
versity, and helped himself to
get his degree by grinding at
Latin grammar while the nag
mooched in the rear of the
mob. Long acquaintance with
Black Brother has given him
an unusually extensive know-
ledge of aboriginal manners,
customs and dialects, and he
has published a work which is
highly regarded by the small
circle of those-who-know. Now-
adays John Mathew attends
bazaars, spins sermons, and
looks after the battered Presb.v-
terian souls in Pentridge Stockade; but all
the time he keeps a keen and interested eye
on the doings of Australia at large.
Bishop Stretch, of N. S. Wales, but at
present in M.L. attending the medical con-
ference, obtained his gaiters as chief officer
to the then Anglican admiral of Queensland,
Dr. Webber. His headquarters were at
Roma, once the metropolis of Queensland’s
cattle-duffing industry, and Stretch started
out to civilise the heathen. In the Maranoa,
Balonne and other river districts of the
south-west there was at first much appre-
hension hut Stretch’s manner inspired con-
fidence, and, in the latter years of his reign,
when the “telegraph” brought the news of
his coming no one even bothered to take the
skin of last night’s beast off the fence. If
the enterprising parties thereabouts could
have placed as much faith in the inspector
of police as in the churchman, how much
simpler the problems of life would have be-
come;
A “PERSONAL ADVT.” TO ADAM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
PERSONAL AND MISSING FRIENDS.
BEWARE, tall brown female, venomous snake,
races. Hunted out Bourke Street and City Road.
• —S. M. Herald.
When the first edition of the Eden Banner
(Which was conducted in a very Abel manner)
Appeared on the grass-plot where Adam was reclining
Peacefully at breakfast —unless he was dining —
Cain threw the paper over to his popper,
Which was courteous, also very proper ;
Adam took it up, and liis face was solemn
As he scanned the Banner, column by column ;
All the news of Eden, and the land contiguous—
Some sensational, most of it exiguous-
Till his eye fell on an advertisement
Which implied: Adam ! to you some hurt is meant.
Be on your guard against a tall, brown female,
Also a Snake—sex unknown, perhaps he-male!
Adam smiled grimly, with a large bone toying,
Then to his son he said: “Isn’t that annoying!
This chap who advertises means well —of course, he
does!
But he reminds me of a dead horse, he does!
He should have published it when the Race began—
Had I but seen it then, there had been no Fall of
Man!”
Gain read the ad. himself—then he gently hinted
That when the Fall occurred the Banner wasn’t
printed !.. . .
“Ah!” said the Old Man, “that’s the sad part of it.
Neither your Ma nor I foresaw the start of it!
But, with a bit of luck, on the First Morning
The daily papers would have given warning!”
Envoy.
Thank Heaven, brothers. When we are imperilled,
Some good angel warns us in the Moaning Herald !
N.S.W.
O. C. CABOT.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Bowron Bros. Export and Trading
Co. (M.L. and Sydney) took over a Maoriland leather
and grindery business as at March 1, 1911, paying'
£23,200 for the goodwill and spending another
£BlO7 in preliminary exes. History since then:—
Written
Profits. Dividends. Off. Deficit.
Mar., 1911.. £31,307
Sept., 1911.. £8662 81 p.c. = £7OOO £1295 30,940
Sept., 1912.. 5666 Nil 631 25,905
Loss.
Sept., 1913.. 4818 Nil 1330 32,053
When the first accounts of this co. were put on the
table on January 4, 1912, it was remarked that they
left a nasty taste in the mouth. The genesis of
the co. was bad. At the moment the co. was being
formed the M.L. Income Tax people were pursuing
Bowron Bros, for a very large sum alleged to have,
been evaded ; and the Income Tax people won. Almost
simultaneously some other Bowrons, who had more
or less represented the firm at the other end, got
into very much worse trouble in London; and although
it was alleged that these people had nothing to do
With the present co., the present co. was paying
£23,200 largely for the use of a name which most
people wouldn’t have valued just then at more than
about 21d. And while the new co. was thus pushed
under a cloud, and dragged still further behind it
by the shocking amount spent in flotation exes., the
early management did nothing to improve matters.
All it did was to give a coat of whitewash to the
sepulchre. It announced a div. of 10 p.c. p.a.,
which wasn’t a 10 p.c. div. at all, but one of some-
thing slightly over 8 p.c. ; and even that it should not
have paid. The co.’s first full year was equally
unsatisfactory. As remarked in January, 1912,, the
alleged profit for that term was probably a mere
guess; and the guess turned out to be wrong, where-
fore in the following year £2502 had to be debited
on account of profit previously over-estimated on
shipments afloat. Just before the end of the second
term the management was removed from Sydney to
Maoriland, where the principal business is done; yet,
though the new Board issued the balance-sheet for
1911-12, it now declares that it “only assumed con-
trol when 1912-13 had well begun.” Nadu rally the
new Board won’t accept responsibility for past per-
formances. As The Bulletin has remarked 914
times, the profits shown by a co. carrying merchan-
dise can be made little or big according to the
nature of the man who takes the stock ; an optimist,
or a man anxious to make things look good, is liable
to inflate profits by the simple means of overvaluing
the stock in hand. “The directors,” it is now re-
ported, “are of opinion that a portion of the loss
this year is attributable to the method of last year’s
stocktaking.” The directors make numerous other
remarks with acid in them. “The greatest difficulty
has been experienced in getting correct information";
in fact, it was almost impossible until the appoint-
ment of the present secretary and accountant. .. .The
conduct of the business has been pure guesswork and ,
trust in luck. Buying prices have not been gov-
erned b\' what the co. could afford to give, but by
the market which the co. in many cases created.”
The expenses also appear to have been prodigious.
Against the “working account profit from the tan-
nery and grindery departments” there are these items:
“General exes.,- advertising, directors’ fees, manage-
ment, etc., 11 4,163,” and “discount, exchange and
interest, £666:.”
Nevertheless, “given good and experienced man-
agement, the directors have every confidence that the
business will give a good return on the capital
employed.” The Bulletin not only believes that,
under the conditions named, the business ought to
be profitable, but thinks that, in valuing these £1
shares at about 55., the interested parties have taken
a too-gloomv view. Paid share capital is £139,815.
Deduct £32,053, represented by intangible assets,
and there is apparently £107,762 intact. Besides
that there is £60.999 owing to the 8.N.Z., which is
secured by £75.000 of debentures. Assume that the
stock is now fairly valued, and that the stocks afloat
brought at least the amount drawn against them (the
directors say that they had done so up to Dec. 20).
Then, after deducting the amount owing to the bank
from the value of the stock, the co.’s assets on Sept.
30 were:—
Per
Total. Share.
Book debts £20,071 2s. 1 Od.
Stocks (in excess of liabilities) . . 29,544 4s. 2d.
Tannery and other property and plant 58,147 Bs. 3d.
Total £107,762 15s. 3d.
The Bulletin is, however, not prepared to swear
that any of these figures represent true valuations.
The present directors say the stock has been twice
taken and every care exercised to get a fair figure;
but they don’t mention whether or not they have
valued plant, book-debts, property, etc. Still, if
these are written down very severely, there is a great
deal in excess of the present market valuation. As
for the business, no doubt it may have gone to
pieces ; but the profits ascertained by the Income Tax
Department from 1902 to 1907. and those for the
next two years, certified by Modlin, Crane and Co.,
averaged £24,400 a year. There is, then, or at any
rate has been, big money in the business ; and there
seems no reason why in future there shouldn’t be,
given good management. There will, however, need
to be a fresh issue of capital, probably at a prefer-
ence, in order to reduce the present excessive liabili-
ties; and this probability must of course affect the
position of existing shares.

There is a tremendous row on in this camp. On
January 5 “Jim” Clark, Edwin Munro and others—-
the two named being by far the biggest shareholders
in the co.—issued about the most blistering circular
sent out of late years over the names of responsible
people concerning a co.’s management. As the three
directors said in a circular which followed the other,
if the main statements are true the directors ought
to resign. But instead of proving the statements
untrue, the directors merely deny their truth, mostly
in a g’eneral way, and ask shareholders to “suspend
their judgment until we clear ourselves.” If they
can do it it is a giant pity they didn't do it in the
circular. To reply that, “carefully read,” the origi-
nal circular “is an attempt to deprive us of your
support, and to have that support transferred to
themselves or others,” is no answer at all. Of course,
if they believe the allegations (as, presumably, they
do), they want to get the business out of the hands
of the present directors. What else should they want
to do in such a case?
What seems to have brought the trouble to a
head was the carrying of a resolution—“on the chair-
man’s proxies,” so Clark, Munro and the rest say—
declaring .that no shareholder in any other gas co.
shall be entitled to hold any office or be employed by
this co. Clark and Munro are director? of the
Brisbane Gas Co. One concern’s mains are on the
South suburban side of the river; the other’s (the
Brisbane’s) on the city side. The two are not com-
petitors. In fact, The Bulletin can’t remember
a case where two gas cos. are competitors. The
general thing is to welcome on the Board of a small
concern a man who has had large experience, especi-
ally in big concerns. Thus Swinburne, now an Inter-
State Commissioner, was a director on many Boards
where his experience as a gas, power and water-
supply engineer was invaluable. Incidentally, Swin-
burne seems to be interested in the South Brisbane
Co. ; anyway, he wrote ridiculing the prpposal men-
tioned above. However, it was carried, and the
circular explains the way of it. If this motion
was forced on really to block the two largest share-
holders, or any other eligible shareholders in particu-
lar, from disturbing the present directors in their
seats, then the sooner they are disturbed the better.
True, a man’s intentions can’t well be proved. But
if the reason he assiglns is ridiculous, then a fair
conclusion is that the actual reason is the one
alleged by his critics. Apart from the other allega-
tions, the directors have a strong prima facie case
to answer here.
The table shows practically nothing written off
the assets for years ; on the other hand, on one occa-
sion £14,663 was written on to the assets. There
is nothing necessarily wrong about this, though.
Many a gas co., anxious to hide its profits, has,
dropped them into new mains, plant, etc. ; and it is
a fair enough thing for a co. to presently transfer
to reserves the amounts thus spent in excess of the
sum necessary to counterbalance actual depreciation.
Whether in this case depreciation has been thus pro-,
vided for it is impossible to tell from the
Dozens of times this paper has drawn attention to
the fact that Australian gas cos’, accounts have
hitherto been for flic most part so badly presented
that only a very few people behind the scenes could
have any idea of the true position of any particular
co. ; and the Queensland accounts have been worse
than most. In fact, changes having recently been
made in the law in N. S. Wales, the holder of
Queensland gas shares is now more poorly protected
than any other gas investor in •'Australia.' It is all
very well to plead that it is advisable to hide the
facts from the tiger of municipal Socialism, who
might otherwise be aroused to fury ; but in hiding
the facts from the tiger the management hides
them also from the general ruck of shareholders,
and it can easily be seen how knowledge on the
part of the very few and ignorance on the part of the
very many could be made of great advantage to the
few. In any case a thorough overhaul of the South
Brisbane Co.’s affairs by an independent accountant
of ln>h standing is urgently called for; and inci-
dentally shareholders should join in asking the Govern-
ment to bring in a Bill to provide for the publica-
-tion of more ample accounts.
