The accounts now published are very different vege-
tables from those put on the table in the days when
there was no Gas Act, and the one idea among gas
cos. was to disclose as little information aspossible. The
present statements follow, generally, the lines adopted
by the Melbourne Gas Co. The general principle of the
Gas Act (1912) is to limit the dividend on old capi-
tal to 10 p.c., that amount being payable when gas
is sold at 3s. 6d. per 1000 ft., and allowing a rise of
i p.c. in the div. for every reduction of Id. in the
price of gas below 3s. 6d. For new ordinary capital
the div. is fixed at 7 p.c. (with the same proviso as to
increase) and for pref. capital at 5 p.c. No, reserves
may in future be capitalised ; hence nothing is to be
gained by piling up reserves. The idea is that, when
the business of selling gas becomes profitable enough
to permit of more than the abovementioned divs.
being paid, then the public shall enjoy the benefit
of a reduced price ; and to that end the accumulation
of. profits is restricted. The co. may pay for mainte-
nance, etc., of course; but there can be only three re-
serves—(l) for special purposes, limited in the
afro-regale to 10 p.c. of the share capital and pre-
miums on shares;(2) formal reserve, for which pay-
ments are not available until gias is sold, below the
standard rate ; (3) divisible profits account, really the
unappropriated Profit and Loss balance, which may not
at any time exceed a year’s div. at the standard rate.
The co. now for the first time shows its expenditure
on maintenance and reuairs; and. further, it writes
£5OOO off for general depreciation. It is very doubt-'
fill whether the Act allows of any sum being written
off against depreciation, and it seems ■ probable that
this £5OOO will have to be transferred to the special
purposes fund, which is a sort of contingency re-
serve. There ought to be a depreciation or replace-
ment account, because it may not always be conven-
ient to charge a big renewal against one term’s
revenue; but apparently the law doesn’t recognise
this.
The share capital now is £1,675,000 in “A” and
“B” shares, with a 10 p.c. standard, and £255,000
in “0” shares, with a 7 p.c. standard. All are £6
shares fully paid. After providing for the divs., pav-
ing for current repairs and maintenance and putting
£5OOO by against general depreciation, there is only
£1986 left over. This is a very narrow margin; but
whether it is due to any extra outlay on repairs and
maintenance The Bulletin doesn’t know. The Gas
Act is, so far as its general principles are concerned,
very excellent law ; but it will be simply impossible to
prevent a co., if it feels that way, improving its
plant, and thus reducing its visible profits, by charging
up to repairs and maintenance some of what might
more strictly be charged to capital. Certainlv the
co. would not be likely to unduly inflate its profits
just at present, application having been made to
raise the standard price of gas on the ground presum-
ably that at 3s. 6d. per 1000 the co. can’t pay the
higher wages fixed by Wages Boards and at the same
time maintain the standard dividend.

But you’ll probably get a better view of 10 years’
history from this brief comparison:—
Dec.,’o3. Dec.,’l2. Dec.,’l3.
Liquid assets .. £54,338 £84,372 £87,852
Liabilities . . 15,568 Nil Nil
Surplus of liquid assets £38,770 £84,372 £87,85’
Meantime the co. has maintained its fine property in
Collins-street, which is certainly worth more now than
it was 10 years ago. Out of undivided profits it has
thus accumulated £49,082 in the 10 years. More-
over, it has accumulated that amount in hard cash,
which has been invested rather less than half in mort-
gages and rather more than half in public .securities. •
Lor a co. which has paid steady but gradually in-
creasing dividends on a capital of £90,000,' this
accumulation of a solid £50,000 iri 10 years pretty
well gives you the character of the co.—a co. which,
having a full appreciation of its functions and obli-
gations, aims at solidity above all things. It is
gratifying that it gets so much encouragement to
keep on with that policy. Estates in hand as at
December, 1903, were valued at £7,324,006. The
total now is £10,199,585. The increase should have
been greater; still to get nearly £3,000,000 of extra
business is a good performance. The persons inter-
ested in those estates have the satisfaction of know-
ing (1) that the work of looking after the pro-
perty, upon which many of them entirely depend, is
in the hands of men trained for the job; (2) that
beneficiaries are protected against the consequences
of misbehavior, in their -trustees by a paid capital
of £90,000, reserve funds of over £50,000, and
£210,000 callable from the shareholders; and (3)
this trustee cannot die or shift camp to Guam at an
inconvenient (or any other) time. From the share-
holders’ point of view, the position is first-rate. The
capital and reserves are in public securities, mort-
gages (£3S.2IS) and Coll ins-street property, which
has been steadily written down to what seems to be
well under market value; and, in addition to the ex-
cellent security for the principal (assuming that the
mortgages are right), there is the steadily growing
business which yields a profit very rarely less than
half as much again as the dividend hitherto paid.

Those mutton nies that Mrs. Sargent used to make
and sell in a little George-street shop have grown to
big things ; the co., which was formed late in 1908
to take the business over, has now about 35 shops
and cafes in Sydney and a couple in Melbourne.
Starting with a capital of £BO.OOO, of which no less
than £46,235 was represented by nothing but a
name, the c 5. has been able to maintain a 10 p.c.
div., and accumulate £33,478 of profits towards
replacing the intangible goodwill asset by something
more solid. Not that, even now, in spite of the
heavy reduction of the original deficit, the assets
are of the gilt-edged variety. There are freeholds
valued at £45,999, but on the other side there are
mortgages and other liabilities totalling £47,225.
For the rest, the canital is represented by leaseholds
and improvements thereon, £20,600; furniture and
plant, £42,740; stock, £11,125; and cash, etc.,
£.19,005. The leaseholds of course involve an amorti-
zation fund; in other words, this is a wasting asset
which has to be gradually written off out of
profits. Furniture and plant are other items of un-
certain qualitj'. While the; business is a going con-
cern they are doubtless worth the value put upon
them ; but if anything bad happened to the business
(and bad things do hapoen to most businesses sooner
or later, mostly when the people who built them up
are not on hand to preserve them), the crockery with
Sargent’s name on it wouldn’t be worth a very
great deal. The stock, cash, etc., may be accepted
as worth 20s in the £ ; but they run to only about
£30,000, and the capital is £lOO,OOO. However,
there is no falling-off in the profits; those for the
past half-year beat all past records. And there is no
indication of an early change. Australia’s habit of
feeding away from home is growing, largely, no
doubt, because of the great scarcity of Mary Ann.
Bad times will, nevertheless, make a big difference
to the trade. Food certainly isn’t a luxury that one
does without when times 'are not prosperous; but
thousands of the people whose silver goes through
Sargent’s cash-registers would either have a snack at
home or bring a bit to town in a paper parcel if
they didn’t have more money just now than they are
used to. The Bulletin hasn’t the faintest idea hpw
long the business will remain in its nresent condi-
tion. All it can say is that that condition is highly
profitable, that the hold of any catering firm or name
on the public is notoriously precarious, and that,
viewed apart from the business, the assets of the
co. are not of a quality that offer the best, or even
second-best, security for an investor’s principal. In
short, the investment involves a higgish risk, for
which only a substantial return is sufficient compensa-
tion. It isn’t a line for people who want security
first and make the interest return a secondary con-
sideration.

Six months more and, all going well, that part of
this office which attends to the Wild Cat will leave
off work to give R. and W. three cheers. The co.
has put up a really fine performance, thanks largely
to the Greggs, who have been managing directors for
many years. Starting off with no less than £57,500
represented by goodwill in a total capital of £60,000,
it has accumulated profits until tangible assets have
taken the placeof the vast amount originally represented
by nothing but the ephemeral value of a trade name.
At times, hitherto, the accumulations have been
mostly hard cash on deposit; just at present, how-
ever, £42.28G is shown as “advances on properties”—-
not as good as cash, of course, but probably quite
safe, and made with an eye to profitable business. It
may be taken for granted that, at any rate, there
is nothing deliberately in the way of speculation
about it ; for this co. has kept out of the land
gamble, and has diligently pursued the perhaps less
fascinating but mighty commission. Including this
amount of advances as liquid, we get this result: —
1903. 1908. 1913.
Liquid assets .. £28,414 £32,631 £74,315
Liabilities . . 9,720 7,595 10,123
Surplus .. £18,724 £25,030 £58,192
There was a time when it looked as though the co.
might very well distribute some of the capital, since
the bulk of it was nearly always 'on fixed deposit ;
but apparently the directors see a way of profitably
employing most of it at times in the business. As
for the profits, the maintenance of recent figures can't
be expected; we have been having a land and pro-
perty boom, and booms are bubbles that must break
But the margin of profit in excess of the dividend
has been so wide, and there is so much more hard
money nowadays to earn interest for share-
holders, that only a very bad misfortune, or a whole-
sale abandonment of the policy hitherto adopted,
could null the dividend below its present level.

When this co., with others, formed the Carltpn and
United Breweries, Ltd., better known as the Melbourne
Brewery Combine, it was understood that various
breweries would be closed, Melbourne being over-
stocked with those factories. And as it was likely
that this co.’s premises wouldn’t be wanted, and as
they weren’t worth what they cost if used for any
other purpose, it was decided to set aside a certain
sum for writing them down. This sum was really
provided by the goodwill loading in the C. and U. B.
shares handed over to this co. That is, the Combine
handed this co. £72,500 in shares (face value), of
which about £48,000 was not represented by tangible
assets handed over. This amount was put to an
Assets Realisation Fund, and was not included with
the reserves in the table. Now, however, the property
has been re-valued, and the £23,208 required to write
the premises down from book to actual value has
been transferred from the fund. The balance has
been transferred to ordinary reserves, and part of it
has then been used to restore the capital which was
written down from 20s. to 11s. 6d. per share in
1897. So a 6 p.c. div. is now worth a shade more
than a 10 p.c. div. was until the recent restora-
tion of capital; and in starting off with an 8 p.c.
on the enlarged capital, equal to nearly 14 p.c. on
the old, it looks as if the co. has bitten off quite
as much as it can chew. Actually the dividend wasn’t
earned ; though it is true that the exes, were (say)
a couple of hundred pounds more than usual—no
doubt on account of the reconstruction. Giving that
in, the margin is excessively small; and a small cut
in the div. from the Combine, or a shrinkae-e in the
£lOOO or so received by way of rent, would compel
a reduction. As for assets, the £78.825 of capital
and the £lB,BBl of reserves are represented now by
£76,125 (face value) of Combine shares, £16,000
of brewery premises (severely written down), £4221
of pub property and a few sundries —cash and ad-
vances mostly. In view of the large amount of
water in the Combine stock it is impossible to regard
the assets as very solid, or the security as at all
gilt-edged.

The co.’s printers and painters would make it
Aaron’s; but they got it right at last. Of course,
no Aaron ever could be a hotelkeeper. Aaron it was
,\vho to Moses was “as a mouth,” Moses being of a
slow tongue and not eloquent; whereas the hotel-
keeper can supply everything but the mouth. For
many years the capital was £17,500, of which only
£2500 was represented by assets that you could
pour out of a bottle or sit on while you waited for
the other man to ask you about another. Then, in
order to get a renewal of the lease, the co. had to
spend a lot of money. More capital was, therefore,
got in, and at the same time or thereabouts the
directors wisely wrote off some capital so that they
might reduce the inflated goodwill account. More
wisely still, it was decided a little over a year ago
to establish a sinking fund “to provide for the return
of the capital at the end of the lease” (1930), and
apparently £225 a half-year is being put to the
fund. Unfortunately the exact figure can’t be
specified, because the amount set aside (shown as
written off) is lumped for depreciation of tangible
furniture as well as intangible goodwill. What is
called for is a separate account showing the progress
of the sinking fund, with the investments into which it
has been put. In that case the interest on these in-
vestments wouldn’t go through Profit and Loss at
all; and then the thing really would be a sinking
fund. Still, there has been a vast improvement in
the financial management of the concern. And, judg-
ing by the profits, in the general management also.
Reckoning that £225 a half-year is necessary to
restore the capital not represented by portable assets,
and that £75 should be put aside for depreciation
not covered by the half-year’s replacements, a profit
of £1065 could pay a 124 pc. dividend. That
amount certainly has very often not been realised in
the past; but of late it has been bettered. And even
£1375 in a half-year seems distinctly on the small
side for a pub. with Aarons’ connection, position and

Water-Supply engineers will be interested to learn
that further evidence proving the durability of W. I.
and Steel Pipes when coated by the Asphaltum pro-
cess, has just been obtained by the engineers of the
Melbourne and Metropolitan Board of Works. Certain
alterations were made to the main which supplies the
City of South Melbourne with water, and consequently
some pipes were taken up. These pipes were made in the
year 1889, and were coated with Asphaltum composi-
tion in the usual way, and they were laid in ground
that was particularly unfavorable to the preservation
of any unprotected metal, being originally swampy
land, containing much vegetable matter, and filled
with tidal dredgings. On examination, these 24-year-
old pipes were found to_ be in an excellent state of
preservation, and the Board’s engineers were so satis-
fied with their condition that they decided to use
them again. They were, therefore, removed to the
works of Messrs. Mephan Ferguson Proprietary Lim-
ited, at Footscray, Victoria, where these 24-year-old
pipes were cleaned, retested, arid recoated with
Asphaltum composition in a new and improved pro-
cess. They are now being relaid in a position where
they will have to withstand a working pressure of
1351 b. to the square inch. That the Asphaltum pro-
cess for coating pipes is rust-proof and thoroughly
efficacious is proved by the foregoing.*

The profits are shown “after providing for wear and
tear and depreciation,” but what the provision
amounts to no man knows who doesn’t know much
more than the published accounts tell him. A mine
is a wasting asset; but the accounts don’t say
whether the management is writing down this pro-
perty to counterbalance the depreciation due to the
extraction of coal which, having been dug out once,
is gone for ever. There have been Newcastle cases
where a mine was alleged to represent almost t :e en-
tire capital of the co. up to the very moment w e i
the announcement was made that there were o ■ ■'/
three or four years’ supplies left in it. As a matter
of fact, in every case of that sort, the co. had been
paying dividends out of capital. The Bulletin
doesn’t know whether the same thing is being done
in this Wallsend case or not. The property is cer-
tainly not being obviously written down:—
Dec., ’O3. Dec., ’l3.
Liabilities •• •• •• £7BO £6,246
Mining and fixed property . . 89,790 91,890
Liquid assets .. .. •• 77,533 82,680
So mining and fixed property (plant, etc.) stands in
the books at more now than it did 10 years ago,
despite the extraction of a few hundred thousand
tons of coal. Still the mine property may have been
written down, the figures being maintained by ex-
penditure on railway and other property. It is a
case in which the ordinary investor is utterly in
the dark. If he likes to trust partly to luck and
partly to the ability of the co. to use its big cash
balance in picking up other properties in order to
carry on its unquestionably fine and well-established
business, well and good.
Important talks are going on between the
Broken Hill Prop., B. H. South and North B. H.,
outside the Sulphide Corporation the three biggest
mining concerns at Broken Hill. Neither South nor
North has a smelting works or refinery; both sell
their output of concentrates in the open market,
where the B. H. Prop, competes with the foreigner,
the foreigner being in this case German or Belgian.
The B. H. Prop, has very fine works at Port Pirie,
and the proposal now under discussion is that they
should be transferred to a Trust embracing the three
cos. It is ■ greatly to be hoped that the scheme
comes off. The Pirie works are now far in excess
of the B. H. Prop.’s requirements, so far as its own
ore is concerned, and so long as it has to depend
on casual purchases and contracts the business is
liable to be intermittent, which isn’t a good thing
for the town and the State where the works are.
■ * * *
At Newcastle there are 16 hydraulic
cranes, one steam crane and a McMyler hoist; but
the . lot have been unequal to their job of late. Last
week the export was 134,131 tons, as against a
previous record of 126,259 tons for .a week’s loading.
* * * *
The mostly' English-owned Mount Boppy,
the biggest gold-producer in N. S. Wales, has had
to close down owing to the long drought in the
Cobar district. Except for a few patchy showers
there has been no rain worth mentioning in that
part for nearly nine months. For the last six or
eight weeks the mine plant was kept going on the
town supply, but that at last got so low that the
co. had to be called off, and the dregs left for the
townspeople.
* i ♦ * *
At Lionsville, in the Grafton district
(N.S.W.), a certain amount of development is being
done at the Mountain Maid and Garibaldi Consoli-
dated. The reef in these shows, though only inches
in width, shows gold freely. Some good small crush-
ings have been put through the Pucka battery, which
is now being removed to the Garibaldi; but opera-
tions are not on a sufficiently large scale to really
test the value of the various shows.
The Block 14 Torrington (N.S.W.)
seems born to trouble as the sparks fly upward. The
suction-gas power plant is the latest item responsible
for a temporary close-down. Even with everything
in good running order the outlook is poor, the ore
available being horribly low in wolfram and bis-
muth. The mill has now been running for some
months and the concentrates obtained are quite
inadequate to provide any. return for the outlay in-
volved in the erection of treatment plant. Unless
the contents of the ore show considerable improve-
ment the future is of most interest to the under-
taker.
. * •* *
The brightest days of Y Water (N.S.W.) are
past. Besides the obvious fact that the sluic-
ing area is diminishing, it is pretty well known
that the best available ground was treated first to
give the co. a lift over its initial troubles. What is
left to go through the boxes will not only not be
up to past average, but costs will be higher.

And to that loss you must add on a trifle for depre-
ciation. Nor is the future bright. No. 1 plant is
up against a granite bar, and if borings don’t prove
better ground beyond it, it won’t be worth working.
No. 2 plant is on richer ground (nine-tenths of lib.
to the yard), but it is infernally hard stuff. The
eo. has been looking around lately for another
block, and is going to exercise an option on a
fragment. Meantime, exclusive of stores, liquid
assets are about equal to the overdraft and other
liabilities. The paid capital is only £12,500, so
the co. doesn’t owe shareholders anything.
Hawkins’ Hill Reward (N.S.W.) has
been crushing trial lots from the Frenchman’s reef,
on which for some time past they have been driving
north. A crushing of 95 tons for 112 oz. (month)
follows 65 tons for 106 oz. (three weeks). These
crushings, with 20 head of stamps available, are
ridiculouslv small if the reef now prepared for stop-
ing for a'length of 200 ft. is all that it is reported
to be. From the tonnage one would say that it was
either much smaller or very erratic. The main
unwatering level on Frenchman’s reef is now to be
driven towards the Robert Emmet crosscourse, at
which point a rich patch is expected. The capital
is £20,000 in 80,000 shares of ss. each, 17,560 of
which are held in reserve.
In a circular to shareholders Doolette the
elder attacks “interested parties who are spreading
prejudicial reports about the Bullfinch and its plant.”
Doolette laughs at the allegation that the present
mill cannot treat sulphides, but says .nothing about
the yarn that the new manager’s estimate of ore
reserves falls far short of that of his predecessor, the
late Fred Morgan. That is the rock on which the
market is said to have gone to pieces.
* * * *
A man on the spot writes: —Bullfinch’s
creep isn’t likely to interfere with operations to any
appreciable extent. A quantity of ore has of course
been lost for the time being above No. 2 level, but
there is ample stuff available in other parts of the
mine to keep the mill going at its full capacity. It
has been alleged that the creep affected the plant,
but so far there is no sign of this.
* * * *
Edna May (W.A.) has got its No. 2 level
out 100 ft. A big body of 2o'z. stone is still showing
in the south-west face, but in the north-east end a
crosscourse has come to light and displaced the reef.
The latter, however, is thought to have been thrown
a little to the north. The co. proposes to start
sinking the shaft from 150 ft. to 250 ft. this month,
and to install a plant to deal with the sands and
slimes accumulated and current. Local people who
claim to be in the know figure on the monthly out-
put being raised to £lO,OOO, and the profit to
£6OOO, with an additional £l5OO surplus when the
treatment of residues begins. Ore reserves are esti-
mated at 12 months’ supply.
* * * *
Paringa ’ (W.A.) reports a royalty revenue
for the year of £1468. Since 1909, when the co.
successfully engineered its last reconstruction, 27,000
tons have been extracted by tributors and treated
for a return of £35,836. One lode, Geach’s, con-
tributed ■ 9080 tons and £23,353 tp these totals.
Payable ore is still being met with in various parts
of the.workings on.tliis and other ore bodies, but the
management admits that, lately, “expectations have
not been fully realised.” The directors are consider-
ing the recommendation of the consulting engineer to
erect a small plant to deal partly with the ore now
being raised by tributors and partly with low-grade
stuff which at present does not pay to work and
treat at outside mills. Owing to the erratic nature
and value of the mine’s ore bodies, no estimate of
reserves is attempted.
* * * *
Paringa (W.A.) has sold its open-cut and
surface-rooted Cassidy Hill lease to a tributor who
has made a living out of it for some years. The
show was an impossible one for a cp; to try to work
at a profit, as nearly all the gold is found in small,
patchy leaders, which are frequently far between.
*** . . *
An annual conference of leaseholders and
prospectors sitting at Coolgardie has-been giving the
Scaddan Government a piece of its mind. One dele
gate complained that struggling leaseholders get
frowns instead of funds from the present Ministry,
and were unduly stirred up for rentals ; another con-
demned it for winking at, instead of putting a stop
to, speculative pegging at new rushes ; a third,
apropos the State mills, complained because the de-
duction for gold in tailings was too great and the
extraction allowance too small; several growled
about the delay in the payment for tailings; others
clamored for reduction : in rentals ; and nearly all
agreed that the Government was discouraging the in-
dustry, and that, the Department was being run by
officials and not by the Minister. In reply, the
conference is referred to by its Ministerial and other
opponents as a gathering of storekeepers, publicans,
etc. ; but as it is generally the local pub- and store-
keepers who back the prospector, they have a better
right to attend such a gathering than most people.
* * * *
The rise in Boulders is attributed in the
West to favorable reports about the co.’s Alaska
option. There has certainly been nothing in the
shape of developments at Kalgoorlie to justify any
rise. The breaking of new ground in some of the
higher “lower” levels is certainly being attended
with fairly good results ; but there is nothing in the
bottom. This year’s returns will probably average a
dwt. less than last year’s, and next year’s a dwt. or
so less again.
* * * *
Front Yilgarn (W.A.) : The outlook has
never been brighter. A number of the co.-owned
shows are looking well, and many of the smaller ones
are being nibbled at. Several are expected to be
floated shortly. Three boring plants are in operation
on various parts of- the field, new treatment plants
are about to be installed on the Edna May Central,
at Weston’s ; the Transvaal, at Southern Cross; and
the Butcher Bird, at Mount Jackson ; and systematic
prospecting is going on everywhere. The acquisition
of the Corinthian by the adjoining Corinthian North
will mean a considerable increase in the reserves of
the latter show ; and as working costs have been re-
duced to a low figure, big profits are expected to be
made —for a time at least. The final payment on
the Comet lease is due next month, and as the option-
holding Marvel Loch Co. has made another call it
looks as if it intends to complete the deal. At pre-
sent portion of the show is being worked by tributors,
but their lease is just about up. The note of warning
sounded by The Bulletin at the outset in regard to
the Comet has been fullv justified by results. The
reef is disturbed by granite bars at a comparatively
shallow depth and is difficult to follow. There is
some talk of getting Dr. Maclaren to look into the
matter. On present appearance the property has no
depth.
“Oof Bird” (W.A.) : Fancy values are
being reported by Adelaide syndicates holding options
at Holden’s Find, and the market has jumped in
consequence. One expert—Henderson, of the Mara-
roa —testified to the existence over good widths of
values ranging from 575. to 91s. ton; yet he
guardedly adds that “his impressions' are, on the
whole, favorable.” The naked truth about the
matter is that neither Henderson nor any other
engineer of standing has yet been induced to let him-
self go on the question of prospects. Appearances
are against the field being much in depth, and some-
thing more than the statement that, “on the whole,”
someone’s impressions are favorable is needed before
the public should rush in with big money.
* * * *
The Hodgkinson revival in N. Queensland
awakens many old memories, for between 1877 and
1912 this field provided 130,358 tons of stone averag-
ing £4 4s. The Tyrconnel, near Kingsborough, has
started crushing. It continues to open up a big reef,
in places 6ft. wide, at the 150 ft. level. Reserves
are small, because the show has been much worked in
the shallow ground, and it is impossible to say how
far the shoot will extend. The New Minnie Mox-
ham is also a revival. Its two levels are at 134 ft.
and 215 ft. on the underlay. At the lower level the
drives total 100 ft. in length, and show a reef 18in.
thick. So confident are the directors that they have
purchased a Cradle crusher in imitation of the Red,
White and Blue plant at Maldon (Vic.). No one
at this stage can, however, guarantee a tonnage of
payable stone. The quantity as yet proved is very
moderate.
Big low-grade propositions at the Oaks
goldfield (N.Q.) supply oread and dripping. Last
year 7182 tons were crushed for £11,899. The claim
that shows the most stone is the Mountain King,
which in. 12 months put through 2110 tons for
£1798. Mining (or, rather, quarrying) costs here
are low. Oaks Amalgamated crushed 1249 tons for
£1392. The short supply of water partly accounts
for the small tonnage. The shareholders are a hope-
ful crowd, and a much improved milling plant will
shortly start crushing for them. Thompson’s Oaks
put through 185 tons for £1053. Speculators have
lost pretty heavily on both the Amalgamated and
Thompson’s. The Amalgamated was reported to have
big ore reserves, but they proved very low grade;
and Thompson’s Oaks never had any reserves of much
consequence, though its* rich little crushings pro-
vided good bait.
* * * *
It looks as though what little there is of
the congestion disease in Queensland is going to be
partly cured by Nature. This interfering individual,
according to Government Geologist Dunstan, hid coal-
seams, up to about 45ft. in thickness, under an area
of 40 square miles near Bowen, and Dunstan has only
recently found them. According to an official state-
ment, “When a large extent of country between
Bowen and the Central Railway line and further
south is prospected, the probable coal reserves of the
State will be found to be enormously increased beyond
the estimate thus far given; and with an area of coal
country greater than that of N. S. Wales, there is
every probability of our cpal reserves being found
equal to, if not greater than, that State as explora-
tion and development increase.” As the new coal-
areas lie back of Gladstone and Bowen respectively,
and as the best natural deep-water ports on the
Queensland coast-are at those two places, while the
Brisbane River has to be dredged continuously, and
the back-of-B.risbane coalfields raked with a small-
tooth comb, it looks as if something like Divine in-
tervention is happening to help the neglected North.
The Mammoth, the Glasgow-owned North
Queensland copper group into which Australia
bought pretty heavily last year, got its first real
market attention lately when ' shares flopped from
40s. to about 265., partly on Glasgow sales, partly
on the unexpected suspension of smelting. The
Glasgow sales weren’t important, and the stoppage
of smelting was misunderstood. Audley Smith, the
very competent man who is the co.’s consulting
engineer, in a report made last month, says: “The
first plans of the co. were to mine fairly clean ore
and pick at the surface up to 9 p.c. or 10 p.c.
Recent developments have proved the. ore to be much
larger” ; and, as experiments showed that the stuff
could be cheaply dressed up to 9 p.c., smelting was
discontinued, pending the completion of a dressing
plant. He estimates that 43,000 tons of ore will be
dressed up to 23,000 tons of 9 p.c. ore ; and, altogether,
he puts the quantity available for smelting (includ-
ing that 23,000 tons) at 39,800 tons. This is ex-
clusive of “probable” ore, which has been pierced
but not opened up, arid on present appearances should
double the quantity mentioned above. Audley Smith,
while not committing himself to figures regarding
this “probable” ore, thinks well enough of the pro-
spects to recommend better equipment, including a
converter plant for the production of blister copper.
The mines, he says, “are still new ventures,” and
will call for a lot of development; but “they are
very promising on account of the large quantity of
interesting ground which has yet to be opened up
and the results which have already been attained.”
Costs he puts at £36 2s. 6d. per ton, or £5 less
with local converting; and he values the gold
and silver in the ore at £ll 13s. per ton of copper.
With copper at £65 this would give a profit of
£lB 11s. 6d. per ton, or £5 more with the converter
plant going. This means a profit in sight of (say)
£70,000 for a group of mines which have still a
great deal of promising ground to develop.
* * * *
Six months’ hard trying bv South Ger-
man, Maldon (Vic.), to tone up the market on the
strength of Baxter’s tribute hasn’t had much result.
The rise from 2000 ft. has been on improving values,
but all the puffing hasn’t convinced the unbeliever
that there is any permanency about it. The co.’s
own tally for the half-year was 122 tons from
2000 ft. for 440z.,' while tributors got 2041 tons
handled for gold worth £3742, an average of about
365. a ton. This grade would pay well, but the
trouble is to get enough of it. Maldonites want the
co. to try reefs west. As the Board isn’t too flush
of cash,' it won’t frivol money in theorising, prefer-
ring to hang on to the tributors’ shoot as long as
that gives a hope to build on.
Dick Hamilton, of the Boulder, has gone
back to the West, after his seizure of the Magdala-
Moonlight has-beens. at Stawell (Vic.). His deputy
is Gregory, of the Kalgoorlie staff, whose duty it is
to perforate the Cross, the Scotchman’s and other
reefs with the diamond drill. Samples of the faces
of the old stopes have been pronounced O.K. Hence
the moving of the diamond drill into the mines.
Six months ahead Stawell will know if it is to get
£200,000 of Boulder cash, or is to write itself
down as a derelict mining tramp. A million and a
half were poured into Stawell in the old days,
chieflv from where the flat reef functioned with the
vertical. “Saddle-reef” Dunn is cocksure that the
district system comprises huge saddles and “the
best-defined ore channels in the Commonwealth.”
Think how old George Doolette will roll this out
to London shareholders if the drill shows the big
reefs to be more than puff! The scheme of treatment
will, at any rate, be easy to the Boulder men, being
the usual roast with sliming and filter-pressing.
Victoria’s gold yield for 1913 was 437,984
oz., as against 479,281 oz. in 1912. The worst
feature is that the tailing-off has been continuous,
and the January figures of this year show a further
decrease of 3000 oz. If the Sludge Board biffs out
the dredging industry, as is likely, the State output
will drop below 400,0000 z., which will be less than
half of what it was 10 years ago.
The walk-in-walk-out sale talked about by
J. L. Wharton to the St. Arnaud (Vic.) citizens who
want more work done on the Lord Nelson mine there,
recalls that the show has been one of the bad bargains
of the Collins House “toffs.” They tacked the North
Lord Nelson on to the old Lord Nelson, and at one
time were as proud of the fusion as a pup over the
finding of a buried bone. All the mine has done since
has been to pay its way. As this won’t buy oil for
the Collins-street motor, St. Arnaud can have the
Nelson outfit, at a figure. And if it wants to keep
the mine open it will probably have to take it over or
do something of the kind.
The sugar coating to the second call of
Is. 6d. by the Mount Balfour (Tas.) is the announce-
ment that two “groups,” working together, have sub-
mitted a proposition for financing the whole under-
taking. Apparently this means that the big French
syndicate, lately talked of, has gone into retirement.
* * * *
“D.” : Some Kalgoorlie managers have hit
on a new method of locating pay ore. The trick is to
invite parties ■of men to take small portions of the
mine on tribute; 100 ft. on a particular level is the
usual offer. Then the miners with local knowledge
apply, indicating their pet spots. The managers
forthwith try these spots—and suddenly discover that
it was never intended to let those particular places
on tribute.
Molybdenite is worth looking for now that
the price has risen to nearly £4OO per ton. The
mineral is generally associated with wolfram, and
bismuth, and milling is then required to separate
the wolfram-bismuth ore from the other.


A rumor has been circulated that the
Misses Clapp are giving up business, but
there is no truth in the statement. They
will be in Sydney in February, and again in
July, as usual.*

WOMAN'S
LETTE
Sydney, February 9, 1914.
My Dear Arini, —
The Stuart family, which lives out Stan-
more way —Papa is a big contractor in his
busy moments —is off to Maoriland for a
holiday jaunt. The travellers —who, despite
their name, have made no urgent claim upon
the British Crown—will start in the North
Island with samples of hot and cold mud,
have some fishing at Taupo, splash down the
Wanganui Rapids, then go south to the
lakes that lurk in the shadow of snow-topped
hills.
Prof. Moors, with his missus and daughter,
are leaving Sydney to attack Maoriland via
Melbourne and the Bluff on a long holiday.
They start at Invercargill, the cold, silent
place where the streets are a mile across and
the houses on the other side are about two
feet high, and all prohibition houses at that.
Invercargill is a pious place, but the ice-man
brings the religion round in a frozen state
every morning. The Moors will work up
northward to Auckland —thence, possibly to
Vancouver.
Invercargill, by the way, is the place where
it is a kind of religion to have a brass door-
step. It is made rather like a pillow-sham,
and slips on over the original whitewashed
nuisance. New arrivals in the squally town
begin by turning up their noses at the brass
doorstep fetich. Then Mrs. Newcomer finds
that even the milkman is not quite satisfied
with her social status, and the butcher’s boy
says he is not used to anything but brass
doorsteps (it means more polishing, of
course), and the greengrocer reckons she
must come from the islands where they don’t
have doorsteps. _So in the end another yel-
lowed sepulchre is added to the list.
The Walter Buhmeyers, of Bexley, just
back from a sojourn at Ivatoomba, report
the thrills that go with a dogged pursuit of
hired help on the mountain-tops. A fur-
nished cot on the heights is described as a
home away from home. You scrape the
saucepans in the same old way at an alti-
tude of 3000 ft. above the level of your Syd-
ney chores. Thirty shillings a week was
offered in vain to any strong, upstanding
girl willing to lend a hand with the domestic
grind. The pursuit of the unattainable got into
the Buhmeyer blood. Every day the least
exhausted member of the family went off in
search of Help, and returned —badly beaten
—after about one round.
Did you notice that Mrs. George Reid
took charge of a gold medal from the Ship-
wreck Relief and Humane Society before
leaving this township? Chairman Green, of
tb'ti Association, handed over the trophy to
Mrs. George to pass on to her High Com-
missioner. This entitles G.H. to a life
membership of the N.S.W. Society, whatever
that honorary non-life saving billet may _be
worth. Perhaps at long last G.R. is going
to plunge in and rescue his drv dog.
W. Redmond, M.P., the Home Ruler over-
seas scout who’s been here on a health trip,
goes off in the Marama to-day (Mondav).
Parson Hopcraft, who died the other day,
was, I think, a South Australian and a son of
an Anglican canon in the City of Spires. Be-
fore taking over the cure of souls at St.
Peter’s, Watson’s Bay, the late cleric had
the tough billet of following a venerable
landmark named Smith in the Canberra dis-
trict. Parson Smith had been in charge of
the Anglican conscience thereabouts for
something like 50 years, and only gave un
from the compelling circumstance of old
age. When a youngish man with modern
notions followed an ancient tradition, hoary
with the rime of sentiment, he bumped up
against something as soft as an ironbark
rail. Parson Hopcraft gave it up. But he
made a useful buffer between half a century
of Smith, and the present shepherd, Parson
Champion, formerly headmaster of the King’s
School, who now rounds up stray sheep on
the Yassberra plains.
Jack Playfair, who topped the municipal
poll at Waverley the other day, is only 23
years young. The juvenile alderman is a
popular infant, and incidentally he is a son
of Meat Magnate E. Playfair, who butchers
in a big w T ay hereabouts.
Ruth Bedford, a clever daughter of Man-
ager A. P. Bedford, of the Permanent Trus-
tee Co., recently returned from England
with a neat inky halo round her best hat.
Her latest effort is a charming book of
nursing rhvmes, called “Rosycheeks and
Goldenhead.” The illustrations by Mabel L.
Webb are good enough to keep youngsters
out of mischief for half a wet day. (We
have wet days sometimes, though they’re
hard to catch just now.) “Not a centipede”
is a rhyme that will, go to the right place in
every small boy’s cranium, as thus: —
Supuose, when I began to run,
That halt my feet should stop, for fun,
While fifty more went rushing past—
What would become of me at last?
The George Binnie family, or most of it,
of 4D station (this is the brand of the Binnie
wool) in the Quirindi sheep area, left for
England on Saturday.
Mrs. Paul, a versatile Sydney woman who
dabbles seriously in Art, recently landed
here after six years absence from her native
gum-tree. In Paris, where she studied in
the Julien School and at the Collorossi Aca-
demy, Mrs. Paul was struck by the number
of leading American teachers who were
having Refreshers in Art, as students in
those well-known studios. During three
years residence in the States the vivacious
Australian was profoundly impressed with
the power the generally voteless American
woman wields in civic affairs. Incidentally
canvas seaside camps on the American coast
are cheap and comfortable propositions com-
pared with the frequently uncomfortable and
exorbitant seaside hotel or boarding-house.
“You can buy a three-roomed brown canvas
tent house in the States, with a well-made
hardwood floor, for £30,” muses the returned
traveller. “I’m surprised they’re not a
craze here in the surfing season.” As the
Waringah Shire Council nearly fainted dead
away when a canvas settlement was sug-
gested by the Seaside Camps’ Association
I suppose there’s something contraband
about the cloth dwelling that ordinary folk
don’t understand.
Ex-Governor Chelmsford, who was a good
tip for the G.-G.-sliip a week ago, has ap-
parently been beaten by an eyelash.
The new man, Mr. Munro-Ferguson, is a
long-headed Scot, and he trots in double
harness with a daughter of the first Marquis
of Dufferin. who vice-royed in India years
ago. The Dufferin title is young enough to
be only taking its second airing with the
present wearer of the bauble.
Long Jack Cormack, recently of the
N.S.W. Tourist Bureau, writes from Lon-
don. He finds it cold and damp and doesn’t
hope to see it any damper. Also the atmo-
sphere is pale grey and at times a trifle
suggestive of pea-soup. Londoners call it a
mild winter, but some people would call
almost anything by any name under the
sun. With Mrs. Jack he went to a serious
afternoon party given by a Mrs. Stovin, at
Connaught Place. As he is in England on
the Australian Government irrigation
racket, he gave the company (by request)
a hint of doings in the Murrumbidgee area.
The party was related to “the Australian
League” (in London) in connection with
“The Colonial and Continental Church
Society.” It aims at passing round the hat
on behalf of outback clerics in this con-
tinent —that weary, disreputable, degrading
old cadged hat that some well-meaning per-
son is always passing round to make the
world think we are paupers. Cormack drew
a touching picture of Sky-Pilot Free-
man—about 6ft. 6in. of Bush Brother —
literally building a place of prayer, and
making adobe bricks to do it with. His
Anglican Legs of Ballarat. Mr. Joe Bar-
ling, the Cecil Darleys, a contingent of
Saumarez Smiths, etc., were in the com-
pany. The gloom was made visible by wax
candles, and two gorgeous footmen stoked
the visitors with light refreshments.
Mrs. Dillon Bell, of Neutral Bay. gave
a huge tea-spread to farewell the Edgecliff
heiress, Miss Ducker. who is packing up for
the world tour I told you about some time
ago. About 40 persons poured wet goods
down their thirsty throats in honor of the
lady.
Mrs. Moreau, a well-known Darling
Pointer, farewelled several Sydney dames
who are on the wing, the other day. Mrs.
Albert Nathan. Mrs. IJenriques, Mrs. Arthur
Gleeson, and the much tea’d Miss Ducker
were the birds of passage who had this
lunch, bridge and final clatter of teacuns
hurled at them by the hostess. Mrs. W. F.
Brennan took a hand at bridge amongst the
best players present, and Mrs. A. E. Hughes
was one of the nicest-looking young persons
present.
Artist Percy Spence is packing up his
paint-box for a flight to the Fog. He takes,
as excess luggage, a huge canvas, “Arrival
of the Australian Fleet.” This painting is
to be handed over to George V. as a slight
token of esteem from the Prince Alfred
Yacht Club and others. The King has
graciously notified the donors that he will
not turn it away from his royal doorstep,
or cast it into outer darkness, or chase it
with execrations into the wilderness.
Shakspeare’s Tercentenary party will get
a fresh fillip from the April f£te on the Syd-
ney Cricket Ground. Those who believe
that the Bard is in need of moral or financial
help will be able to rush to his memorial
festival on Sydney Cricket Ground eight or
nine weeks hence. A fresh bone of conten-
tion in the shape of “a literary and artistic
stall” will be arranged by some of our women
journalists. Why don’t they have two stalls
—a Shakspeare one and a Bacon one? They
might each be divided into two sections—
one for people who believe Hamlet was mad,
and one for those who think he was simply
disguising his brains.
Concerning journalism, a small, strenuous
American man, Prof. Williams, of Missouri,
has been dined and wined by the N.S.W. In-
stitute of Scribes (not Pharisees). After
President Ileney had flung a few remarks of
an affable nature at the man from Missouri,
Livingston Hopkins (better known as
“Hop” in two hemispheres) supported the
toast to his compatriot. When run to earth
later on, “Hop” remarked: “Prof. Williams’s
speech iast Friday was up to sample as a
specimen of American after-dinner oratory,
and as one of our morning contemporaries
would put it, his accent ‘left nothing to be
desired.’ It was disappointing in one respect
only. England expects that every American
should ‘guess’ and ‘calkalate.’ Prof. Wil-
liams never guessed once. He seemed pretty
cocksure on everything he said —even upon
the gospel of a school of journalism... .He
makes vase rhyme with face.’. ..It is only
fair to add that our distinguished visitor
spoke of ‘your noble ’ instead of ‘your beauti-
ful’ harbor—an instance of versatility in the
use of epithets highly commendable in a high
priest of journalism.” “Hop,” like many
other authorities, rejects the vain imagining
that journalism can be taught in any school,
and believes in the famous academy—Experi-
ence.
Tenor Cecil Sherwood (sometimes mis-
called “Percy” by his friends) is going to
sing with all his might and main in the
Town Hall. March 9 is the date of this
farewell warble. Cecil, who is just looking
over the golden rim of 20 years, is a son of
Superintendent Sherwood, of the police. For
the last 12 months he has been studying
vocal gymnastics under Andrew Black, who
advises him to get into opera, via a stiff
course of European training. So the boy
goes off to Milan on April 15, to begin work.
He has been singing, more or less, since he
lifted a juvenile soprano at the age of
seven, in music suited to his then infantile
understanding. Perhaps he’ll make a noise at
Covent Garden in four or five years. Mrs.
Trefl6 took the chair at last week’s big meet-
ing re the Sherwood farewell concert.
The first of a series of matinees in aid of
the Royal Hospital for Women and the
Renwick Hospital for sick babes, occurs on
Feburary 20. Her Majesty’s Theatre will
be the jumping-off place for this charity
cycle.
Alfred Hill’s Maori opera, “The Weird
Flute” and an operatic outbreak by Fritz
Hart, “Pierrette ” are to be produced to-
gether, if the National Australian Opera Co.
materialises behind a bank account. Hill
has a strong dramatic instinct to back up
the attractive harmony that throbs under
his hat. Fritz Hart, a slim, boyish-looking
musician, used to jerk his baton over a thea-
trical orchestra here. Now he is deputy
something at the Marshall Hall Conserva-
torium (Melb.) while the Chief Crochet is
away.
Cricketer C. Kelleway has bustled off to
Maoriland on an excursion of business
sprinkled with pleasure. He is going to
keep his ears open for any shouts for help
from the Australian XI now batting in the
Dominion, and hopes to renew his acquaint-
ance with Maori pals in the geyser district.
When Kelleway was playing cricket over
there about four yea,rs ago, he went to
Rotorua with two or three members of the
team to have a look at the breath of the
Devil as it comes up from down under. Ro-
torua entreated them to have an exhibition
match, and celebrated the event with a
gigantic picnic. To make up Kelleway’s
side —which played Rotorua—five or six
Maoris were picked. Brown Brother in
Whakarewarewa (this word goes on in-
definitely) is still yearning to remind the
Australian cricketer that a Maori made top
score on that eventful occasion.
Miss E. Grosholz, a Maoriland singer who
has been away from her native shores for a
considerable time, recently returned from the
other side of the globe. She leaves Sydney
in the Maunganui to see whether the
Dominion of Massey is in its old accustomed
place or whether it has gone wandering after
strange gods and Cohens.
How this hamlet amuses itself may be
guessed from the fact that one leading music
warehouse here takes about £230,000 a year
for booking seats in the principal theatres,
etc. This sum does not include picture shows
nor various other amusements, but it covers
that particular firm’s concert booking.
Another reason to believe that capital has
not wholly fled from N.S.W.
The Chisholm sisters, who hail from Lis-
more or thereabouts, are off to England in
March. They are the girls who have been
doing things in the musical line with some
small local thunder. Pianist Maggie won
the Emmeline Woolley scholarship in 1913.
This gives her a free show alternately at the
Royal Academy of Music and the Royal
College of Music (London) for three years.
Sister Lena has snatched a scholarship for
her fiddling. She’ll get a fresh embellish-
ment or dado of technique in London for
her pains. Mrs. Tom Hughes, Mrs. Macken
and! Mrs. McDermott were responsible for a
gathering that pledged itself to pull a com-
plimentary concert into position on March
5. Concordia Hall will be the place. Wil-
liam McMillan took the chair at this
meeting to abet the Chisholm sisters’ fare-
well to their native clod.
Dolores leaves this town on February 14
for her tour through Maoriland.
The Scottish tourists, James Craig and
his daughter, who have been wandering
round the edge of this continent, finished
their Australasian cruise with a comprehen-
sive snack of' Maoriland scenery. Now they
are taking a stack of complimentary remarks
—in strong Doric —back to the land of
kilts.
There’s nothing new about the transpar-
encies of the moment. In the days of the
French Consulate feminine dress became so
sketchy that Parisiennes actually died of
what was called “an epidemic of the French
modes.” The three-flounced (or terraced
skirt) which is coming into grace, is said
to trace its origin to the days of Moses. “In
those days,” says a French writer who
sneaks as if he had been personally present
at the borrowing of the 10 Commandments
from the code of Khammurabi, “it was not
knife-pleated as it is to-day. but vras draped
in tiers.” The lampshade lady, however, is
of later date than Moses, for I think she
grew in Paris.
The Eternal Rag, feminine gender, is
again holding up the traffic in our Best
Shops. Autumn fashions, by right of iron-
clad precedent, always bud in sultry Febru-
ary. In consequence, our Nicest People are
languidly mopping their damp countenances
as they gaze respectfully at furs that would
be cosy wear for an Esquimau ladw
The pony jacket is a smaller size this season.
It is inky black, and a cross cut between a
mantle and a jacket, and it ends in a neat
tail that finishes off a pouch of drapery be-
hind. Black fox fur shuts out any draughts
round the neck. A pony coat runs into any-
thing from fSO to £lOO. The enterprising
woman who hurried into the business —say
in the Shetland Isles, where you can buy a
whole pony for 42s. —before fashions changed
to something harder to kill, would make a
competence for her old age. Wild beasts are
reluctantly contributing their skins and
claws to lovely woman. But the weak points
about leopard or tiger mats, apart from their
fiendish price, are their shrieking patterns.
White tailless ermine—also for the Rich —
made up like a fall of snow inset with lace,
is another Arctic fantasy in February.
The French frocks that floated over wire
frames at an autumn show last week are
mostly built in tiers or terraces, edged with
fur. Black tulle or net evening gowns, for
instance, with capes of the same material,
show the 1914 tendency to hide the back of
the neck. That is to say, you pretend to
hide your shoulder blades by wearing a cape
rather more transparent than a butterfly’s
wing.
Beauty red—twin shade to carnation
—and tango are the colors that fight a bilious
mustard-green for first places in the ever-
lasting dress parade. Skirts are not slit—
they are wide open to the knee in front, in
these Paris models. There’s a kind of lap
over, of course, but the lap wouldn’t cover
one per cent, of stocking on., a breezy day.
Tango is the color of a ripe tomato as it
sizzles in a frying-pan, and the tango dress
is rather more so than the X-ray garment.
Oddments to pile up the trimmings on the
new frocks are plaid and shot silk, and shot
velvet. Queer, baggy skirts —they bag at
the hips and fit the ankles after the fashion
of sticking plaster—like the old-fashioned
riding-habit when you harpooned it at the
sides with pins after getting off your ancient
three-horned saddle—appear to be It this
year.
For some time conscientious pedestrians
have been cultivating habits of keeping to
the Right, not expectorating on the foot-
paths, and preferring to swallow their tram-
tickets rather than let them lie around the
already untidy scenery. Now they have to
hurriedly unlearn the first commandment
and keep to the left —by order of the police.
While the white cotton gloves of the Law
are pushing or prodding bustling persons,
to the left they might, by way of a change,
move on the crowds of male idlers who keep
neither to the right nor the left, but stand
stock still in every busy thoroughfare, filling
the atmosphere with lurid language and
otherwise obstructing the traffic.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.
“A III.”: Caton Woodville, the military
artist, has published a book of reminiscences
in which he asserts that he was. the first to
give the British Government an idea of the
military preparedness of the South African
Republics before the last war. “Tripe !” as
Joe Kook would say. We have the word of
that fine soldier, General Butler, then in
command at the Cape, that he was in pos-
session of the fullest details as to the equip-
ment of the Republics, and passed them on
to the War Office. These details were
gathered by such men as Colonel “Mike”
Remington, who, as a whisky traveller in
Johannesburg and Pretoria, got the very full-
est notes of armaments, etc. It was the
British War Office and not the S’African
Intelligence Department that was at fault.
Why the War Office did not make more use
of the information is not known to this day.
It may have been dunderheadedness; more
like it was the same old swelled-headedness.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, February 9, 1914.
My Dear Yarrie. —
A somewhat belated item of English
Christmas news from sprightly Marjorie
Raphael (late Heinbockel) throws a humor-
ous sidelight on the new responsibilities of
that interesting young matron. In a letter
to a Melbourne friend she mentioned that
one of her guests—Arthur Villiers, son of
the Lord Jersey who reigned in Sydney long
ago—had got her to donate a Christmas pud-
ding to a Boys’ Club at the East End of
London. “ ‘Pudding’ is a wide term,” said
Mrs. Norman Raphael. “In this case it will
have to provide for 250 boys, so it is worse
than it sounds.” By the way, many admir-
ing comments on the clever little Melbourne
girl are blowing in from across the water.
Her small size in feet has been noted, also
her creamy complexion and Madonna-like
profile. And above all, the free and en-
lightened character of Marjorie’s conversa-
tion is remarked wherever she goes. Her
native wit is distinctively Australian. The
smart set must surely vote her “ripping.”
Marie Lohr (Mrs. Val Prinsep) has given
her first-born the straight-laced name of
Jane. From which one supposes that poor
old “Jane” is getting to be fashionable
once more. Time was when it stood
for blue blood and bad luck in English his-
tory. For instance, there was Lady Jane
Grey, who was proclaimed queen before her
head was cut off. Previously Jane Sey-
mour, Queen of Henry VIII. and mother of
Edward VI., had cheated her husband of a
royal execution by most inconsiderately
dying a natural death. Perhaps it was the
fate of Jane Grey that finally put the name
out of fashion in the best circles and re-
duced it to the housemaid level. That almost
historical lady of beer and the police-court,
Jane Cakebread, didn’t improve its popu-
larity either.
Mrs. Leslie Stuart, who will send along
some precious antiques to the Denman Loan
Exhibition, is still pondering whether to
include her Napoleon dessert dishes and let
the precious, fragile things take their chance.
The dishes are a beautiful blue, and have
the initial N encircled by the modest laurel
wreath affected by the big-little man. They
are also embellished with burnt-in miniature
portraits of famous Court beauties, signed
by Humberts. Mrs. Stuart is also sending
a pencil portrait of Hallam. It belonged to
Tennyson, whose distinguished ghost perches
on one of the top branches of Mrs. Stuart’s
family tree or weed. The gay little blonde
lady aforesaid is shortly to be left a grass
widow for several months, her husband hav-
ing to go to England on business. The trip
is too brief to disturb the house and family
over, so she is staying comfortably at home
with the children.
Miss Haywood was married from the
house of her sister, Mrs. Leslie Stuart, with
a great deal of homely fuss, including brides-
maids, cake and so forth, but no outsiders.
The bride is a daughter of the late clergy-
man Haywood, who used to be chaplain at
Port Arthur, Tasmania. Mrs. Haywood
now lives quietly at Sandringham. ' She
feels that she has done her duty by her nine
daughters, of whom Marjorie is the eighth
to marry. The last remaining girl is the
youngest, and has plenty of time left in
which to. find an altar-partner. Woodside,
the Toorak house of the Stuarts, is com-
pletely surrounded by its gardens, and when
all the motors had oiled up the drive, and
the last and latest perspiring guest had
rushed to the festivity with his mouth full
of apologies,. the party was as secluded as
if it were being held at the Back o’ Beyond.
The bride’s mother has an old-fashioned but
charming and sensible horror of the display
of presents—-“vulgar,” she calls it; so the
flashing glories of the guests’ gifts could only
be peeped at through tissue paper wrappings
in. the privacy of bedroom bureaux. The
bride, embroidered nearly all her trousseau
dainties with a little help from her sisters.
Two little Stuart' girls—Nancy and Lav-
ender —helped three grown-ups to brides-
maid the ceremony. The bride herself was
a sweet occurrence in a simple Frenchy
frock and a veil of cut tulle, unadorned and
unhemmed. The bridegroom, Mr. Rutty,
wore an appropriate air of delight and some
male clothes of the usual unsensational pat-
tern. All the big clan of Stuart sent greet-
ings and beautiful house furnishings, and
the married sisters and brothers-in-law (God
bless them!) added to the presents. (I love
to see the rich being kind to one another.)
Parson Hughes, an old friend of the family,
tied, the important knot —at least, it was
an important knot to “the high contracting
parties,” as they say in the treaties.
The de Grut dame is shaking the dust of
this city from her feet (also her hair,
shoulders, garments and other belongings),
and is going gadding for a spell. Mammas
who have just “got off” a daughter are apt
to feel like a balloon cut from its moorings.
Dudie Griffiths is the next girl programmed
for a flqffy wedding. She is to be altared
this week to Ken Niall, brother of Margaret,
who marries Gubernatorial Help Burnham
a month hence.
Bachelor-doctor McGee, of Collins-street,
is making off for a long foreign trip. Pretty
sister Fairie goes along as chaperone.
The Mayoress of St. Kilda has been
seriously ill since her husband assumed the
rabbit skin and dust-bin of office. The lady
is now convalescent and is to make a brave
try to do her superfluous duty by her sub-
urban realm at the end of the week, when
she is to hold a reception at the local Town
Hall and do a long handshake with the most
loyal and abject section of the ratepayers.
The heroism of the unnecessary!
The memory of Janet. Mrs, Clarke gets
freshened up periodically through the untir-
ing efforts of her devoted family. Daugh-
ter _ Mrs. Landale has just commissioned
artist McCubbin to paint a full-length por-
trait of the deceased benefactress for the
Women’s Hostel of Trinity College—a pet
charity of the lady who is gone.
The engagement of an attractive Melbourne
girl, which was gushfully alluded to by vari-
ous women as something too exquisite for
words, has been cancelled in the most un-
romantic circumstances. The young man, it
appeared, lost his chance when the subject of
marriage. settlements was tactfully brought
up His evident anxiety to make his future
quite safe at her expense wearied the lady,
and she dismissed him.
The Dudley Countess has been flying about
—in the air. One of her recent trips was
as passenger with Gustav Hamel on the
monoplane that was built specially for loop-
ing the loop—quite a frivolous proceeding
for so cold and stately a lady.
Rippon Lea, the old Sargood home at
Elsternwick, is looking like its former self
just now, being arranged for a charity fgte.
The event is fixed for Saturday, February
14, and there will be soft Japanese lanterns
and fireworks when darkness falls on the
scene and the lover throws his arm like a
boa-constrictor round his lady’s waist. The
indispensable “object” is the Prahran stall
at the forthcoming Alfred Hospital Bazaar.
Female political scrambles are real god-
sends to some of the scramblers’ husbands.
You see, your real Leaguer allows the home
and the husband to go to pot when there
is any excitement on, and the speed with
which the husband proceeds to pot (as re-
presented by a late cafe) shows how glad
he is that women are taking an interest in
public affairs.
Artist Will Longstaff is holding a private
view of the picture he painted at the special
request of the Bananaland Government —a
fine portrait of Lieut.-Colonel W. H. Tun-
bridge. As a pretty side-show there is a
just-completed portrait - of little Miss
Deschamps, whose proud father is delighted
at the Longstaff portrayal of his daughter’s
bewitching smile. There is also a portrait
of the late Otto Schmidt, e fine-looking, well-
groomed man who was widely known in the
paths of commerce. His son is so pleased
with the portrait that he has ordered a
replica to send to Germany. The hard-work-
ing Longstaff has a handful of water
color studies of the nude —an Australian Eve
wandering pensively on the shady banks of
a blue Australian billabong.
Flying-man Hawker climbed the invisible
stairs of the sky in his sputtering chariot on
Saturday at Caulfield, and thereby amused
many thousand people. He ascended almost
at schedule time with as little fuss as a man
starting off flatly in an earth-borne motor-
car ; and he gambolled about in space like the
noble but aimless and unemployed albatross.
A pink carpet of faces looked up at his re-
mote boot-soles, and the owners of same said
that it was wonderful and they never could
have imagined it, which was quite' true of
the majority of them. More banal remarks
were made and more feet trodden on, than on
any previous local occasion, and on Monday
morning the daily papers let their hair down
and went on about it in the usual old-maidish
manner. It must be very gratifying for
Hawker —if he likes that sort of thing—but
he must have been amused at the excitement
and unaffected delight of the simple villagers
of Melbourne. V.A.T.C. members, though on
their own premises, had to pay their half-
crowns. John Madden, with a grey felt hat
pulled well over his ears, waited in the vice-
regal loose-box for the G.-G., in com-
pany with an aidelet (a local, amateur
Help). The Denman dame, wearing appro-
priate white wings in her 1 at, was otherwise
garbed in black, like Miss Hugessen, who
accompanied her. The little G.-G. and Help
Barttelot both paraded in brilliant striped
ties of black and scarlet. The girl who
eventually flew —Miss Dixon —spent a great
deal of thought on her dress, and a Collins-
street milliner thereby 'got a good advertise-
ment. The hat was shiny black waterproof,
and stuck in the side was a bright waterproof
waratah. She was distinctly annoyed at
the necessary goggles, and peeped under them
when the rude crowd shouted at Hawker,
“You’re on a good wicket!” It certainly
looked as if they were eloping. They soared
together into the blue, and the sputter of
the engine gradually died away as they
dwindled down the skies and disappeared.
The aviator, though, was merely taking her
home as the crow flies when it’s sober. It
was an unexpected act on his part, and it
took some time for the crowd to realise that
he had gone for the day and that all was
over. Then they began to press and squeeze
towards the gates. Once the cry went up,
“Here! he’s coining back!” and all hands
stretched their necks and looked back. But
it was only a bird hovering curiously on
the edge of a cloud bank, like the one Hawker
had once flown into. The crowd on the flat
was troublesome, and the mounted police had
to work hard to prevent an accident. As it
was, two women were booked for flights at
£2O apiece; but the mobbing made passen-
ger-carrying so dangerous that the two had
to toss for the get-away trip, for which they
both longed.
Dolores, who is bound for Maoriland,
steamed in and out of here in the N.D.L.
Zieten. In four months’ time she will be
“doing” Australia and delighting it with
song. The dimpled adorable woman, in order
to escape the publicity which it is the life-
work of the press-agent to supply, travels
under a name which must be almost as well
known as her platform one, so widely and
persistently has it been advertised. Oyi of
consideration for her, I am carefully omitting
it from this letter. The steamer was delayed
by labor troubles, and importunate friends
climbed the gangway of the ship on which
she was resting under her other name (I re-
fuse to mention what it is!) and shook Inn-
hand, and betrayed her in delighted shrieks
to the people to whose sea music she had
graciously listened undetected. Rough on the
singing and playing passengers, wasn’t it V
On Friday she had quite a big dinner-
party aboard, and the diners after-
wards stayed chatting on the deck in
the cool until midnight, when the
delaved boat cast off and backed away into
the moonlit bay. The white Belgian train-
ing-ship close by looked ghostly, and the
spars showed up against the distant blaze of
Luna Park. A travelled German close by
sighed, “Luna Park ! There is one even in
Jerusalem and mad Arabs shriek on the
scenic railway.” Dolores has still more
songs for the concert-haunter. She grows
not a day older in looks or spirit, and never
ceases to study or seek fresh material.
Tenor Paul Dufault is simply breathing
press and other incense in Maoriland. The
last. report shows that he was gravely re-
ceiving the homage of Christchurch—the
city the residents call “Choo Choo” and
scribble “Ch. Ch.”
Julius Knight now wears a feather in his
hat when doing the Block. It is only fair
to mention that it is not an ostrich feather.
It shows great self-restraint on his part, I
think. A sweeping ostrich plume is a
great temptation.
Stewart Dawson, the watch and jewel-
lery people, are said to have just paid
£85,000 for Nicholson’s Corner, at the
place where the Collins and Swanston streets
crowds bump into cine another on Saturday
mornings. Evidently there is money in
watches and gewgaws, in spite of the fact
that 17,963 shops in Melbourne divide
the trade. It is only 30 years ago since the
Stewart Dawson people fell into Melbourne
and started business with a staff of one man
and a bookkeeper boy in a small office up a
stairway in a part of Collins-street
that wasn’t of much consequence. Instead
of a shop-window for the public to put its
nose against, the Scotchman from Liverpool
plastered his advts. in the weekly papers
that got into the country ; and 25 years ago,
dropping into Melbourne on one of his perio-
dical tours among his branches, he
deserted the small office up the stairs
and brought a man from Maoriland to write
something new in signs on a little shop in
Swanston-street. And now the little shop
has swallowed the corner.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
While the thermometer has been registering- any-
thing. over a hundred, our departing Excellency has
been weltering in farewells. He began (piano) with
the Y.M.C.A. on Monday, and has worked through
factory inspectors, the Boy Scouts and Heaven knows
what to (fortissimo) the Adelaide Club’s dinner on
Friday night. On top of that he entertained the
Greys—Madam and the daughter for a few hours;
Monsieur for a few days—and has given his odd un-
occupied moments to the job of packing.
Beyond these items nothing much has transpired—-
except a record consumption of liquid refreshment
and a continued snarling between Mayor and military
over the civic entertainment of Sir lan Hamilton.
Also, it having been decided that the Glenelg railway
line shall be evicted from King William-street, a fresh
controversy has arisen as to what shall happen at
the Glenelg end of the rails. A proposal to build a
station immediately in front of some large and “de-
sirable” residences, effectually blocking their view of
the sea, has caused the owners to raise the person
who slew Abel. By the time Glenelg has decided
what it wants, steam suburban railways will be as
pre-historic as horse-trams.
Two South Australians of note have been snatched
from us—not by death, but by other States. Dr. F.
W. Wheatley left on Thursday for Melbourne, where
he is to instil' wisdom at the Naval College. He is
an old Prince Alfred College boy, who, after captur-
ing a degree or so at the Adelaide University, set
about teaching a few of the things he knew in Queens-
land and elsewhere. For the last couple of years he
has been at Oxford, where he earned the right to add
some more letters to his name. The other loss is
Dr. May Moffatt, who goes to Tasmania as Medical
Inspector of School Children.
The S.A. Yacht Squadron races, which occurred at
the Semaphore on Saturday, were patronised by the
Governor (guarded by Mr. Row), the E. W. Morrisses,
Miss Boothby, the Gifford Taits and various other
folk, who were more or less bored with the racing,
and more or less interested in one another —-and their
tea.
The Brighton Institute for the Blind, Deaf and
Dumb is richer by It 5000, an “anonymous” gift
having turned up by cheque (presumably signed by
someone) in an ordinary envelope, in the ordinary
way just as if it were a mere half-guinea. The
money is to be invested, and the committee rejoices
at the idea of having an extra £2OO or so a year to
spend.
Mrs. Charles Hasp, the Broken Hill millionairess,
whose first husband was a German boundary-rider
on Mount Gipps, isn’t to marry her Field-Marshal
Baron after all. No! the lady didn’t change her
mind. The change occurred in the other partv. He
put on immortality. After an illness of about five
weeks he died in London.
We won’t want as many new motor-cars this year.
The Govt. Statist a while ago promised us 19J
million bushels of wheat ; but now that the harvest
is in the bags there is less than 17 million bushels,
which means a poorer average than any time since
1904. Some suburbs are half-full of retired farmers
who have sold their grain-orchards on time-payment;
and on a still evening the air in those parts is full
of lamentations over unpaid instalments.
We very nearly had the new G.-G. Munro-Fergu-
son for our Governor once. It was early in 1895,
when Ivintore was quitting with his pockets full of
Broken Hill scrip, that Ferguson was offered, and,
it was cabled to us, accepted the local job; and
The Bulletin printed a picture of his knees pro-
truding from a swagger Highland costume. In those
days he was M.P. for Leith, having found a perch
after being very badly beaten by the Crofters’ man
in Ross-shire. But he was then much better known
as Rosebery’s- private secretary. However, he was
too rich or something, and at the last moment
wouldn’t come ; so we got the awfully dreary Fowell
Buxton instead.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.
“The Elderly Bo’sun” pipes a piece:—
The naval base business has got a good deal mixed
since the Cook Government undertook to import an
alleged expert to elucidate it. So far, it seems to
be only Cockburn Sound that is giving trouble, but
the imported expert isn’t sure. No imported expert
ever is. This one now requires so much information
that he might as well have remained in the country
he came from for another year or so. By that time
the local experts who are expected to find the infor-
mation for him may be dead or the Cook Government
(as is more probable) will be out of a job, or it may
be found necessary to abandon some of the bases
tentatively chosen and choose others. As a matter
of fact, any decently good harbor, if it is deep
enough, will make a naval base, and any navigating
officer is quite competent to choose such a place.
When Pearce was Minister for Defence he talked
about getting a floating dock to begin with, which
would be much cheaper than a graving dock, and
could be taken where it was wanted to pick up a
wreck. Apparently it is not understood either by
Millen or his imported alleged expert, that floating
docks are part of the regular equipment of a modern
navy. It is more than 50 years since the two oldest
British dockyards at Woolwich and Deptford on the
Thames were abolished, and yet Chatham, Portsmouth
and Plymouth, which are all over 300 years old, are
still going strong, and each is quite unlike the other.
Chatham is miles up a river, with its dockyard on
a peninsula formed by a deep bend. Portsmouth, the
greatest of British dockyards, is on a low, naturally
swampy flat, its harbor formed by a lot of muddy
•creeks radiating from the entrance at Spithead. Both
Chatham and Portsmouth have their outer anchorages
at the Nore and Spithead. Plymouth is in quite a
different class. In the wars against France in the
18th century it, with Torbay, made a good base for
the fleets blockading Brest. Naval bases are of
many kinds, and there can be no fixed rulea for
estimating their value for any given purpose. Any-
how, as the racket we’re afraid of is likely to be
most strenuous away up North, what about making
some inquiries concerning a base thereabouts for the
overhauling of our ironclads when they crawl out
of the scrimmage?
It looks as though, if there were any
White JSlave traffic in Melbourne, quite a
number of that city’s girls would be only
too willing to don the shackles or whatever
other harness there may be. The police at
headquarters are weary of listening to the
tales of weepful damsels who call in late at
night to tell their troubles and borrow
enough to take them home. One and all
have been “mashed” by fascinating strangers
in the city or near the Central Station, and
have then gone for a stroll to the gardens
over Princes Bridge. They sat down ou
well-lighted lawns or rustic seats and they
bestowed their purses or handbags and hats
behind them or under the seat. Of course,
when they rose to go the purse or bag and
sometimes the hat too had disappeared. The
police have a fair idea of the “fascinating
strangers” against whom they would like to
lay charges of larceny, but the damsels
unanimously decline to appear if an arrest is
made; also they omit to state why they
didn’t take better care of their luggage. So
all the law can do is to issue a warning to
bare-headed damsels and let it go at that.
“A III.”: Though the offer of Tuta
Nahoniho to raise a Maori regiment for
service in S’Africa was turned down by the
British Government (B. 29/1/T4), a good
many Maoris and half-castes slipped into the
regiments which sailed from the Great Loan
Land. They took a considerable risk, too,
for the Boer showed little consideration to
colored men captured with arms. Ilenare
meant no more to him than Umslopogaas.
One big half-caste in the First Maorilanders
had a narrow escape at Sanna’s Post, where
de Wet smashed Broad wood’s column. Hav-
ing lost his horse and his rifle in the melee,
our brown brother was buried in thought
beside a Cape cart, the driver of which had
vanished, when the Dutchmen rode up. One
of them, taking him, in his filthy khaki, for
a “Cape Boy” of the transport, signed to
him to mount the cart and drive. He did
so, but at nightfall he informally resigned
his job and fled back to his battered regi-
ment.
