“One Mother”: —
“Mil” (B. 22/l/’l4) praises the administration
of the Aborigines Protection department of the North-
ern Territory, and declares there has been an im-
provement in the condition of the natives. Maybe;
but how does he justify the tearing away of young
children from their mothers, because they are half-
caste? Surely black mothers have some right to
their children? They have hearts the same as white
mothers. The Protectors even separate sisters. The
children are sent to mission stations or “adopted,”
to be brought up as servants to whites. This in the
name of Christianity and civilisation! I do not
think anything can justify such cruelty.
Presumably, in the name of Christianity and civilisa-
tion, “One Mother” would leave the partly-white off-
spring in the camp of the black mother of the
dubious character. Mothers have rights, certainly;
but so have children—the right to a fair chance, to
begin with.
* * * *
“Te Babarantiko”: The Central and S.
Eastern Pacific islander is just as omnivorous a
feeder, as far as his limited resources permit, as his
brother of the Western Pacific, but, with rare excep-
tions, he is always willing to share his scanty fare
with the casual visitor or wayfarer. There is a
strict etiquette in these matters. The proffered food
can never be accepted off-hand. The visitor, when
the biscuit, bit of fish or taro is pushed over, must
always declare that he is “full,” and push it back.
Often the bits of food are pushed back and forth
over a dozen times, but the visitor always con-
cludes by eating it. It is not good form to ask for
food, although my friend Na Bua did it one day.
He appeared at my front door holding in each hand
a tin of “blown” salmon, with ends bulged out like
an importer’s turnrny. I recognised the tins as two
out of seven I had thrown into the lagoon, cursing
the dead loss on a case full. Did I want them ?
No. Could he have them? What for? To eat.
He could have them, but I advised him not to eat
the stuff. He made no comment, but pulled out his
knife. “Not here on my verandah!” I yelled. A
while later he reappeared showing every evidence of
having indulged in “blown” salmon, and asked
permission to build a fire. The salmon was not as
good as he could wish, but he thought it would go
better roasted. He, his wife, Nei Toratoa, and a
small boy waded through the seven tins of salmon,
and though I watched for signs of poisoning it
never incommoded them.
* * * *
Dr. Mjoberg, the scientist who was sent
by the Swedish Government to interview Binghi
under his own gum-tree lately, in a letter to the
N.S.W. Linnaean Society, spoke a word on behalf of
that lonesome and vanishing person. The doctor,
regarding Binghi with the eye of a scientist, con-
siders him, as the representative of “one of the
oldest races on earth,” an interesting individual in
his native or untrousered condition, and opines that
the only way to keep him out of the uttermost tomb
is to keep him out of trousers. That is to say,
Binghi must be preserved from contact with white
brother, even white brother with the Bible. And
Mjoberg reckons he knows the very place for the
Purpose —Mornington Island, at the bottom end of
the Gulf of Carpentaria. It is 37 mil* long by 17
miles broad, and local Binghi has scarcely ever seen
white brother, and has no dreams of white brother’s
’bacca. Likewise, of white brother’s Bible and its
corollary, white brother’s rum, which is brown.
Mjoberg puts his plea forcibly:—
In the southern portion of the Continent they are
almost extinct. Only in the central and the inac-
cessible northern portions of Australia do they live
their true life, although even here the degeneration
has plainly spread, threatening them in the near
future with a similar fate to the aborigines of the
now civilised parts Only in one spot in Queens-
land, on Mornington Island, do the natives still live
untouched by white civilisation. They are living the
free and undisturbed life of their ancestors, governed
by their own unwritten laws and strict morals, the
latter even surpassing those of the whites. I do
not hesitate .to say that the establishment of a mis-
sion there will be the death-blow to the aborigines—■
that the hitherto happy blacks will be changed into
the same repelling and pitiable type which one
only too often observes in different parts of the con-
tinent. I ask, “Will this be permitted to happen
without a warning voice being raised? Is it not
wrong to thus degenerate the happy aborigines by
means of civilisation?”
Presumably, in order to get an answer to the ques-
tion, he wants Binghi to have a front seat on the
business paper at the next British Science Associa-
tion’s Congress.
* * * *
“Marloo”: The published returns of the
herd-testing associations of Taranaki (M.L.) should
make the coastal dairymen of N.S.W., who are
almost prostrated by labor troubles,
sit up and take notice. The tests of
the Mangorei Ass. show that the
best and worst cows earned, respec-
tively, 555. 6d. and 9s. per month;
and as 13s. 6d. a month was the cost
of feeding a cow, it follows that you
could either make 425. or lose 4s. 6d.
per beast, according to the kind of
beast Strawberry happened to be. The
best milker tested by the Strathford
Ass. produced 53.821 b. of butter-fat
for the month, and the worst 9.121 b. ;
the best 10 cows averaged 42.811 b., and
the worst 10 only 14.331 b. a month.
In the case of the Midhurst Ass. the
best herd averaged 43.31 b., and
the worst 16.631 b. of fat. The best cow
gave 59.671 b., and the worst 5.41 b., while the average
was 28.671 b. The best 10 averaged 45.61 b., and
the worst 10 12.141 b. To the harassed dairyman
these figures give a hint as loud as a steam siren
or a politician’s wink to have the herd systemati-
cally tested, and to thereupon cast out the cherished
Strawberry if the figures prove the lady to be better
suited to the butchery than the ba'il.

“G. Back”: This is not a snake lie. It’s
a true one. It all happened in the course of a day’s
work in South Queensland. Writer one morning
killed a short, thick-set snake of a species unknown
to him. It resembled a death-adder, but was too
long for one. And it wasn t an adder, anyway.
Then he left per horse to help muster a paddock 10
miles off. Along the road he jumped down and slew

“Tyro”- “Leo Lear” (B. 22/l/’l4) thinks
the tubercular cow could be banished in no time
if all dairymen were compelled to house and hand-
feed. He’s wrong. The one cure is the tuberculin
test, with the axe or gun to follow. Many dairy-
men about Sydney keep their herds under the best
conditions, and still the diseased cow is common with
them. Not long ago the well-fed and
carefully-selected cows at Rookwood
Asylum were tested with tuberculin,
and badly-infected animals were found
among the best as well as the worst
conditioned in the mob. Some of the
poorest were acquitted, some of the
fattest were convicted. If condition
has anything to do with the disease,
why was this thus? Also, the infec-
tion of this fine herd could hardly
have come from “dried saliva left on
the grass by a sick cow,’’ as enough
grass for a cow to spit on, or a para-
site to roost on, is rarely seen in the
Rookwood paddocks. I don’t under-
stand “Leo Lear” when he says:
“You can’t breed cows for milk unless you
feed cows for milk also.” If he means that a cow
is a good, bad or indifferent milker according to
what she’s fed on, then “Leo” would be as big a
failure with cows as I am with paragraphs. A
moderate cow can’t be made good by any sort of
feeding—not if you handed her the whole bill of
fare, and told her to order any old thing she liked,
from the soup down to the toothpick.
* * * *
“Marloo” : I reckon, for bush people, ours
is a champion musical family. Spend a night with
us, and you’ll know. Grandfather’s good on the
nasal organ. Grandmother’s always busy righting
the family discords. Father spends most of his time
wetting his whistle. Mother is continually harping
on the one string. Aunty is full of crochets and
quavers. Uncle is the champion hummer of the
town. Sam (he’s my brother) is mostly exercising
at the bars. The lodger or two we’ve had practised
the scale. As for me—well, they call me the lyre
of the family.
“lad”: At Wondalga, where the Tumut,
Batlow, Adelong and Tumberumba roads shake hands,
there are the tumbled remains of one of the wildest
of the old bush pubs of 60 years ago. It was built
in the time of the Adelong rush, when miners were
as thick along the banks of the Adelong and Wilson’s
creeks as rabbits are now. A dancing hall, to which
girls were brought from Victoria, was part of the
concern. It was common enough to see 100 horses
tied along the fence, while the owners danced with

rough,
And, when his victim groaned awhile, he grunted
“That’s enough
Resistin’ th’ police is what we call your dashed
complaint.
A citizen with rights you-are? When you’re with
me, you ain’t!”
The poor bloke faintly bellowed “Help!”—but there
was none; and then
The duty-loving, zealous slop gave him some slops
again.
The copper dragged his prey into the watchhouse—
what was left
Of him —well past-protesting, both of speech and
wind bereft,
And impolitely sat him where the watch-house harvest
sits.
And the citizen just sat there, dumb, with cobwebs
on his wits.
He crouched there, cowed and coppered, just a bat-
tered human wreck,
Without a prayer to utter, feeling limply at his
neck.
The copper pulled his helmet off and wiped his
heated brow,
And took a breath or two, and at his victim shouted
“Now !’’
And to the sergeant he explained: “This feller stopped
an’ spoke
To me as if he thought I was —w’y, just an or’n’ry
bloke!
He ackshly stopped an’ arsked me for the time, an’
so”—■ “Quite right!”
The watch-house boss rejoined: “You’ve done yer
jooty well, this night! ”
Of course! When some obscure one words the cop
upon his beat,
Saying “Good-night” in passing by on sober midnight
feet,
Plainly, the cop’s clear duty is to show him who is
what,
And that the cop is anything but plain bloke—is
it not?
And, though slow courts may fine the dorg five bob,
or even ten,
The Fourth Degree is—shoulder-high, and slam-bang
down again!
VICTORIA.

“Machete”: The storm-bird or channel-
billed cuckoo has a much wider range than a recent
Bulletin correspondent gives him, for his raucous
whoop is heard all over Papua as well as Eastern
Australia. On the north-east coast of Papua, where
it rains regularly every afternoon, he is a correct
tipster, though in drier latitudes his name may be
Mud in this respect. I have often wondered why
the limits set on the migration of certain Papuan
fowls are so arbitrary. The storm-bird goes and
comes from the Australian mainland as he likes.
Yet the Papuan horn-bill, a much stronger flier
and a great wanderer, never takes his Hebrew beak
across the Str.aits. The wingless cassowary is found
on both sides' of the water, like the white cockatoos
and many parrots ; yet a common Island parrot tribe,
the males of which are scarlet and blue, while the
ladies are plain green, never by any chance visits Aus-
tralia, though it ranges all through the Solomons
and the islands north of Papua. Again, the goura
pigeon is common on the N.E. coast of Papda up to
the Musa River; but past that you never get him,
though the stream is so narrow that he could kick
his wife across it, if he felt that way.
ONE BRUTE AND ANOTHER.
SHE ( romantically): “Oh, George darling!
Supposing the lion broke loose, whom would
you endeavor to save first —me or our
child?”
GEORGE ( laconically): “Me! ”
‘Pick’: “Sumach” (B. 9/10/’l3) backs
the crow for cowardice. I entertain just the oppo-
site opinion of the Satanic fowl. He has the bump
of self-preservation well developed, and hates being
about when anyone is fooling with a gun, I’ll admit.
Yet I have seen him attack almost all animals, from
the horse downwards, and, as for other birds, there
are few that don’t give him room when he is out on
the razzle. Personally, I was once fool enough to
climb up to a crow’s nest, and the reception I got
from the wedded couple was all I wanted. Years
later I had a lot of sympathy for Tommy Burns
when J. Johnson knocked him out. I’d met a couple
of niggers myself, and I knew what it felt like.

“Taff”: Up here in the North Ah Fong
looms large as a producer of maize and potatoes,
and in marketing his goods he has nothing to learn
from Sussex-street. A few years ago the pursuit
of a crust ‘ carried me to Atherton
(Queensland). Purchases of maize
being made at the farms, it always
took a few days to get delivery at
the railway station, and if during the
interval the market advanced it was
guineas to gooseberries there would be
no delivery, or the sample offered
would be much inferior to that
originally submitted. The excuses of
Johnnie were endless. “Horse he very
sick, he no go” ; “Cart he break down,
no can get in” ; were two of the stock
lies. Threatened with law, Ah Fong
would vanish to another farm, and all
inquiries at his humpy would elicit:
“No savee, boss he no home.” The
Chinese potato-growers at Townsville
also worked a sweet joke. The crop
had turned out unsatisfactory, many
of the tubers being very small, and
therefore unsaleable. Buyers insisted, of
course, on the large variety. On
examination, the middle of every bag
was found to contain about 201 b. of
humble trash. Making a tour of in-
spection, I found one of the subtle
heathens working the swindle. He
first put a foundation of good mur-
phies in the bottom of the bag, and
then inserted a kerosene tin with
both ends cut out. After packing around
the tin with good potatoes, he filled the
tin with the rejected spuds, withdrew
it, and topped off with the pick of his
crop. Of course that swindle was
workable for one year only; but
Johnnie knows more than one swindle,
so he isn’t downhearted. Why should
he be? China has lasted 5000 years
or more, and the bottomless decep-
tion of it's pigtail hasn’t been fully
expounded yet.
‘Tiaki”: I think I know why the crowd
which buttons its collar at the base of its skull per-
sistently decries the skimpiness of the garments with
which Eve clothes herself. It is because the person
who fixes his collar aft has seen too little of Eve.
Years ago we used to watch mixed parties of 40 or
more Maoris bathing together. Meri would glide
into the water, and as she stooped down her last
garment would be drawn up over her head and flung
on shore, and her body would be hidden beneath the
waters. I never saw or heard anything indecent at
these swimming carnivals. I never heard young
Henare make any comment then on the quality of
Meri’s understandings or figure. But now that
Meri has taken to hiding her once free striding
limbs beneath a costume or hobble, it is no uncom-
mon thing to hear Henare of the cigarette and
straw hat variety commenting on the small portion
of Meri’s brown ankle which shows through the open-
work hose. And I’m quite sure that Meri who
wore the invisible bathing costume was as virtuous
in mind and body as the present-day Meri in the
tailor-made.
The Meat Trust: An Ounce of Theory
and a Ton of Practice.
With the coming of the American Beef
i rust to Australia, now a generally ad-
mitted fact, there seems to be a remarkable
disposition in some parts to close eyes to the
obvious, and hail the octopus as a benevo-
lent beast in spite of what has happened
where it has already got to business. Thus
Dalgety’s Review, a journal naturally de-
voted to the interests of the stockowner and
those connected with him commercially,
after pointing out the importance which the
meat industries are assuming in Queensland
(it is anticipated that the year will be a
record for trade done, and, with sundry pro-
ducts, that the industry will be worth “con-
siderably over £3,000,000”), complacently
remarks: —
Tlie idea held in some commercial circles that the
American company, shortly to commence its opera-
tions in Brisbane, will become an Aaron's rod to
swallow up all the others; that ultimately it will
buy up all the existing companies, and control the
whole of the marketable stock and export trade, Is
only realisable if there should not be sufficient energy
and enterprise in the present companies and stock
pioducers to compete with this invading company,
and to conserve their own interests in the market.
It would be unfortunate if this were to take place.
But all industries are open to competition, which is
the natural stimulus to work and trade, and from
which no profitable occupation can escape. The en-
trance of this company into the field ought to mean
only another competitor to be met and contended
with, and so should give a fresh impetus to the
industry in all its branches. It will naturally
increase the opportunities of the stockowners for the
sale of their stock, and quicken the activities of
the export trade. And this should be the
result. Probably, this threatened disturbance of the
customary routine of supply and demand may awaken
the State to see the great possibilities it possesses
of producing much larger meat supplies for the
world s market. Queenslanders, with 'British @pital
at their backs, ought not to be swamped out of exist-
ence in their own country.
By way of contrast, here is an extract firom
the Buenos Ayres Herald :
There has been such an astonishing belief in the
vastness of the Argentine territory and the difficulty
of obtaining anything approaching exclusive control
ot its resources, that even a month ago, in London,
the representatives df the companies most affected
by the Trust loudly declared that it was impossible
tor such an organisation to succeeds The logic of facts
however, is unanswerable, and we saw, on Saturdav
last, a frank admission by the majority of the firms
interested in the export of beef to England that the
state of affairs had now arrived at such a pitch that
urdess the Government could suggest some manner
of dealing with the difficulty, all the freezing estab-
hshments, except those belonging to the Trust, would
have to close. Shipments of beef for the last month
or two from America to England have been sold at a
loss of from 30 to 45 dollars per head. This can
only be done with a view to securing a monopoly of
the meat trade. Only an association with very des-
pci ate intentions could venture upon a business in-
volving such enormous losses as those which are ad-
mitted to be sustained by the frozen meat exporters
at the present moment. The only possible object
CO .V, , e freeze out all the companies competing
with the Trust, in order that the latter might have
the monopoly of Argentine trade. A Trust with
enormous capital behind it can afford to work at a
loss for a considerable time, when it has the confi-
dence that its competitors have no such unlimited
backing, and that once they are eliminated the lo«i
sustained during the struggle can be amply compen-
sated m the future. The proceedings of the Trust
in America are well known, and this is not a ques-
tion between the Americans and the British: it is a
question between fair and foul trading. It will be
a sorry day for Argentina if legitimate traders are
to be liable to be squeezed out of existence by
associations which are immoral in every commercial
sense of the term.
reason have we to expect that things
will be in any way different in Australia?
The Argentine companies were strong;
backed up, many of them, by British capi-
tal, they were on the whole vastly stronger
than those of Australia against which the
Trust will find itself in competition. Yet,
atter actual experience, even the Argentine
competitors of the vast Yankee combine
mid the struggle hopeless; and now that it
is too late there is a plaintive cry that “un-
less the Government can suggest some way”
to save the trade after it has been lost, “all
the freezing establishments, except those be-
longing to the Trust, will have to close.”
And the Argentine is not such a fool as to
pretend not to know what that means. The
Trust doesn’t at great cost get the field to
itself for nothing. It is out to make money
out to control both markets; out to re-
duce the buying price to the lowest possible
penny and to raise the selling price to the
uttermost possible farthing. What mad-
ness is it for Australia to assume that its
own experience will differ from that of
every other country into which the monster
has spread itself!

Tree-planting by Prison Labor.
Herewith “Norbell’s” rejoinder, and this
exhausts the subject:—
In the matter of “Lux” v. the Holman Govern-
ment on the question of afforestation by prison
labor, I regret that my learned friend has nothing
new to adduce. He repeats his former statement
that tree-planting by prison labor is a vain thing,
liable to sap the morals of the prisoner, leaving him
to lace an unfeeling world utterly unable to earn
his living honestly. Again, “Lex’' asserts that the
present prison system of N.S.W. teaches the prisoner
a useful trade, and turns him out, when his time is
up, fully equipped to earn an honest living. He
emphasises once more the perfection of N.S.W. prison
discipline, and proves that it is without fault or
flaw, by citing the fact that the criminal population
is annually decreasing in his thrice-blessed State.
Also he reiterates the statement that the afforesta-
tion scheme in Maoriland is a failure, “a notoriously
uneconomical” and “ethically pernicious failure.”
Permit me now to reassert my assertion that no
prisoner in the gaols of N.S.W. is ever taught a
trade sufficiently well to enable him to earn a bare
subsistence, let alone a decent living, in free com-
petition with the skilled but unconvicted artisan
outside. Even the Director of Prison Labor, whose
report I quoted, and who might reasonably be sup-
posed to have a partiality for liis own system, admits
the hopelessness of expecting the prison-taught
tradesman to earn a living at his trade on his re-
lease. “Official figures go to show,” says “Lex,”
that the majority of the men take to those (gaol-
taught) trades on their release, and thereafter main-
tain themselves and their families by them ” I
would challenge “Lex” to produce those incontest-
able official figures,” only I am assured that no
such official figures exist.
tvt i/,,, Lhc , efficac y> not to say perfection, of the
JN.S.W. prison system is to be deduced from the
decline of the prison population in N.S.W., what
about Maoriland ? Does not the same touchstone
apply there? The latest published statistics (“Offi-
cial Year-Book,” 1912) inform me as follows:
charges before magistrates in the year
19°7 were 40.09 per 1000 of the mean population:
in 1911, only 37.54 per 1000. In 1907, the sum-
mary convictions were 33.14 per 1000, as against
31.56 in 1911. In the superior courts 450 convic-
hons were recorded in 1907 (there were as many
ln iin 1911 there were 403. At
the end of 1906 there were 891 prisoners in Maori-
land gaols; at the end of 1911 there were 873. It
therefore follows, by “Lex's” own reasoning, that
the ethically pernicious” Maoriland system has
brought about a marked diminution of crime It
would thus almost seem as if “Lex,” in sternly re-
proving sentimentality, had fallen into misleading
sensationalism.
When this afforestation idea was first mooted
here by the Holman Government, it was expressly
laid down that the prisoners to be employed at the
tree-planting camps should be very carefullv-selected
first offenders; yet “Lex” asserts that the work
will involve the association of criminals of all de-
grees of villainy. If, as he asserts, there is an
entirely different class of offender at Bathurst Gaol
from that of Parramatta Gaol, and the “apparently
penitent are kept out of touch as much as possible
with the palpably incorrigible,” why not cull the
first tree-planting gang from the A 1 Bathurst Pen
of the Apparently Penitent, and leave the “palpably
incorrigible to learn the useful and reforming trade
of making doormats?
. accuses me of inaccuracies, and cites the
tact that I had supposed Darlinghurst to be one of
the gaols where trades were taught. I admit the
error if it be one; but I was guided by the official
report. For the rest, I assure my learned friend
that T am fully aware that “separate treatment”
and soutary confinement” are two different things
to the official mind, the latter being the term applied
solely to punishment for breaches of prison disci-
pline; but I have a very strong belief that “Lex”
knows well that “separate treatment,” so called,
means something much more severe than the two
words suggest.
Finally, I am still wedded to the opinion that
afforestation, or re-afforestation, by prison labor is
a good thing for the State and for the law-breaker.
The healthful camp life must be beneficial to the
prisoner. It should make him physically better
able to .start life again on his release than if he
were pottering about in the matmakers’ shops in
Parramatta Gaol. And on top of all is the incon-
testable fact that every tree planted is an asset for
the future. If the Maoriland scheme is an economic
failure it has certainly caused millions of sturdv
young trees to grow there, and one day they will
be of greater value to the country than a pile of
prison-made boots and doormats as high as Mount
Ngauruhoe.
FAILURE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is something in driving-rain that has a
peculiarly vivifying effect upon woman’s capacity for
amorous emotion.— I “Red Page” extract from a recent
book on love, and women, and things.
Her simple name is Mary Ann.
I thought that vulgar once, maybe;
Now from Be-ersheba to Dan
No fairer name exists for me.
Her eyes are just the sweetest things—
Their color yet I have not guessed.
Hqr hair like golden ardor clings
About a face which I attest
Is lovelier than the loveliest.
One fault alone she has, and this
Is greater than a score might be—
She has all virtues and each bliss,
But yet the vixen won’t love me.
I’ve tried my best to stir her heart;
I give her all things seeming fit
A spacious pleasure to impart.
Love's labor’s lost and all his wit—
She doesh’t seem to notice it.
So when I read that in the rain
k A maid of stone might tender grow,
To soften Mary’s callous vein,
I dragged her gaily to and fro
When fell the clouds in thunder down,
And raging seas and rivers met.
There was a chance that she might drown,
But nought of comfort have I yet.
Alas! I only “got her wet.”
VICTORIA.
SILAS SNELL.

“R. 8.”: The new Oxford movement to
’purify the English language is to turn its
attention first to “those natives of London
( and Essex who call a lady a lydy,” and it
js suggested that Australia should be in-
Icluded. But who expects correct pronun-
ciation of uneducated, book-ignorant and
‘therefore language-ignorant people? The
“movement” might better begin moving at
“Awxfud” and “Caimbwidg,” and after it
lias learned to march it should make for the
West End and correct the shocking speech
of the naicest people. Here is a little list
of West End errors:—“Naitchah” for
“nature”; “A” for “I” ; “may dair” for “my
dear”; “whair” for “where”; “quayet” for
“quiet”; “sleep-ah” for “sleeper”; “whay”
for “why”; “wiah” for “wire” ; “taime” for
“time”; “plaice” for “place”; “gyerl” for
“girl”; “mastah” for “master”; “happay”
for “happy” ; “umpiah” for “empire” ; “pur
his yers” for “pull his ears”; “taired” for
“tired” ; “chay-ar” for “chair” ; “Injur” for
India” ; “audjins” for “audience” ; “Heah !
Heah!” or “Yer! Yer!” for “Hear! Hear!!’
They drop the terminal “g,” too, and “rip-
pin’ ” is used as an adjective implying
merit; while the superlatives of “beauty” ex-
haust themselves in “Top-hole—what?” The
worst English on earth is spoken in London
by the people who feel annoyed in Paris
because their Kensington French cannot get
itself understood.
“Armes” : The most important recent de-
velopment in the Australian National Army
is in connection with “wireless” signalling.
As I write it is being practised by over 500
signallers at Heidelberg, near Melbourne.
Every Light Horse and Infantry regiment
has sent representatives. The Signalling
Corps, as a separate unit, consists of 160
officers and men, a good enough staff for a
school of instruction. Major Rossitor, w r ho
commands, finds in general great eagerness
to learn the new trick ; and indeed it is the
most delightful thing in the military experi-
ence of most of the 500. Armed with this,
see how a commander-in-chief, in the field,
will be able to handle his army. Even
modern Moltke never dreamt of such possi-
bilities. It is only a little less wonderful
than the handling of naval forces in a battle
controlled from the Admiralty Qffice.
Mounted despatch messengers will almost
disappear. The portable field stations have
a range of 20 miles. The poles, of course,
are jointed, and easily shifted. The weight
lies in the engine—-1601b. The cost of each
set is £625. So the money goes. One
trouble may be found in what is called
“jamming” ; that is to say, the enemy may
fill the air with clashing air-waves, so that in-
telligent communication between the sections
of an army may be difficult—even impossible.
Thus the Heidelberg camp picked up a mes-
sage from the steamer Orvieto, out at sea;
and while it was picking that up it couldn’t,
of course, pick up anything else.
“Thank Yer, Sir”: An Australian busi-
ness man who has just returned from a
round-the-world tour showed me the Italian
brigand’s notice reproduced below. He said
he didn’t want to go travelling any more in
low-wage countries, where the waiter and
the porter and the chambermaid had to bush-
range the hotel guests in order to make a
living. After tipping everybody in Eng-
land, bar George and Mary, the Lord Chief
Justice and a few others, he passed over to
help Europe along, and did his duty faith-
fully until h'e found this in his hotel bed-
room at Milan:—
TIPS.
Travellers who wish to give tips to the servants
are respectfully requestet to note here the amount
for each of the servants they intent to gratify, and
to intrust the direction with the distribution.
Dining-room waiters
Upstairs waiters
Maids
Valets . . * . .
Porter
Night-porter
Page boys
No. of room
That stung the free Australian citizen so
badly that he risked a«sassination by being
as mean as J. I). Rockefeller while he was
about the premises. You can’t get that man
to say a word against high wages. He has
seen the European working-man crawling
for the extra tuppence, and spitting when
fip didn’t get it, and he greatly prefers the
Australian variety who strikes occasionally
and never gets under your feet.
The Northern Territory, from the stand-
point of the plain bloke :
Port Darwin and the little towns one runs into on
the Pine Creek line possess points of interest and
amusement that few other parts of the Commonwealth
can show. I don’t intend to say anything about
the splendid climate, the prolific soil or the evident
mineral wealth of the country; all these things
have been told of before. What struck me first and
most forcibly was the way liquor was consumed, but
that was perhaps because I am a novice, falling asleep
as early us 2 a.m., thus losing two clear hours
hard drinking. Item: Dodge sam-su and “square.”
Both are guaranteed to make you empty devils out
of your boots for a week after leaving off. Sam-su
is a Chinese drink made from rice. It smells like
raw bailiff, and tastes like fried conscience and
brimstone. Oblong bottles, square bottles, round
bottles, queer shaped bottles and just bottles are to
be seen in heaps everywhere; monuments of bygone
sprees. One more feature of Darwin that impresses
the new arrival is the astonishing number of Govern-
ment officials. Clad in white boots, Panama hats
and white suits, they swarm on the landscape. As
a drunk Methodist said: “They toil not, neither do
they spin, yet Trumper in all his glory was not
clad as one of these.” There are superintendents
who don’t superintend; directors who don’t direct,
and other officials who don’t fish.
The local train runs twice weekly, and carries
more “deadheads” to the square yard than any
Domain figtree.
If any information is wanted about the back coun-
try, Darwin Government offices are the wrong places
to seek it. Rather wander round the pubs and inter-
view the men who have been on the spot.
The mining industry is not getting the attention
which it deserves; rather the reverse. There are
several mines shut down with valuable machinery on
their leases. It seems to be the policy of the Mines
Department to tear this machinery up and ship it
out of the country. I myself have been engaged in
this work of destruction. The railway line seems to
be just as far off as ever. One unduly impatient
inhabitant says that if he could get a bottle of
“square” every day till they start operations his
descendants could live and die drunk even unto the
sixth generation.
Each boat brings fresh arrivals, and the next
takes most of them away. A lot of them are from
the cities, and have the idea that the Territory is a
place where you wear white suits and tennis shoes,
and watch blaclcfellows work. When they are handed
the same old pick, shovel or axe their departure is
hurried and uncompromising.
The Territory is not the place for a man whose
only assets are his thick skull and brute strength ;
at ieast under present conditions. There are too many
of us hete now.
For a White Australian, Darwin is not the place
to come to to see his theory in practice. Stores,
laundries, etc., all carry good old Australian names
such as Yet Toong, Man Fong Tan, and so on. Even
the Government officials, hotels and boarding-houses
employ colored labor. Most plain blokes are of the
opinion that the Government officials should not
employ colored labor either in their departments
or in their homes. But, then, plain blokes don’t
count when opinions are being collected about the
Territory-—especially by the Cook Government; like-
wise the Labor Government; ditto any other old
Government.
The Glenelg railway, which Richard
Butler last year promised to have “out of
King William-street by Christmas” (loud
cheering), still lumbers the southern one-
third of Adelaide’s mile-long chief street.
The Minister for Works 1 they call all these
Ministers “Commissioners” in S. Aus.) now
suggests April as a likely time. All pas-
sengers will then be turned out on
South Terrace, but franked for a tram-ride
to the Queen’s statue, which is a little
nearer the heart of the city than the present
terminus. Doubts are, however, expressed
as to whether the tramway service can
handle whole trainloads of patronage thrown
at* it in lumps at short intervals. Sydney
does it, of course, more or less badly; and
the trams at Mosman handle whole boat-
loads more or less horribly—and a Mosman
boat-load is as large as a Glenelg train-load
any day. But it will mean some extra bump-
ing and racketing and swearing; and when
all is said and done, the new system will
probably kill and maim three times as many
people as the old train. But the train that
crawls up a wide, quiet street has been
laughed at so often that Adelaide blushes
every time the thing is mentioned; and it
will neglect a lot of more urgent things to
abolish an old institution and drop its rail-
way passengers out on the very edge of the
city.
A party of Sydney shark fishers lately
played it low down on a seaside boarding-
house keeper. The hash-tower warden, in
the interests of business, had written a let-
ter to Sydney Sun, contradicting a previous
report that there were herds of sharks wait-
ing for the summer bather in the waters ad-
jacent to the hashery. The shark hunters
thereupon organised a Sunday expedition,
journeyed up to the resort, and, hiring a
strong launch (they weren’t going to risk
their lives in the ordinary row-boat) caught
seven man-eaters within an hour, without
once shifting their moorings. The brutes
ranged from 12ft. to 16ft. in length, and
each of them thrashed the surface like the
propeller of an empty freight steamer. Then
the weary crew rubbed balm on their blis-
tered hands and wrote to the Sun record-
ing their horrifying catch of 30 yards or so of
sea-death in the place where the summer
visitor is urged (by advertisement) to come
and bathe in peace with his wife and family.
Nothing has since been heard of the hash-
tower warden ; he or she is as silent as last
pight’s stew.
On this year’s Anniversary Day the grand-
sons of “the pioneers” got back to their fore-
fathers’ way of doing it, and wound up with
a feed. The first man to strike the brilliant
idea of celebrating the birth of a nation by
eating something was Isaac Nichols, Syd-
ney’s first postmaster, away back in 1817.
The dinner was held in the house where
Nichols lived and took in the mails, at the
corner of George-street and Circular Quay,
where the fire station now stands. The
postmaster issued invitations to about 40
guests, who sat down to the tucker at 5
p.m., and did not rise till 10. It was after
this dinner that an afterwards well-known
song, composed for the occasion, was sung.
One verse:—
When first Australia rose to fame,
And seamen brave explored her shores,
Neptune with joy beheld their aim ;
And thus expressed the wish he bore;
Rise, Australia, with peace and plenty crown’d,
Thy name shall one day be renown’d ;
Then commerce, too, will on thee smile,
Adventurous barks thy port shall crowd,
While pleased, well pleased, the parent Isle
Shall of her distant sons be proud.
The dinner was such a success that for
some years it was an annual event; but if
any other original songs were sung to the
fed they haven’t been handed down as worth
repeating.
THE ONLY REMEDY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I beat her because she deserved it. She has a
horrible tongue at times, and the beating did her
good.— Convicted but courageous wife-beater , on
being remonstrated with over the back fence by a
deputation of neighbors.
Because of the devil that lives in her mouth and
the dust-storm she calls her brain,
I cut me a length of the red-gum tree and I wal-
loped her well again.
I walloped her well with the door closed tight, and
she yelled till her voice gave out.
Though she called me “Murderer!” “Wretch!” and
“Beast!”
I knew that she didn’t mind in the least
And was grateful for every clout.
She contradicted, she nagged and snarled through
the whole of a blinding day,
And I knew very well she wouldn’t stop if I gave
her her own sweet way;
She bid like a hell-cat for what she got, and I knew
as she blubbered there,
That she felt a hiding would do her good,
So I gave her good measure of muscle and wood,
And I think that I acted fair.
There’s many a woman and many a maid with a
fiend where a tongue should be,
And I know very well what I would do if they only
belonged to me;
I’d wallop them well when they needed it —which
they do about once a day.
And I wouldn’t knock off if next door cried
“Shame!”
For a maid and a wife are just the same
And the stick is the only way.
But the sour old maid who is not so old is the one
I would like to fix;
For the venom is thick on her envious tongue and
she’s never done with her tricks;
I’d wallop her well for the good of her soul (which
has lost all its maiden sheen) —
Yes, I’d wallop her well in a friendly way,
Till she hadn’t a poisonous word to say
And her mind was fresh and clean.
N.S.W.
CONSTANT WIFE BEATER.

By the Oldest Inhabitant.
February 6 was the 63rd anniversary of Black
Thursday, a day still remembered by hands
as a visitation from Hades, and to-d y OStW
SS hSSSK.' It fed n. s. iv.le ? . The next
night a great anti-transportation tea-meetmg was he
in St. Patrick’s Hall, Bourke-street, Melbourne.
The Sydney Telegraph hankers horribly
after its nigger. In a recent article on the
Territory it deplored that the white man—
with practically no railways and hardly any-
thing else to help him —hadn t farmed that
huge pastoral country; and it added that a
report’’ showed white labor to be 20 per cent,
less efficient up North than in Sydney. \\ hat
report ? And who made the report. And was
the reporter talking of the Territory in gen-
eral or the low-lying scrap where Darwin is
situated? Also was he talking through Ins
belltopper ?
Gilruth, the horse doctor who has charge
of that ill-treated steed, the Northern Terri-
tory. is going down to Melbourne to see the
Minister for External Affairs or Infernal
Nuisances. Although no one seems to know
definitely what the visit is for. there is an
impression that the groom of the wilderness
is going down to have a consultation with
the remote and inexperienced trainer and
decide how best they can save a little money
<>n the .poor brute’s feed. What is really
required is that the Territory’s present poli-
tical owners should be treated for blind
railway staggers, parliamentary roaring and
the greasy heel of opportunism. If Gilruth
is a really courageous horse-physician and
knows his job, he will recommend some such
course of treatment without delay.
A brief extract from an interview with the
president of the Sydney Wharf Laborers’
Union in a recent Saturday’s paper : —
He proceeded to sly that if the workers Stood
solidly the whole world over the working class
would win their battle, and receive for the first time
the full, result of their labor.
Surely not quite for the first time ! Leaving
out such cases as those of Adam and Noah
and Robinson Crusoe, there were our ances-
tors, the cave men, who paid no rent nor
interest nor taxes nor patent fees nor royal-
ties, nor any other exactions. Some people
reckon that the worker got the full result
of his labor during quite a large section of
the world’s histoyy—or want of history.
Then there are the Eskimo and other casual
examples to-day. And to come down to real
business and instances closer to hand, there
was New Australia, in Paraguay, where the
worker got even more than the full result of
his labor, for the colony did the parasite act
On a very poor colored community for free
land, and also cadged on a very dead-broke
Government for cash assistance. Also, there
was the Daily Post in Sydney, on which
the worker got the full result of his labor,
and the end was such utter insolvency
that the ill-paid and dreadfully-overworked
editor was diddled out of a great deal of his
salary. Labor’s weak point always lay in
its reluctance to pay the price of the best
brains to manage the job, and its refusal to
give anything like a free hand to the brains
it purchased. And as a result, there have
been a good many occasions when the toiler
got “the full result of his labor,’’ and found
it was less than he received when he only
got part of the result. Badly directed or ill-
organised labor, or labor that struggles along
with antiquated appliances (which is much
the same thing) may put in a heap of honest
endeavor and hardly earn adequate beer.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The Age surmises that Gov.-Gen. Den-
man’s break-down is due in no small measure
to the worry and difficulty of dealing with
John Bull “while six independent State vice-
roys have his ear.” Poor Bull —seven Aus-
tralian governors clamoring at one ear,
while seven Australian treasurers bite the
other!
The appointment by the N.S.W. Govern-
ment of one of its members—Flowers,
M.L.C.—to a salaried billet as President of
the Board of Fire Commissioners is an un-
usual departure. Plenty of Ministers have
glode gently out of the Cabinet and then
taken salaried jobs. Some have strained
the public conscience by making quite
sure of the salaried jobs before quitting the
Cabinet. But to take the salaried job and
yet stay in the Cabinet is rather extra, so
to speak. Some people would say it was
more than extra.
GUBERNATORIAL UNREST.—A
WARANING.
Now that the appointment of a successor to Lord
Denman as Governor-General of Australia. . . .the im-
pression caused by the recent eviction cannot have
had other than an unfortunate effect in the circles
from which Governors-General are selected.
—Sydney Telegraph grimly.
Our morbid artist endeavors to picture the
positively horrid slate of affairs which might
eventuate should all the best families at
“Home ” be goaded into more drastic action by
the ungracious behavior of our—haw !—
colonial legislators !
Tfl e announcement last week that plain
Munro Ferguson might be the next Governor-
General finished, of course, with the assur-
ance that he would be made a peer before
exportation. These peers are a weariness.
When Parliament meets, the Senate majority
might do some good work by resolving that
this country wants a Governor-General
named Mr. William Smith or Mr. Samuel
Bowler.
According to Riley, M.H.R., reported by
the Sydney Telegraph, Bobbie Burns wrote
that “rank is but the penny stamp, a man’s
a man for a’ that.” “I am pleased to say,”
said Riley, “that Burns is often quoted in
the Federal Parliament. His words help us
greatly in that they enable us to express the
correct thing.” As a matter of fact, Burns
was referring to the borrowing N.S.W.
Labor party, and really wrote: “Our policy’s
a penny stamp—we ‘stick it up’ for a’ that.”
For many years S.A. and Queensland
fought for the distinction of being the most
pawned State on earth in proportion to popu-
lation. Now Westralia, under the Scaddan
Government, has won in a canter. Last
financial year it borrowed at the rate of
£ll 2s. Bd. per inhabitant. So it is getting on
gaily towards a liability of £lOO per human,
including the oldest inhabitant and the
baby; and all its rivals toil amid the dust
cloud in the rear with the dogs running after
them and barking in mockery.
ROUGH ON
THE EAR.
The Governor-General is a Governor-General in
name only. He is merely one of seven independent
Viceroys, nil of whom have the ear of the King.
—Melh. AGE, 27/l/’l4.
The great art of thankfulness for small
mercies, being an extract from a recent S. M.
Herald leader: —
We are glad that both the Prime Minister and the
Minister for Defence have during the last few days
emphasised the importance of proceeding with the
work of defence in Australia UNFLINCHINGLY....
The Australian people have resolved to see this thing
through.
It is very good of Joe Cook to be so
darned unflinchingly in defence of a policy
which he treated, as long as he
dared, with scorn and loathing and
abuse. The Federal Labor Party carried
the defence scheme amidst the breakage of
the heart of Joseph and the horror of
Joseph’s associates. Now Joseph finds that
it has come to stay, and he is unflinching—
with his jaw anyhow—in its support. He is
the sort of leader who has to be dragged be-
hind the vehicle at the end of a rope until
the destination is reached. Then he proudly
takes his place in front and looks as if he had
been hauling the carriage all the time.
From Canberra, the Federal capital site
of a young nation that had a million places
to pick from :
This decayed village of mean shacks called Quean-
beyan has taken on a new life since the advent of
the Federal capital by supplying the toilers with
life s necessaries at 50 per cent, on city prices. It
likewise empties the village offal in the dreary trickle
which sometimes meanders past its untidy back door,
and which the misguided map, with rare enthusiasm,
calls a river. The inhabitants of the village indulge
but little in the pagan habit of bathing ; and as the
water isn’t fit to drink they mostly don’t mind it
not being laid on. The sanitary arrangements can be
smelt for miles; and the mighty Molonglo, from
which the eaoital of a continent is to draw its
water, is so dried up you can walk across it in a
dozen places, and in the other places it smells almost
as bad as the sanitary system.
Is the Sydney wharf-laboring profession
hopelessly overcrowded, or what is wrong?
The wages are Is. 6d. per hour in ordinary
working time and 2s. 3d. for overtime, and
most of the hours of the 24 are overtime.
Yet it is solemnly alleged by an ex-president
of the union and other persons who ought to
know, that the average earnings are between
2bs. and 30s. per week. If plain time and
overtime are lumped together at Is. 9d. per
hour that means 16 or 161 hours work a
week as a steady thing. The demand for an
increase is based on the alleged inadequate
results under the present scale, to which the
shipowners reply by alleging that the absurd
numerousness of the men is the trouble, and
that no wage they can afford to pay would
keep them all in comfort. By way of re-
joinder, a good deal is said about the loss of
time while waiting for jobs owing to the
casual habits of ships. At a recent wharf-
labor meeting it is alleged that 3500 members
of the union attended, which seems a tre-
mendous lot at one meeting, but still Sydney
is a whale for commerce. An independent
investigation of this matter should be inter-
esting.
. A persistent rumor in N.S.W. Labor
circles just now is that Premier Holman will
not lead the narty at next general election.
The bulk of the crowd (the non-parliamen-
tary section of it) is sore over his attitude
on the referenda, though it doesn’t seem to
care a straw about the fact that he turned
dog on the party’s alleged decentralisation
policy, and went back on its pledge to reduce
borrowing. These are big, national
matters, so what the devil do they
signify ? Holman is credited with
knowledge when to flee out of the wet. So
the general impression is that he will build
himself an ark of durable materials and float
to the Agent-General’s office in London.
“H.H.” writes: — I
I don’t know all the iniquities the Botha Govern-
ment may be accused of, but I feel kind of sorry
for it. It is up against the Jingo crowd, which
thinks it disgraceful that men with names like Botha
and Smuts should run the show. It isn’t too well
liked by the cosmopolitan mine-owning gang of
capitalists, who promoted the late war so as to bring !
Jo berg under British rule and get cheap Chinese
labor. Now that Jo’berg is under Boer rule, though
the British flag is flying, and the cheap Chinamen
have been chased out of the country, this crowd,
which owns most of the papers, and used to fill them |
with alleged Boer “outrages,” doesn’t absolutely
weep on Botha’s capacious Dutch and Protestant
bosom. Then there are the innumerable niggers and |
the Hindus, who are hostile to any white Govern-
ment ; and the missionary push which wants the j
nigger treated as a brother on religious grounds; j
and the Industrial Workers of the World, who want
him to be a brother because he is a fellow-toiler.
Among all these, Botha must have a hard row to I
hoe, and is probably a soured voortrekker. If one
could imagine Ireland, with the addition of, say,
16.000,000 colored savages—that would make the
proportion about right—who were equally prepared
to cut the throat of Belfast or knock out the brains
of Cork as soon as trouble started, and almost
any ruler would bp prepared to deport almost any-
body rather than let the trouble develop. Then
Botha probably likes the men—mostly English—who
made the late industrial row just as much as Brother
Snowball likes a Roman Catholic. And he has
more reason, for he fought round Ladysmith in
person, while Snowball didn’t carry a rifle at the
Boyne. Probably the Boer Prime Minister didn’t
wait to analyse his feelings or sort out his reasons.
There must have been a joy in calling out, on any
pretence, the old armed forces of Boerdom, and
seeing them again in good working order. Also, in •
depriving the hated British garrison of any excuse
to interfere on behalf of law’n’order, and 'thus ex-
hibiting its beastly superfluousness. Also, in seizing
by the collar and the slack of the pants certain mem-
bers of the race that lowered the old vierkleur—-the
flag of red, white, blue and green—and starting them
off illegally to London, of all places in the world—
London, in which were brewed so many Boer mis-
fortunes. In the recent disturbance, Louis Botha
probably couldn’t disoblige all the people he would
like to have aggravated, but as an aristocratic land-
holder and an old fighting Boer, he tried, I think,
to gratify his own political sentiments, and, at the
same time, to annoy as many of his ancient foes as
he could.
AT THE RACES.
On Wednesday morning Mr. George Reid will be
present at the Sanatorium Race Meeting on the Epsom
Racecourse. The winner of the principal event will be decorated by Mr. Reid with a blue ribbon, presented
by the Mayoress.
We trust that the sporty nag didn't mistake George for a punching ball.
Affairs are looking up in Freetrade Eng-
land, for George V. has fixed the minimum
wage for laborers on his Sandringham estate
at 16s. per week, whereas the regular local
rate is 14s. And yet England isn’t a land
of cheap living. That, country imports stacks
of food from Australia —wheat, flour, butter,
bacon, ham and sundries —because it can
get them as cheaply in Australia as any-
where else. And Bull acquires this food at
Australian rates plus freight. It doesn’t
spell gorgeous luxury for the sixteen-shilling
laborer, but then a bacon-rind goes far among
the Bull poor.
A SUICIDE TOO LONG DEFERRED.
Mr. Cook has definitely announced his intention of
forcing a dissolution at an early date. — News item.
A USTRALIA: "lt's time you put yourself out
of your misery , Joe. You've posted enough
notices about it."
It was proposed at the Labor Conference
sitting in Sydney that the penal clauses of
the Arbitration law should be repealed so
that the person who now unlawfully doesn’t
care a hang for the law should in future be
legally authorised not to care a hang for the
law. It would be cheaper to repen l the
whole business. A statute with no penalty
attached for breakage is so very little of a
statute that it isn’t worth spending money
on it.
Premier Scaddan of Westralia is appar-
ently filled with a joyous resolve to have a
good time and blow the consequence. His
shortage at June, 1912 was over £134,000;
next year it was over £190.000; this year it
is estimated at over £135,000. If Britain
shortaged at the same rate it would have
gone about £70,000,000 to the Devil in three
years. And Scaddan the Madman has got
his borrowings up to £ll per head per an-
num, which bursts the worst records of the
past twice over. Scaddan calls himself a
Laborite, but the term Slave-dealer would
describe him better. For selling posterity
in bondage to the capitalist he holds the
record.
THE PAWNSHOP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Holman rejoices in the cheaper money phase of
the London market. — Telegram from Maoriland, which
the N.S.W. Premier is visiting.
Thank Gawd, the pawnshop’s open,
It’s Monday morning now,
When dawns fresh light and hope in
Each eye and heart and brow !
We starved through dreary Sunday,
While not a bob we raised:
But Uncle starts on Monday,
For which the Lord be praised!
We’ve pawned the bed, the table,
The carpet and the chairs;
If only we were able
We’d pawn the blooming stairs!
The chimney-top will go in
The pop-shop like a flash,
So long as Uncle Cohen
Will find the ready cash.
Untouched by care or worry,
Oh, let us borrow more,
And bravely let us hurry
To reach the pop-shop door;
No cloud the sky discloses,
We see no chance of hurt
While Reuben, Ike and Moses
Will lend upon our shirt.
So Holman, use your brilliance,
And workman, use your brawn,
To borrow yet more millions,
And put yourselves in pawn.
Since borrowing is easy,
Let all your future go
Into the fingers greasy
Of Reub and Ike and Mo.
N.S.W.
M.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Billy” Bolton, who passed out at North-
cote (Meib.) recently, might have died very rich but
for the bookmakers. As it was, he came down to the
old age pension level. He took up land under Duffy’s
Act in 1869, and prospered exceedingly as a farmer,
stock salesman and butcher. Then he weht in for
horse-breeding, and made a lot of money at that; but
meanwhile he had acquired a passion for the Turf.
Breeding horses had given him a desire for backing
them, and he stuck to the turf long after it had be-
come too corrupt an institution for honest old sports
like “Billy” Bolton. The books got all his money.
And they didn’t even gather in force at his funeral.
It took some of the English billiard ex-
perts a week to discover that George Grey will soon
play his heart-breaking “losers” off the red as easily
with the ivory as with composition balls, and that
his recent match with Newman was merely a trial
gallop for a championship race. If John Roberts of
old could change from ivories to bonzolines' and back
to ivories without losing any of his supremacy over
his rivals, there never should have been any question
as to j'oung Gray being able to adapt the strength of
his bonzoline stroke to a different kind of ball. He
could not have done it to perfection without practice,
but he has now had sufficient practice. He will pro-
bably win the championship with ivory balls, and
then the question of altering the rules of the game
in order to queer the Gray-stroke will be decided
anon by gate-money considerations. If the public
are wearied by the monotony of hundreds of consecu-
tive strokes from baulk, the striker will probably be
restricted, by a new rule, to the centre spot on the
baulk line. But until the Gray-stroke begins to
affect the cash value of professional billiards the
Association will have no reason for further meddling
with the rules.
Football with its casual barracking of
plaj'ers and occasional obliteration of the umpire,
isn’t in it with the quiet game of quoits, the amuse-
ment in which you stand a yard or two off and
throw a few iron rings on a peg. Melbourne, for its
sins, suffers from a Quoits Association, which owns
a “ground” of its own in North Melbourne, and
periodically holds tournaments or jousts. At its
most recent one matters were willing, to put it
mildly. Isaac Shortodds was prevalent and appar-
ently one particularly brawny player, and his
backers were determined to collect the stakes and
wagers somehow. Unfair play led to argument, and
when the discussion was over, one. player had had
his cheek laid open with an iron quoit, another had
lost his sight through a kick on the eye and minor
damages were frequent. The Ass. officials witnessed
all this but took no steps beyond hiring a few
policemen for the rest of the tourney, and of course
the, brawny player won. It is not a difficult matter
to win at quoits if you can kick your opponent’s eye.
Whether Isaac Shortodds was an accomplice or a
victim doesn’t appear.

The first round of District-Club Cricket
in Adelaide shows ..East Torrens, the team including
Mayne, Whitty, Middleton, the Chamberlains and
Andy Smith, too strong for the rest; Adelaide a
bad second. East Torrens has lost only one match
in 2J seasons.
* * * *
Sydney on Saturday last was a good place
to be out of. The morning! brought sweltering
heat and a haze of smoke ; the afternoon was broken
up by a southerly storm. It was the commencement
of the ninth round of Sydney First-Grade Cricket,
but the storm swept most of the players from the
various pitches. At Parramatta there was thunder
and lightning, and a sky so gloomy that Central
Cumberland, which was at home to Redfern, sug-
gested making an afternoon of it in the pavilion.
So there was no play there at all. On some other
fields they got in about an hour of battifig when
the sky split and the bottom fell out of the deluge.
Still, at University Oval the ’Varsity got loose on
the Gordon bowling, and slogged it unmercifully, and
when the rain came the Students had piled up 355
for the loss of four wickets. Of this huge total the
gentle Barbour was responsible for 152, and his
club mate Tozer hit up 57. They were the only
batsmen of note that afternoon. Glebe, up against
Paddington, lost eight wickets for 165. North Syd-
ney had hit 36 off the Waverley bowling for the
loss of two wickets when the storm ended things.
Balmain came into possession of 37 for one wicket
off Western Suburbs, and Sydney banged 45 for two
wickets off the bowlers of Middle Harbor. Among
the bowlers the greatest were C. Winning and R. B.
Stafford. The former took three Glebe wickets for
Paddington for 31. and the latter (of the same
team) took three Glebe wickets for 39. The match
between University and Gordon was a very brilliant
affair. The Students hit up their 355 in 155mins.,
and that in a wre.tched light. When the glim was
at its worst 50 were put up in 22min., and when
Farrar left 78 had been compiled in 33min. Farrar’s
own contribution was 36, and included seven fours.
When Tozer joined Barbour they hit up 128 in
47min. Barbour’s huge score of 152 was made in
a trifle less than two hours.
The Rev. Waddy and most of his cric-
keters are now back in Australia, and report
having had a mighty good time in Ceylon. Accord-
ing to the batting parson, the team saw no sign of
resentment on the part of the Cingalese over the
case of the colored doctor who came along as tem-
porary medical officer of the Orontes. The team
spent 25 days in Ceylon, played nine matches and
won eight. The other was against an all-Cingalese
team. Minnett was the top scorer of the tour with
37, and the best bowling was that of Cameron, with
33 wickets for an average of seven.
On Saturday, on wickets that hadn’t been
improved by a wetting the night before, Melbourne
pennant cricketers tackled the tasks set them by
their opponents on the Saturday previous—and mostly
failed badly. ’Wood had gathered in a moderate
208 for its first innings, and East felt cheerful;
but Ryder found a bumpy spot and bowled his dead-
liest, getting nine for 67. East’s total was 132.
P’ran, closing with 308 for two wickets (Healy 136
n.o. and Stephens 149 n. 0.), demolished Northcote
for 118, of which Bevy made 68. On a second go
the bell rang when nine ’Cote wickets were down for
95 narrow squeak. It was P’ran’s first victory
this season. North likewise closed, with nine down
for 295, then ran the Students through for 110, but
couldn’t repeat the performance in the time. Fitz-
roy had scored 197 at its first try, and
should have dogwalloped Carlton; but its bowlers
failed to leap to the opportunity, so the old Blue-
and-Whites put up 267 for five wickets, and gathered
in the two points. Veteran Warne made 67 n.o
and inter-Stater Sewart 100. Richmond and
Essendon galloped through the better part of four
innings in a violent hurry, the former making 138
and 130 for nine wickets and the latter 190 and a
lamentable 30, whereby Richmond gained three points.
Melbourne collected 330 in the first innings of its
practice match with St. Hilda, and then polished off
the latter for 113, ex-Queenslander Ironmonger tak-
ing nine for 30, which was a feat. Another feat
was the stumping of four Saints by Henty ; but he
discounted this largely by allowing 26 extras. Still,
he did his share.

WHIRROO!
The fighting spirit of Old Ireland arises in
the Irish-American battle at the Sydney
Slonchium. Eddie McGoorly wins on points
over Pat Bradley.
The cricket match between Tasmania and
N. S. Wales at Sydney Cricket Ground, which started
on Iriday last, was not an exciting episode. The
lassies were dismissed for 163, the top score being
Eltham’s 46. His best assistants were Robinson
with 33 and Myers with 31. On Saturday the Ma
State team got going, and lifted 403 on to the
board for the loss of five wickets. It was plain
assault and battery. Of this huge score, Macartney
smote 101 and carried his bat. Cranney made 75,
Taylor 83, Ratcliife 51, and Andrews (n. 0.) 43. On
Monday N.S.W. continued. Macartney lifted his
little score to 110, and the side piled up 451. Tas-
mania. faced with a deficit of 288 on the first
innings, just crumpled up, and was disposed of for
108, N.S.W. thus winning by an innings and 180
runs. There is no joy in this sort of thing, and it
is mere waste of good voice to crow.
* * * *
The Australian XI at Auckland (M.L.)
dismissed Auckland for 251, the only show of stub-
born resistance being put up by Hemus, who perpe-
trated 112 off the N.S.W. bowling before he was
bagged by Noble off McKenzie. Sundries, 21, also
gave some trouble. Australia then went to the
sticks, and hit up 35 by nightfall without losing anv
timber. On Saturday the battue continued, Waddy
(130), Armstrong (128), Ransford (159) and the
rest lifted the score to 655 for eight wickets. On
Monday the Australian innings came to a sudden and
startling close, the last two wickets falling in nine
minutes, and leaving the total score 658. With 407
m arrears, Auckland lost heart or abandoned its
stomach or something. It gave up the ghost in the
second innings for 186, and so lost by an innings
and 221 runs. There is no joy about this.
The first leg for the Northcote Cup was
an easy win for the Sydney yacht Rawhiti 11. But
the second bout was one to be remembered. Victoria
was represented by the Killara and the Rip. When
they drifted down to the line there was hardly a
breath of air, but overhead clouds were gathering
fast and forked lightning was sprinting over the
billowing masses, while grumbling thunder gave
warning of what was to come. A sudden puff danced
on cloven hoofs across the water. The Rawhiti 11.
shook out a balloon jib and caught it, and went
after the Killara, which had drifted over the line.
Then there came a warm, hot wind from the west,
and away went the Killara like a fleeing insolvent.
The Rip went after with a bound, and the Rawhiti
was left in the rear. At the first flag, off Quaran-
tine, the Killara was 12sec. ahead of the Rip, and a
minute ahead of the Rawhiti, and was walking
through the water beautifully. Then Rawhiti began
to increase the pace, and in a little while Rip was
third, and Rawhiti was racing after the fleeing
Killara. Hereabouts the western wind failed utterly,
and a dead calm fell down on the harbor and left
the sails flapping lazily. But the corpse lasted for
a very brief space. Out of the stillness came a tear-
ing southerly. All in a moment the premises were
foaming and boiling like a pot. Killara went away
with the first blast, and rode, on the wings of the
wind, as the poet says in his sober moments. The
Rip also flew faster and passed Rawhiti. But they
had more sail than they could carry, and it was a
case of shorten or go under. Rawhiti had done
her reefing in the shelter, but had lost 14min. doing
froth, and Killara, with shortened sail, was slogging
through it, gaining all the time. Rip, over-canvassed,
' vas . hopelessly out of it, and Rawhiti was far
behind. The race was as good as won—when, with
a report like a gun the mainsail split, and Killara
was a deposed monarch. The only question was
whether Rawhiti could overhaul the rolling Rip.
It did, and finished with 25sec. to spare. But, all
the same, it was luck, and not navigation, that gave
Rawhiti the second leg-in, and so kept the Northcote
Cup in Sydney.
* * * *
The Sydney Sailing Club had an awful
time in the squall that swept Port Jackson on Satur-
day. When the boats got under way the north-
east breeze was light as thistle down. It was more
quiet and peaceful than the tread of a cat’s feet, or
the stepping of an unsupported rumor. But shortly
after 4 p.m. it chopped round, and hot, vicious
blasts came driving out of the west and toppled over
the Mona at the new Pile Light. It blew the sails
right off the Eagle. It picked up and dashed down
the Acme, 11. C. < Press and Quibree, and a round
dozen only avoided capsize by lowering away every-
thing all at once. Only seven of the 23 starters
managed to battle through it. The police boat and
the pilot steamer came to the rescue of the swamped
and capsized squadron, and while they fished the
crews out of the water the Pastime flew on to victory
in spite of its huge sails, followed by the Golding,
Eunice and Kismet. It was a great afternoon for
ducks, bunyips, whales, teal, mermaids and other
marine fowl.
Dear B., —There was something of a hustle
last week in Sydney haunts where they wager and in-
dulge in illicit acts. The reason was (1) it was
announced that the installation of the Tote was
almost a certainty during the coming session; (2)
the press gave it out that betting dens were going to
be raided much the same as opium and other joints.
Everybody was preparing to get out. But in the
end nobody got out, no raids were made and nothing
happened. Ichabod Shortodds came out bold as brass
on Saturday when Athenic, which had been a hot
favorite for the Newmarket Handicap, went to
pieces, joined the halt and lame, and blew to smith-
ereens the hopes of those who had got in early.
Ichabod had the laugh, for he knew so well that, an
hour before the announcement of the breakdown,
he was laying 10 times the odds he had laid 24
hours previously.—Yours etc., Blow the Odds.
* * * *
The disappearance of Daddy Levien from
N.S.W. Legislative Assembly is a matter of much
concern to certain people who frequent the race-
courses of the Ma State. Daddy’s annual Bill to
legalise the Tote on N.S.W. racecourses always got
lost, tripped up, bogged, or killed by accident; and
while Daddy was on the job early each session with
a foredoomed Bill no one else could get a say. Now,
anything is possible, and it is known that about half
the present Cabinet is virulently in favor of the
introduction of the Tote and the other half is mildly
opposed to it. The position is full of danger. Also,
there is a suspicion that the use of the Tote is NOT
illegal under the present Act. After all these years,
when things were taken for granted, some new law
authority has got his eye on the matter, just as the
belated theologian got on to the story of the Creation
in Genesis, and the new man thinks the old opinion '
is aged bosh. The whole question is whether in-
vesting money in the Tote can be considered “wager-
ing.” If it is a form of wagering or betting, then
there is nothing to prevent any club installing the
Tote right away. And there is more than one club
anxious to make the experiment. So Ichabod Short-
odds is perturbed. To end the suspense he pro-
poses to get a Bill introduced to define “betting” as
the making of a wager with a person “registered
to lay the odds.” It should take a fair amount of
bribery and corruption to get that through.
This coming Saturday will see the Yankee
athletic team in all its warpaint at Sydney Sports
Ground ; and it will be the best lot that has crossed
the path of the N.S.W. Athletic Association for many
a long day. The team is well worth seeing, and it
is up to the Sydney public to blow along and waste
some shekels at the gate.
N.S.W. amateur champion cyclist Stanley
Burtenshaw, who goes to Bananaland to uphold the
tail of the Ma State in the Australasian Champion-
ships, takes on the office without having to submit
to the formality of a “test.” He is so easily above
the balance of the pedallers that the N.S.W. Cyclists’
Union passed him the honor with both hands. C.
M. Cutliffe will be the second string in the Ma
fiddle in case of the champion blowing up or other-
wise going wrong.
The bottom fell out of the Australian
Sculling Championship match last week when chal-
lenger Prank Hagney failed to materialise with the
final £4O deposit. So Charlie Towns retains the
title and the gauds appertaining thereunto. But
how has the glory departed when an Australian cham-
pionship falls through for the lack of a miserable
40 sovereigns!
Miss Edith Carr, a well-known Bathurst
(N.S.W.) lady rifle-shot, beat all her previous bests
last Saturday, when at 800yds. in 15 shots she
threw 12 bullets into the cow’s eye and scored three
“inners.” Officially the score was 72 out of a
possible 75. The nearest were two male competitors,
P. Neale and E. Tennant, who each scored 70. The
conditions were unfavorable. During the whole of
the shoot a strong wind was blowing in fitful gusts
right across the range.
Milburn Saylor, the long-armed freak
Yankee fighter with the enormous punch, came to
grief at Brisbane last Saturday night, when he fell
into the arms of Eddie Newhouse, of Victoria. Find-
ing the Victorian an awkward customer, Saylor held
him with the left and drove his right at the Aus-
tralian’s jaw. As he had already been cautioned
several times, the indignant referee took a hand, and
immediately awarded the laurels to Newhouse. It is
understood that this little episode makes Saylor
lose a certain bet involving anew suit of clothes.
The result was a surprise. Another mild sensation
was the defeat of McCoy in Melbourne by Nat Wil-
liams. Each weighed 9st. 91b., and the house was
enthusiastic towards McCoy. But Williams proved a
skilful and elusive foe, who baffled the rapid Vic-
torian with still greater rapidity. Right up to the
18th round McCoy was a bewildered bruiser who
could not understand where his enemy had escaped
to or how he did it. But in the 18th McCoy got
home a right cross, and Williams wandered round
and pervaded the place generally, being all but out.
After that he was on the defensive and was more
elusive than ever, but as the referee reckoned he
had the majority of the points for the fight he gave
him the verdict. The decision was received with
howls of protest.
* * * 41-
Little Waldemar Holberg, the Danish
boxer, left Australia by the Sonoma on Saturday last
for Yankeeland. He was one of the pluckiest fight-
ers that ever came to this country, but bad
luck dogged him persistently during the whole of
his stay in Australia. Asa warrior he is very much
handicapped by the brevity of his reach, and many
of the men he had to meet carried their top-pieces
quite out of the Dane’s field of action. But Waldemar
cannonaded their bodies when he couldn’t reach their
heads, and his plucky fight against Hughie Mehegan
will be remembered for years. On that occasion he
fought himself to a standstill.
Expectation ran high when Eddie
McGoorty and Pat Bradley entered the ring at Syd-
ney Stadium last Saturday night. The betting was
-.10 to one “on,” and dozens of gentlemen who seemed
to think they were gifted with prophetic powers
laid two to one against Pat Bradley lasting five
rounds. Some reckless wights laid odds, he wouldn’t
last one. McGoorty did not advance to the attack
with the same contemptuous self-assurance with
which he swept down on Smith. He approached as
he would approach any boxer unto whom he had
respect. McGoorty looked about half a stone
heavier, although at the afternoon weighing there
was only about 2oz. difference. Certainly, he was the
taller, and blessed with the longer reach. And he
had the skill to make use of it. There was no sign
of trepidation about Patrick. He didn’t hang back
in the breeching; stilt ,jless did he flee in terror
from the scene at the sight of his adversary. He
came out as he always comes, and began captiously,
as is his wont. MpGoorty got a specimen Pat’s
goods in the first round when, stepping in to deliver
a left hook, the top side of his cheek received a
swing which, had it landed a couple of inches lower,
would have meant mischief. It soon became obvious
that McGoorty had noted the fact that Pat’s, wallops
were loaded, and h,e was not there to take risks.
The audience cheertd madly when Pat went to his
corner at the end of the first round uninjured.
And when he survived theg'second round and seemed
to some to have more than held his own, the whole
lunatic asylum broke lO'tfse. But McGoorty was
playing the right game. He was there to defend his
championship. It was not a fight to be fought with
a sudden rush. This fellow was -made of sterner
stuff than Smith, and all the he was liable
to sting with either hand. St)^ 1 McGoorty settled
down to outpoint Bradley, and did it. Slowly but
surely Patrick was worn down. But he always
came back with the ghost of a smile on his lips.
He was up against a man who could hit just a shade
faster, and the death-laden smite that Pat was going
to let flv so often was anticipated. And with
anticipation the aim went wild. At the end it
resolved itself into a determined struggle on the part
of Bradlev to keep on his feet for 20 rounds, and,
knowing that he could still sting and that the battle
was actually won, McGoorty did not risk being
knocked out by going in to get what he did not
really require. Which was sound wisdom on the
part of McGoorty.
The general note of comment on the fight is one
of disappointment at McGoorty’s showing, and
that thoughtful fighter is now suffering for the ease
with which he.knocked out Smith. That has been
accepted as his staiidard,' and every time he fails
to live up to it he will receive hostile comment from
the crowd, if not from the press. It is very absurd,
but it is a fact. As far as this winter is concerned,
McGoorty deserves more praise for beating Bradley
on points in 20 rounds than he did for blotting out
the radiance of Smith in half, a round. That, night,
anyhow, Smith could not fight. It was not a battle,
but a battue. On the other hand, Bradley could
fight, and fought like the Devil ; but plucky, tough,
and hard hitting as Bradley was to the finish,
McGoorty beat him decisively, and surely it is better
to decisively beat on points a man who covers
himself with glory than to wreck one who had
temporarily left his glory at home along with the
cat and his old clothes. There is now a possibility
of McGoorty being matched with Jerry Jerome, and
Duke Mullins, that master-maker of fighters, has
undertaken to get Jerry fit. DT-ke Mullins has a
stout heart, but this writer is willing to wager it is
utterly broken before he can get the colored brother
into the white man’s ideal state of fitness for a
battle. Anyhow, if the battle does take place it will
be mighty interesting, and most of all to Eddie
McGoorty.

"O.K.” : A large majority of officahs com-
plain that the prohibition of the sale of in-
toxicants by the canteens at the military
training camps has tended to demoralise the
public service in the interests of Private
Enterprise. There is more drunkenness
among the men than of old, because they
get from outside quite as much liquor as they
formerly bought in camp, and the quality is
not so good. The canteen profits, which were
devoted to regimental purposes, are now
diverted to the pockets of licensed publicans
and skinners. What was intended for a
compulsory temperance movement is merely
benefiting the intemperance cause at the ex-
pense of military discipline. Even the tee-
total officahs, who hate the very name of
derrink, would rather have it sold in the
canteens than brought to camp by the men
under their belts or smuggled in for future
consumption. Tbe liquor traffic at the can-
teens could be kept under reasonable con-
trol, whereas the sale of poison to soldiers
at the pubs' is beyond control, and the same
men who would have gone comparatively
sober to bed after a booze on the spot are
now likely to come rolling home in a state
of insubordination. Of the two evils the
carrying of drink into camp on the quiet is
preferable to the rowdy way of bringing it
along, therefore a majah who would punish
any other breach of the regulations finds
himself winking at the grog smuggle. Better
for the men to carouse in moderation on the
premises than to be fined as drunk-and-dis-
orderlies by magistrates outside. Finally,
the non-Wowser officahs consider themselves
aggrieved in being doomed to suffer a dry
canteen. They also are as malcontents in a
temperance township where the contents are
a ridiculously small minoritv.
