It is not often that an athlete comes back
so decisively as Maoriland's champion pro. ped.,
W. F. Trembath, did at the M.L. Cash Champion-
ships. This great half-miler retired two years ago,
but recently began to look towards the track again.
The experts reckoned he couldn’t come back, but at
Auckland he dead-heated in the 220yds. with T. J.
O’Callaglian in 22 2-ssec., won the 440yds. as he
liked in 53 3-ssec. ; and cantered home in the half-
mile, 20yds. ahead of the field, in lmin. 59 l-ssec.
On the last day of the meeting the officials thought
they’d like to see the champion really extended
over his favorite distance, so they put him on scratch
in a special half-mile handicap, and sprinkled the
track in front of him, out towards the limit mark,
with all the alleged champions they could rake up.
When the gun went, Trembath didn’t appear to have
a dog's chance of making up those handicaps; but,
running beautifully, he raced through the field in
great style, and broke the worsted with something
to spare, in lmin. 55 4-ssec. This is the fastest
competition half-mile ever run in Maoriland by
either an amateur or a pro.
“Moana”: Athletically Auckland (M.L.)
is pawing the air just now because, in an Invitation
Scratch Race, its pet amateur, A. W. Dormer, beat
Power, of Dollarland, by five yards over a mile in
4min. 24 3-ssee. In spite of all the enthusiasm,
however, I do not think that Dormer, great runner
though he undoubtedly is, can honestly be consid-
ered a match for Power, fit and well. The Auck-
land ped. was wound right up for this event. He
certainly ran the race of his life ; but he finished all
out. The Yankee boy never struck form in the
Borrowing Isles. He was dead stale all the time,
and had great difficulty in running the mile under
4min. 40sec. It was only the extra training, the
dry weather, the hard track and about a ton of
grit that put him as close to Dormer’s heels as he
got. You have only to see Power run, however, to
realise what a genius he is in that particular line.
Last year he ran all the season in the States, and
never took longer than 4min. 22sec. to cover a mile.
Several times the watches showed better than 4min.
20sec., and on one occasion the time was 4min.
17 l-ssec.
* * * *
“Len” Brown, the Queensland Rhodes
scholar, who has represented Oxford and England
at Rugby football with such brilliancy, is the son
of the Traffic-Manager of Queensland Railways. \
brother, Guy, made some good scores in inter-State
cricket for Queensland some seasons ago. “Len’s”
fault as a forward in Australia was a touch of slow-
ness. As he is now reckoned the best forward in
England, he must have hurried up.
An old critic’s verdict: Cricket form in
Australia is not really as inconsistent as it looks,
although N.S.W., which takes the Shield, has lost to
S.A., while Vic., which can’t even extend N.S.W.,
has won the only match in which it was up against
S.A. Ma really is too good for the rest, who are
about equal to each other. The surprise packet
is South Aus., which on its day may do anything.
Last year it slaughtered, on its own Oval, N.S.W.
by an innings (Massie not coming off on his first
visit to the ground that has damaged so many bowl-
ing reputations) ; then two fairly close wins over
Vic. sent the Shield to hang in Adelaide’s Art Gal-
lery for a year. This season S.A. won in Sydney,
by a bare 19 runs. Crawford did that, and it is
in Crawford that the inconsistency is to be found.
On his day he is a World’s XI man; but his day
comes only sometimes. All the States have fine bats-
men, but it is bowling that wins matches. Given
Crawford and Whitty in form, S.A. may beat Bards-
ley, Trumper, Macartney and co. any time; but last
month, with Whitty away with a leg, and Crawford
“off” with a thumb, S.A. descended to a fairly even
draw with the Maorilanders. To express the incon-
sitency in figures, it seems barely 2 to 1 on N.S.W.
against S.A., quite 6 to 1 on Ma against Vic., and a
level-money chance between the other two.
Ex-Sydney mermaid Annette Ivellerman
and her swimming partner, Robert Brennan, lately
met an unusual sort of accident at Hamilton, in
Bermuda. They were doing Annette’s nymph act in
a glass tank filled with SOOOgals. of water. The
blessed tank burst, and a cataract of water swept
Annette and her companion over the jagged edge of
the broken glass, which cut them so badly that they
had to be hurried to the hospital.
* « * »
Canterbury, the boss cricketing province
of Maoriland and holder of the Plunket Shield, has
been having a strenuous time lately. Seven of its
best men were in the team that was taking violent
leather-hunting exercise in Australia recently, so
rival provinces considered the time was ripe to get
away with the bauble. Otago and Wellington hurled
in challenges that demanded immediate decision, so
the Canterbury selectors picked a gang of promising
boys and engaged in prayer. The Scotchmen from
Otago came along first, and did up 187. Canter-
bury replied with 189 (Caygill 47), and things were
interesting. But the boy defenders fell upon Otago
in the second innings, and sent the team back to
the pavilion for a paltry 71 ; Monaghan (four for 19)
and Wilson (three for nine) doing the damage.
Canterbury knocked up the required runs for four
wickets, and felt like cabling the tourists in Aus-
tralia that they could stay away for good. Having
cut its teeth on Otago, it invited Wellington to come
along, and, winning the toss, went to the sticks
and notched 367. Wellington could only scratch
169, but saved a follow-on. Canterbury’s second
venture added 253, and Wellington faced the hopeless
task of making 453 or going home without the
Shield. The best it could do was 208, so the Shield
is still where Reese and his merry men left it. Can-
terbury’s nose is now very much in the air, and it is
asking the other provinces if there’s any other game
they can play.
* * * *
At Auckland (M.L.) recently the crack
Australian pacer, Denver Huon, was sent over two
miles in hurried pursuit of the M.L. harness record.
Without apparent effort, he beat the previous best,
and put 4min. 28 l-ssec. on the slate as his con-
tribution. The Trotting Club, at whose meeting the
record smashing was featured, showed its joy by
presenting the fast moke’s owner with 100 sovs.
*■ * * *
Announced that the Sydney Olympia
Stadium (Newtown) has secured Tahmmy Burns,
Pelkev. Holland and another, described as a “suit-
able lightweight.” Presumably Tahmmy comes as
manager only, and without any ideas of re-entering
the ring. Where the profit is going to come in is
hard to see. The Olympia is a small place com-
pared to the Rushcutter’s Bay Stadium, and it has
no “circuit.” Considering the sort of maw the average
American boxer has, the managers of the Olympia,
which will hold only about 5000 or 6000 people,
will not stand to make much out of the foreign
stars.
* * * *
R. Spears, the Dubbo (N.S.W.) cyclist,
who some time ago went abroad to seek fame and
fortune, seems to be on the right track. Before
leaving the U.S.A. lie several times overthrew the
Vankee Sneed King, Frank Kramer, who has bossed
the ’Merican tracks for the last 12 years. The
cognoscenti tip that on the first opportunity Spears
will be high un in the race for the world’s cham-
pionship. He has already demonstrated that he is
as fast as the world’s best—and he is a rising star.
* * * *
“Moana”: The par. (B. 22/l/’l4) giving
aviation statistics, dug out by Roger de Paginat, is
decidedly interesting when a little arithmetic is ap-
plied with a view to discovering how many miles have
been flown for every death recorded, in 1908 the
distance per corpse was only 1000 miles; in 1909 it
was 9200 miles; in 1910, 20,700 miles; in 1911,
30,000 miles; in 1912 it had soared to 85,700 miles.
So flying grows safer. The number of miles per
burial is as good a test as you can get.

The Inky Way.
“Jack”: (P. J.) Bourke, of the Golden Mile (W.A.),
has just gone aloft to offer the angels fresh verses
to set to music. He died at Ivalgoorlie, of pneu-
monia. As “Bluebush” his lines have fallen in many
papers, and as “Red Shirt” his caustic prose made a
dab of bright color in the Ivalgoorlie Sun. He was
a Peel River (N.S.W.) boy when he was younger.
“T. 0.1.” : The new Council Chambers of Waverley
(N.S.W.) have been enriched by the portrait Of an
.old-tipie pressman who was the father of municipal
institutions in N. S. Wales —Charles St. Julian, one
of the original members of the Waverley Council
and third Mayor of the borough. Subsequently he
became Mayor of "Marrickvillc, and in the year of his
m.a.Voralt.v, there (1806-7) he compiled and published
“The Municipalities Act of 1867, with Notes, etc.,
and a Draft Code of. Bylaws.” Mr. St. Julian was
born in France in 1819, and 18 years later emigrated
to Adelaide (S.A.), where he wrote for the Aus-
tralian Chronicle and subsequently for the Com-
. micrci'al Journal. In 1843 he joined the staff of
the S. M. Herald, which he left four years later
for the Sydney Chronicle, afterwards known as the
Free Press. In 1849 he rejoined the Herald, and
remained as law reporter until 1872, when he re-
ceived the appointment of Chief Justice of Fiji from
Kjng Thakambau. He had for some time nreviously
been Consul at Sydney for Fiji. He died at Levuka,
• November 26, 1874, just after the islands were
ceded to Britain. The portrait presented to the
Waverley Council is the work of a grand-daughter
of “Sir Charles” (Thakambau knighted him), and
is, the gift of the St. Julian family. St. Julian’s
father-in-law, E. J. Hawkesley, was also distinguished
as a pressman. lie founded the radical paoer, the
People’s Advocate, and was the fearless critic who,
for having denounced a son of Sir Charles Fitzroy
as a card-cheat, suffered fine and imprisonment. Mr.
Hawkeslev’s 1 later years were spent in the Govern-
ment Printing Office; but in the first ’7o’s he took
compensation, and followed his son-in-law to Fiji.
From 1849 to date, a period of 65 years, the S. M.
Herald has had only three law reporters —Charles
St. Julian, who became Chief Justice of. Fiji ; Pem-
broke Latharn .Murray,. who became ’ an. officer of the
Australian ’ Artillery; and Horace Rogers, who has
grown white and shrunk in the service.

“Billy B.”: There are still a few papers where a
man can-spread himself occasionally, but you’ve got
to go far out for them. I dropped on one the
other day published in North Queensland. It con-
tained a report of a. dance at Proserpine, and this is
how my inky brother saw that everybody got a square
deal. “At 10.30 refreshments were handed round,
and the following ladies were to the fore in seeing
that one and all had all they required (here follow
a dozen names). After refreshments dancing was
, resumed _ until 12 o’clock. Mr.-—■ — supplied. the
music in his well-known and highly appreciated
mariner, and Mr. —, a new .arrival, proved himself
the best M.C. it has been our lot to see-in the dis-
trict. Mr. : —— attended to'the boiling of the water
for tea and coffee, and like everything else in connec-
tion \\'ith the -evening . there was nothing halfhearted
about it. Songs were sung by the Bros., which
need no comment, and a stranger, Mr. ——■, also
obliged with a song. At the close Mr. , chair-
man of committee, in a few well-chosen words,
thanked everyone for their attendance and intimated
that it was intended toehold entertainments once l a
month.” Has anybody seen in the city daily papers
for years anything so delightfully refreshing as the
homelv remarks concerning the gent, who boiled the
water ?
Tom Johnson’s successor (pro tem., if not for
good) on the N.S.W. railways tried journalism for a
while after leaving King’s School and Newington
College: his beat on the Inky Way was up at Mudgee.
But the life didn’t grip John Harper, so he passed
into the clerical branch of the Railways.
* * * *
“Looniac”: Apropos Desmond, the poet of the
first little Labor paper that “Billy” Hughes had any-
thing to do with, and to which Holman .was also a
contributor. Desmond and I lived together in Wel-
lington (M.L.). He was the most uncompromising
rebel against “Lawnorder” I ever met,'; and for that
reason was often an embarrassing companion. Walk-
ing down Lambton Quay, night or day, he would
remark suddenly: “I am going to address this
crowd. You assist me.” And straightway he would
damn capital and privilege more emphatically than
any Domain orator, until he was “moved on.” He
would stand in front of some commercial magnate’s
residence, and recount the man’s social imperfections
to a delighted audience. Wellington, was .dis-
cussing the swiftest and simolest method {of ' exe-
cuting him when he migrated to Sydney and!, puriued
his mission there. At the tithe of the rbank crash
he got into trouble for pasting up on Jjhi door of a
financial institution which had so far stood the
strain: “(lone Bung.” As it was a savings bank,
which held the hard-won trifle of the poor, many
people thought it was the wrong place to
start a panic. Another favorite exploit of
this irrepressible rebel was to dress up in rags and
tatters and ostentatiously walk up the aisle of a
fashionable church to the front news, as a protest
against the “Christianity” of the richly-bedecked
congregation. Hughes’s statement that Desmond
“exuded noetrv” hardly expresses it. lie lived
poetry and delivered it ertampo'-c in his harangues.
One piece of his at the time attained a world-wide
fame, travelling through the Old World and America
—“Barr, of the Western Chain.” 1 wonder where
Desmond is now. And .if he is dead,. I ponder who
killed him !
* * * *
• Passed over at Boston (U.S.A.), as tho result, of
a fall in the hunting field, Herman D. Umbstaetter.
founder and for many years editor of the Black
Cat. It was what is known in Amvrka as a fad-
zine, its fad being fiction. Umbstaetter’s idea was
' that there. Was a live demand for a small monthly
that would publish five or six good short stories,
without the usually unnecessary illustrations, and sell
for five cents. So the Black Cat was born, and was
a success from the first meow.
* * * *
“Riggles”: This is the way they run newspapers
in well, it’s in Maoriland. For years past two
strong morning papers have foisted petty four-page
' reprints upon the public as “evening papers.” The
threatened irruption of a competitor scared these
moribund productions into new life and forced them
to secure new talent.' To one of them came a man
full of push and energy, and with his spare brains
streaming behind him. Answering the ’phone one
day, he found that the private sec. of the Minister of
Public Health had an important statement to hand
out about the removal of quarantine restrictions
between the Commonwealth and M.L. “Very sorry,”
replied Energy; “we have no reporters in at the
moment.” “Oh,” said the sec., “but what can you do
about it?” “The only thing I can suggest,”
answered Energy, “is that you ring up the other
paper, give it to them, and ask them to give us a
proof.” Greatly marvelling, the sec. did it. “Right
O,” responded the other paper. “I’ll be over.” He
was> got the item, wandered back to his own office,
and then delivered the proof personally to the
“rival.” “Ah, thanks,” blandly said Energy.
“Thought you people’d be sure to have somebody,
so I asked the Circumlocution Office to ring you up.”

OtHer StudebaKer Models from £285 to £4lO.
Best Motor Car Value ever offered in Australia
from StudebaKer's point of view!
Taking it all round, can you name any car that gives such standard
value for the money? There’s a reason. We could easily sell for a
higher price, but would sooner sell three cars than two. The smaller
margin of profit means a big sales-slieet. Our policy is to provide the
best ONE-PRICE car possible, to ensure large sales and Satisfied
Owners —and we’ve done it.
We do not claim this to be the “BEST CAR IN THE WORLD,
nor do we make any claim or any assertion that we are not prepared to
stand by and PROVE. What we do maintain is that, for value, oui car
has no equal anywhere, certainly not in Australia —and that is. endorsed
on all sides by those who have fully investigated the proposition.
So Studehaker is satisfied.
from the Dealer’s point of view!
All centres have long been taken up by dealers of standing, and years
of experience, who have fully realised the possibilities. That is a strong
point in itself. They realised that, as a selling proposition, we had
to meet the public demand —they advised us what the public demand
was, and how to set about the thing in the best way —and we did it.
In this way both Studebaker and the Dealers are satisfied.
from the Public point of view!
Our whole case stood or fell by the “PUBLIC VERDICT.”
Some months ago we started to advertise. The response was mag-
netic. Inquiries poured in, sales went up by leaps and hounds. Liberal
shipments of cars were sold on arrival. Dealers wired us for supplies
on all sides, and sales are increasing every day.
We presented them with a “one-price Car.” There is but ONE
Price. No more—no less.
In Equipment, Design and Satisfactory Service we presented to the
public more than they had ever been offered before —constituting them the
JUDGE AND JURY. We set out to win the PUBLIC CONFIDENCE
—and we’ve done it.
So Stndebaker, the Dealers, and the PUBLIC are all satisfied.

IN
BANANA LAND
One Uyematsu, agent of the shipping
circle of Nagasaki, discoursed thus to the
Brisbane press last week: —
Japan is over-populated ; Australia is under-pop.u-
lated; yet you do not want u5....1n our shipbuild-
ing yards we do everything. The European officials
once employed have been gradually replaced by Japs.
.... Good-night!
Well, what has the Jap to complain about?
He doesn’t want Europeans in Nippon, and
the Australian doesn’t want Japs in Aus-
tralia ; and each takes steps to carry his
wishes into effect.
“Caber” : Of the 43 members who sat in
the Queensland Assembly in 1877, whep(
George Fox, just dead, was first elected,'
only four remain—Sam Griffith. Peter
McLean, the evergreen Charlie Buzacott,
and the very ancient Peter Fitzalan McDon-
ald.
“Johnny” Woolcock, Queensland Parlia-
mentary draughtsman, is a good specimen of
the self-manufactured Australian. He first
came into prominence at an early age, by
delivering milk to the nobility and gentry of
Kelvin Grove, a Brisbane suburb, where his
family ran a dairy. Subsequently a Gram-
mar School scholarship set him on the road
which led through the University to the
Queensland Bar, of which he is now one of
the leaders. Some years ago he collaborated
with one Pain in a consolidation of the
Queensland statutes, and he lias published
other light literature of a similar class.
“P.F." : Some weeks ago Tiie Bulletin,
talking about clerical alderman, said that
the public-spirited Father Minnagh, of Bun-
daberg, could be the next mayor of that town
it he. liked. A few days ago, at the municipal
elections, his Reverence topped the poll.
Here’s a health to you, Father O’Flynn!”
Surgeon Thomas Fitzgerald, of Melbourne,
delivered scores of true verdicts in his time,
and another of them after manv years has
now come true. When Dr. Murray, R.C.
Bishop of Cooktown, was at Echuca (Vic.)
18 years ago, he damaged his knee in a fall
from a horse. “Fitz” fixed him up
and the patient looked and felt as
well as ever. “But,” said Fitz, “if
you. live till you turn 60, that knee
is likely to go again for want of native oil.”
Dr. Murray is now 68 and he goes about on
crutches. He has applied for and is expect-
ing to receive a coadjutor at an early date.
QUID PRO QUO
SINGER: “ Have you the 'Owl’ in C? ”
NEW HAND: “The ‘Owlin Sea' sir? No sir; but we ’ave ‘Asleep in the. Deep ’ if that
would do, sir."
Another mystery in municipal valuations
has been dug up in Brisbane. Ten years ago
the city was valued at £5,538,000. To-day,
in spite of borrowing and booming, the rate-
payers are asked to pay taxes on only
£5,232,000, or £300,000 less.
“G. Up”: Excepting me, Queensland’s
learned Governor is the poorest public
speaker that ever mumbled the language. I
lately squeezed into a Downs agricultural
gathering to hear his Ex. discourse on the
exhibits. “What’s he like?” I asked of a
heavily-bearded farmer whose broad back
blocked further progress. “Like!” he
growled, turning round. “Like! Like a
horse chewin’ pumpkin !”
“Boro Belle”: Mackay, which was to have
been ruined through the withdrawal of
Tanna from the sugar industry (vide Tory
press forecasts seven years ago), is witness-
ing a boom in motor-cars, no less than 30
new ones having made thejr appearance on
the streets during the past 12 months, while
fully 40 now honk through the district.
Even a number of poor, down-trodden
cockies own cars, while half a dozen others
have invested in motor-tractor engines to
do their own and neighbors’ ploughing,
water-raising, chaffcutting and wood-saw-
ing. This they find a Profitable sideline.
Another sign of ruin is seen on the sand-
fringed edge of the town, where a big harbor
scheme, embracing the construction of a
three-mile ferro-concrete viaduct to carry a
railway, across to Flat Top anchorage, is in
full swing. Over 100 men are already on
the job. The works will take five years to
complete, and will cost a quarter of a mil-
lion. So the results of the expensive white
labor fad are surely coming home to roost.
As headmaster of Brisbane Grammar
School, “Regy” Roe, now Queensland's
Inspector-General of Education, acquired a
nickname through his anatomical peculiari-
ties. That, however, never prevented Past
and Present swearing by him. Though of a
most unmilitary figure, he always led his
School cadets' to camp. As a soldier he
achieved fame by telling the late Colonel
“Tom” Price what he thought of him—a
thing very few people did, though many felt
like it. It was at Lytton camp, and, after
some rqanceuvres, the officers were gathered
about “Tom,” who was then commandant.
After “Tom” had given his opinion of things
in the style which made him famous, he
asked if anyone had any blankety sugges-
tion to make. Then it was that “Regy” had
a suggestion. He suggested that his
superior’s language was horrible.
Bananaland has been taking itself very
seriously in educational matters lately. In
the last four years it has established a Uni-
versity, half a dozen high schools, and a
teachers’ training college; it has raised the
school age from 12 to 14, introduced medical
and dental inspection of schools, and formed
a staff of itinerant teachers. Half a dozen
years ago it was . down in the trough; now
it holds itself up for comparison with the
rest of Australia. Its bills have advanced
in proportion to its progress, and this year’s
grant per head of population is the heaviest
in the Commonwealth. But it has the satis-
faction of claiming the smallest proportion
of un-scliooled children in the Commonwealth
in spite of the vast distances between some
of it's humpies.
Jenkinson, the new mayor of Brisbane, is
master-printer. For years he represented
Fassifern in the Legislative Assembly, but
was outed by that Wienholt who later tried
to bump Andrew Fisher in Wide Bay and
took the count himself. He has attacked
the fossildom of the Brisbane Council dur-
ing his period as alderman, but the stirring
of those dreadfully dry bones will call for a
louder trump than “Jenky’s.” His brother
ill the South Brisbane rabbit-feathers is
“Joe” Allen, fruit auctioneer, whose chief
claim to distinction is that he is the Wow-
sensh chairman of the Board of Cricket
Control, cricket worship being “Joe’s” great
redeeming vice.
Derelict.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The old ship rots and reeks of perished tar,
And rust, and many strange and stagnant
things ;
She hears the free seas falling on the bar
• And sees the answer of a yacht’s white
wings
But derelict, she shall not pass again
To those green wonders of the purple main.
Yet sometimes in a sunny hour she dreams
Ashore, where jungle courts the shell-
pricked reach.
Afloat once more before old trades she seems,
Or in some reed-walled creek Swings to the
beach,
While, where the vine across the cave mouth
lies,
Glow the twin lamps of lurking golden eyes.
Scarlet hibiscus and gay orchid shapes
Flash like a note of passion through the
trees,
And pal-m crests scrawl their runes above the
capes f
As though to write a legend of the seas,
And, brown and amber, to her shining sides
Stray cocoanuts are bobbing with the tides.
And one slim native girl with little feet,
Smelling of groves of orange trees in
bloom,
Threads through the tale a laughter low and
sweet,
And sudden heart-throbs pulsing in the
gloom,
To stealthy tryst the barefoot seamen move
While lifts a yellow moon to shame their love.
Faint musk-breaths drift like kisses on the
_ night,
A bird forgets the dark and chants of day,
On coral tips the breaker curls are white,
And phosphorescence streaks the livid bay,
A shark’s pale belly in the shallows gleams,
And brave she rides —a derelict of dreams !
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, February 7, 1914.
My Dear Tiiargomindah,—
The Chiel and Lady Macgregor are doing
a round of shows in the Downs, what time
the \ ision and the Paget child sojourn with
the Whites, of Pikedale. Goodness knows
what George and Mary would do without an
energetic plenipotentiary here. How the
shows and other things would be opened
passes ray comprehension.
The new mayoresses of Brisbane and Bris-
bane South are Mrs. Jenkinson and Mrs.
Allen respectively. The former was a Miss
Ferguson, daughter of the late Lieutenant-
Colonel Ferguson, of Gympie. She is the
mother of five sturdy young Australians.
Mrs. Allen has spent 32 years in Banana-
land’s capital since she was born in Devon-
shire.
The National, Agricultural and Industrial
Association has appointed J. Bain, at present
secretary of the Bathurst Society, as its new
secretary. He is a Brisbane man, and, like
all Brisbane men, is full of energy and
hungering after hard work and lots of it.
The John Hicks entertained a party of
youth and more or less beauty aboard their
motor-launch the other day, in honor of the
two Hicks, daughters, Beatrice and Gladys,
who are off to Melbourne next week.
Mr. and Mrs. William Thorne had a
houseful of visitors to-day, to bid them good-
bye before they sailed for England. The
ceremony was conducted without a hitch.
Mrs. Malcolm Cameron, wife of the well-
known medico, departs for Canada next
week, and yesterday Mrs. Goddard handed!
afternoon tea to some 20 females in celebra-
tion thereof. Son Dudley will go along
with his mother, so that Canadian relatives;
may see what a young Northern Australian t
really looks like after struggling with the •
climate.
No less than six members of the Wimmen’s >
Club are flitting overseas this month. The
survivors will cheer them up next Thursday,.
with buns and the hot brown liquid sub-
stance that Dr. Dictionary Johnson drank
out of a “dish.”
Captain and Mrs. Svensen left by tie-
Mataram last Monday for the North, after,-
which they will take a short cut to Norway
through Siberia. They want to get to Nor-
way in time to assist that country to aele-
brate its separation from Sweden. Tbe-
Svensens live on the river-bank at East
Brisbane. Occasionally they break the ■
monotony with bacon and eggs, but the river- -
bank is an established habit.
The Teronga Bowling Club fed a quantity
of females on the green last Saturday..
President Denny Day Was ill, so, in his ab-
sence, bacteriological Pound grasped the •
hands of all the guests, and passed them on r
to Mrs. and Miss Day. John Reid, president ;
of the Q.8.A., made a few remarks, after
which the guests were recompensed with re-
freshments.
Last Saturday the Hospital Saturday/
Association massed itself on board the;
Lucinda and went exploring down the river
with its life in its hand. The most tiresome
feature of the affair was that the Lucinda
still berths, at its old objectionable resting
place, and is reached by a dust-strewn route
festooned with garbage vans. Any allegedly
clean country in the world would obliterate
either the Lucinda or the rubbish carts, and
plant a few trees on the river frontage. But
we can’t have everything. We already have
the Ena and the Heatherington and a pro-
spective motor-car for aldermen, so no one
can say we are not progressive.
Dulcie Peterson, an attractive girl who
sings and elocutes especially well, has hung
out her sign as teacher of the art. She
studied in your harbor settlement under
Frank Down and Harry Leston, and is one
of the few who do not consider it necessary
to punctuate their recitations with screams.
Tours affectionately
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Bananaland’s Minister for Agriculture
White, who some time back bewailed that
Queensland was not well adapted for agri-
culture, took advantage of the vacation to
accept a pressing invitation to inspect the
Central district. That his visit was profit-
able was evident from the astonishment with
which he viewed acres and acres of Rhodes
grass, lucerne, maize, cane and wheat, which
had taken the place of heavy scrub. One
farm showed a paddock of 15 acres of
Rhodes grass, which had supported 45 head
of cattle through the six driest months of
last year, and which when the rain came
had to be still more heavily stocked to keep
the grass down. Another showed lucerne
that had been five years in the ground, and
was at the time of the visit carrying a four-
weeks’ crop over 2ft. high. When White'
returned to the town in the corner of the-
State which pretends to know all about the'
half-million square miles which it likewise'
pretends to govern, he sat down to seriously
consider whether it wouldn’t be as well for
the Minister for Agriculture to spend a few
days in the country now and then; or if he
didn’t he should have done so.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
Ferdie: Smartly written ; but too small a
local disturbance to write about.... B1I: Don’t be
afraid. We never say anything unpleasant in this
column. When we want to otter a would-be contri-
butor real discouragement, we drop him a note
written in blood with an axe... .Anxious: Sorry we
have no information about that faraway Pani Ilota
show... .A.M.M.: Glad to hear from you if you
will tell us something that every other person who
has rushed into the Great Fog hasn’t tried to tell
us already. .. .T.D.J.: It’s Sunday-afternoon, and
company-manners name is iguana. Other times it’s
gohanna or just g 0... . Inquirer: Apparently it is an
unregistered partnership ; and the partners may make
what rules they like for the conduct of their busi-
ness.... D. Day: “Margarey Daw” appeared in
“Under a Fool’s Cap,” by Daniel H. Holmes, re-
printed by Mosher, Portland, Maine, U.S.A The
Pigeon: Full particulars of Hodder and Stoughton’s
prize novel competition, closing March 31, 1914,
appeared in lied Page, March 27, 1913 —one condition
being that MS. must be accompanied by a filled-in
printed form, to be obtained from H. and S
Brookfield: Your vague suspicion that Quinn wrote
“The Camp Within the West” is correct. Get “The
Hidden Tide,” from any bookseller. .. .The Native:
“Eaglehawk and Crow,” by G. Mathew, contains a
largie number of abo. words with their meanings.
But why not be original and name your villa “The
Laurels” ? Also, “Hazel Dell” hasn’t been used
before... .Lantern: Ask Angus and Robertson, Syd-
ney. .. .D.F.G.: Maybe you’re right, but we haven’t
seen it before. There are very few sub-editors' who
have seen everything that has been printed....
F.J.E.G.: Don’t see that it throws any new light on
the question.... Faux Pas: Can’t see your point....
Red Gum: There should be a paragraph in it, but
you haven’t made it clear. What do you mean when
you say that “the contractor knocks off 7d. for the
use of his name” ? Who does he knock it off, and
what is his name (Smith, perhaps), and what kind
of a riot does he have on 7d. ?. .. . Domremi: The
fact that the freehold land of the Territory and the
areas held on long lease are mostly in the North is
a good reason why the great Federal property should
be opened from the South, by extending the Port
Augusta-Oodnadatta railway northward over the cool
table-land and starting settlement at the Alice Springs
end. But even the great estates can be broken up for
closer settlement purposes when the country is made
accessible —if any government really wants to break
them up. There are such things as graduated land-
tax and compulsory resumption. .. .Doubter: It is a
pity that the Abo-scribes don’t get together and
agree to tell the same yarn. The sturdy bushwoman,
and the overflowing bush hospitality, and the bush
helpfulness (as evidenced by the occasion when Rain-
bow was in the Yard), and the kindly bush doctor
and nurse —we have heard of them all. And we
really believe in them. Now you want an increased
baby bonus on the ground that the doctor is a
hunks and the nurse a cormorant. And you say
that the mother, when she goes to the nearest town-
ship to be confined, has to pay heavily for someone
to mind the children because the neighbor is a
harridan who will do nothing without payment.
Also the sturdy mother herself —wife of the cocky
who has got rich by “poling” on the swagman—is
so weak. from chronic hunger that she isn’t fit for
maternity. These things are hard to sort 0ut....
Jugend: Try a less religious print. Possibly some
trade journal like the Plumbers and Gasfitters’
Gazette might like it... .E.F.S.: You approach
Nature as if she were Jane Smith. .. .Johnnie H.
(V.) : Well, you get a good grip on the subject, but
you don’t score a fall. .. .L.J.V.: River song dies
away to a trickle. ... G.B. (W.A.): Both outed. .. .
Jack P. (M.L.): Nothing. ... Phoebe T. E. (M.L):
Regret unsuitable. ... Stewart C. R.: Gome in and
sit down: —•
AUSTRALIA’S HOME.
Australia is a friendly mate
Some day its fleet may be very great,
At the Harbor entrance she stands
Like Horatious at the gate,
She fights for love of country,
To uphold her wealth and fame
Like the good old dogs of Devon have
Made old England’s name,
Each boy must have a certain
night,
To learn for his counUv how to
tight,
.If e’er a foe dared our home to plunder,
They’d get the lead as strong as
thunder.

JANE’S BEAU.
Jane had a beau,
You know!
Oh yes, she had a beau !
And this beau Jane had made an awful blunder;
But taking into consideration the way Jane led him
on, it wasn’t much wonder! (Stop here for
refreshments !)
So—
You know,
Jane hasn’t got a beau!
Oh, no !
No more Beau for Jane!
Certainly not 1
It’(isn’t safe!

. . . .Printer: The Sal. Army “home” at Brisbane was
filled with women who were supposed to have been
“rescued” from the flames of perdition. And three
of them were burned because the Army didn’t know
enough about the job of rescuing People from flames
to supply the necessary apparatus. We have only
the Army’s word for the perdition and the suffici-
ency of its holy appliances, but if the arrangements
we can’t see are judged by those we can see, it is
an inefficient old show, and snuffles under false
pretences.
TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC)
CORRESPONDENTS.
Dave H.: Drawings are painful to witness and the
gags are old. . . .L.G.P.: Crude, hard work. You
need training. Humor rather conventional....
W.E.G.: What you can’t draw you leave out or
slur. The result is eminently unsatisfactory from
an art point of view W.B. (M.L): Miniature
hairline stuff —the unsaleable product of the untrained
amateur experimenter. Of course, you didn’t really
expect those jokes to be used —we refer particularly
to the St. Peter item, and the one attached to the
drawing of the distorted latlies swimming. .. .J.A.
fV.): Traces of humor about the vice-regal picture,
though the potentate in the cocked hat is outrageous.
The coroneted lady yawning politely is the best of
it, though even she has a slight hump oh her dis-
tinguished back. The drunk is hopeless from all
angles. Buy some tuition... .W.R.S.: Young man,
something heavy will fall on you if you send us
any more such atrocities!.... Eric D.: You are
young, and don’t know how to go about things....
JM. (at least it looks more like “J.M.” than any-
thing else) : You have no sense of humor, you can’t
draw, and you can't sign your name legibly. Can
you chop wood, or clean windows, or howl for theatre
cabs? If you can do any of these things really
well, you had better give up Art.

From George GeorgesOn, managing director Of
Anderson’s Tanneries, Ltd; (Sydney) : Referring to
“J.D.S.’s” letter (B. 8/l/’l4), our tannery manager,
Mr. R. J. Carter, after many years experience in
England, as well as itt hot and damp climates in
different parts of the World, has thoroughly mast-
ered the drying and other problems associated with
the turning out of highest quality leather in Aus-
tralia ; and whatever may be the results obtained
elsewhere in this country; the leather being manu-
factured by us at Botany is not only free from the
Weaknesses mentioned by “J.D S.,” but is in every
way equal to the best English, ample evidence of
which we shall be happy to have the opportunity of
giving “j.D.S.” And when we have furnished it we
shall be pleased to have his frank opinion as to
whether he has not found that Australia Can, and
is, turning out leather quite equal in color and in
every other Way to the best English. Then, like an
honorable man; we believe he will withdraw the
statement that “Australian leather is hot, and never
has been, equal to the best English leather.”
“Premium”: Sydney’s fire record for January was
275 alarms, mostly serious, the month finishing with
a huge conflagration at Snow’s fluff emporium. The
last few months have seen the brigades Working
bvertime, and the insurance CoS; are badly hit. The
underwriters make tio hostile insinuations against
anybody; but they instance as a remarkable fact
that the end of every boom is indicated, the world
Over, by a tremendous rate Of fire loss. In New
York the first intimation a few years back that
the boom period was finished was the unaccountable
increase of fires. The insurance people have already
summoned a meeting to discuss the whole position.
One idea is to make compulsory the use of auto-
matic fire-sprinklers and detector devices, a scheme
that finds much especial favor among the agents
who import these various contraptions.
“Hamer” : .An interesting point as to the right of
the finder of lost property to the advertised reward
was discussed at the Manly (N.S.W.) police court the
other day. A lady lost a diamond pendant, for which
she advertised a reward of £20. The finder, who
evidently knew the way of people who advertise in
this large and affluent fashion, took a friend along
with him to help him claim the subsidy. The owner
urged him to take part of the £20 in cash, and
accept an IOU for the balance; but he wanted the
lot in hard money. Experiencing a difficulty in col-
lecting it, he issued a summons. The owner con-
tended that the finder had acted illegally in not
handing the treasure to the police, a finder having
no right to retain such property ; but the Law held
that there was no wrong intention, and that the
advertisement amounted to a contract, which the
owner must fulfil.
* * * #
“Observer” : The Sugar Octopus is getting in some
work in the Mackay district (Q.). The old cane-
purchasing agreements between the co. and its
growers having expired, and the Sydney conference
having proved abortive, the co. has issued a circular
intimating the substitution of an “amended” agree-
ment for the ensuing three years. The farmers
are now spending many weary hours in chewing over
the amazing document. Its most outstanding fea-
ture is the deletion of what was known as the “B”
scheme, which fixed a base price for cane of a cer-
tain standard, with an increase in the price for
every decrease in the quantity required to make a
ton of sugar. This scheme was in operation
throughout the Mackay district last season. The
co. proposes to substitute a quality test, and. has
the calm effrontery to admit that the object is to
debar growers from benefiting by any improvement
which may be effected in the process of manufacture.
The circular goes on to state a case, pointing out
that a projected improvement that would have re-
sulted in an increase in profits had been abandoned
by the dog-in-the-manger co., because the farmers
stood to gain a trifle, without having contributed to
the capital outlay. If the company, having purchased
some improved process from the inventor at a very
moderate price, was willing to let a share of the
profits go to the cane-grower, the said grower would
be encouraged to grow more, and that would mean
more business and more profit for the octopus. But
when a concern gets so big in the stomach that it
hasn’t been able to see its own feet for 10 years, it
naturally doesn’t know where it is going.
* * * *
“Copper”: When is the Australian Government
going to get busy on the long-promised nickel coin-
age? The vast copper token the size of a sunset
which at present afflicts the community is an ana-
chronistic curse. Besides rendering it dangerous
for anyone with change for a shilling on him to
fall into the water, it has a fiendish habit of feeling
like half a crown in the pocket, and engendering a
spirit of reckless irresponsibility, only at the critical
moment, to confound its deluded possessor when he
produces it. Even potentates like Charles II. and
Bill of Orange had enough common-sense to issue
small pennies, and it’s up to George V. or Joe
MCGCLLXXVIII, to go and do likewise.

I THINK YOUR REMEDY A FINE ONE
Writes MISS MURIEL STARR.
This Brilliant Actress says she will never again travel
without a bottle of Dr. Sheldon s Now Discovery.
Miss Muriel Starr’s appearance in the
American drama “Within the Law” is
one of the sensational successes of the
Australian stage. She has had a wonder-
ful career of star-r6les in America. Her
Mary Turner in “Within the Law” has
afforded Australians merely an idea of the
work she is capable of doing. This re-
markable writes to the Sheldon
Drug Co., Ltd., as follows: —
SHELDON DRUG CO., LTD., Sydney.
Dear Sirs, —
I have been using your Dr. Shel-
don’s New Discovery for the past
week, and, although I have never
given a testimonial for anything before
in all my life, I feel that it is my duty
now to write and tell you what a splendid
Remedy I think Dr. Sheldon’s New Dis-
covery is. , , ..
I had such a nasty little cold, and it
bothered me so much and affected my voice

The Sentimental Bloke.
THE STROR 'AT COOT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
An, wimmin! Wot a blinded fool I’ve
I arete meself, wot else could I

The dreamt I dreamed, the dilly thorts I
Is up the U pole, an’ joy ’as done a bunk.
Wimmin! 0 strike! I orter known the
Their 11 tricks is crook, their arts is all
The ole world over tarts is all the same :
All soft an’ smilin wif no eait inside.
But she fair doped me wif er winnm ways,
Then eroded me pitch fer all me mortal days.
They’re all the same! A man ’as got to be
Stric’ master if ’e wants to snare em
sure. . , , ,
’E ’as to take a stand an let em see
That triflin’ is a thing e won t mdure.
’E wants to show ’em that e olds command,
So they will smooge an’ feed out ot is ana.
’E needs to make ’em feel ’e is the boss,
An’ keep the upper ’and in ev ry tilt.
’E ’as to make ’em think ell feel no loss
To part wif any tart ’e s trackin wit.
That all their pretty ways is crook pretense
Is plain to any bloke wif common sense.
But when the birds is nestin in the spring,
An’ when the soft green leaves is in the
bud, _ _
’E drops ’is bundle to some fluffy thing,
’E pays ’er ’omage —an’ ’is name is Mud.
She plays wif ’im an’ kids ’ini on a treat,
Until she ’as ’im crawlin’ at ’er feet.
An’ then, when ’e’s fair orf ’is top wif love,
When she ’as got ’im good an’ ’ad ’er fun,
She slings ? im over like a carst-orf glove,
To let the other tarts see wot she’s done.
All vanity, deceit an’ ’eartless kid .
I orter known; an’, spare me days, I did.
I knoo. But when I looked into ’er eyes—
Them shinin’ eyes o’ blue all soft wit
love —
Wif mimic love —they seemed to lpnertize.
I wus content to place ’er ’igh above,
I wus content to make of ’er a queen ;
An’ so she seemed them days....O, strutli
... .Doreen !
I knoo. But when I stroked ’er glossy ’air
Wif rev’rint ’ands, ’er cheek pressed close
to mine,
Me lonely life seemed robbed of all its care;
I dreams me dreams, an’ ’ope begun to
shine.
An’ when she ’eld ’er lips fer me to kiss. . . .
Ar, wot’s the use? I’m done wif all o’ this!
Wimmin! Oh, I ain’t jealous! Spare me
days!
Me? Jealous of a knock-kneed coot like
that! ,
Tm ! Wif ’is cute stror ’at an’ pretty ways!
I’d be a mug to squeal or whip the cat.
I’m glad, I am — glad ’cos I know I’m free!
There ain’t no call to tork o’ jealousy.
I tells meself I’m well out o’ the game ; ;
Fer look, I mighter married ’er —an
then....
Ah strike ! ’Er voice wus music when my
name .
Wus on ’er lips on them glad ev nm s
when
We useter meet. An’ then to think shed
g 0....
No, I ain’t jealous—but—Ar, I dunno !
I took a derry on this stror-’at coot
First time I seen ’im dodgin’ ’round
Doreen.
Tm, wif ’is giddy tie an’ Yankee soot,
Ferever yappin’ like a tork-machine.
About “The Iloffis” where ’e ’ad a grip....
The way ’e smiled at ’er give me the pip !
She ses I stoushed ’im, when I promised fair
To chuck it, even to a friendly spar.
Stoushed ’im! I never roughed ’is pretty
’air!
I only spanked ’im gentle, fer ’is mar.
If I’d ’a’ jabbed ’im once, there would ’a’
been
An inquest; an’ I ses so to Doreen.
I mighter took an’ cracked ’im in the street,
When she wus wif ’im there lars’ Frid’y
night.
But don't I keep me temper when we meet?
An’ don’t I raise me lid an’ act perlite?
I only jerks me elbow in ’is ribs
To give the gentle office to ’is nibs.
Stoushc-d ’im! I owns I met ’im on the
quite,
An’ worded ’im about a small affair;
An’ when ’e won’t put up ’is ’ands to fight
(’E ses, “Fer public brawls ’e didn’t
care”) —
I lays ’im ’cross me knee, the mother’s joy,
An’ smacks ’im ’earty, like a naughty boy.
An’ now Doreen she ses I’ve broke me vow,
An’ mags about this coot’s poor, “wounded
pride.”
An’ then, o’ course, we ’as a ding-dong row,
Wif ’ot an’ stormy words on either side.
She ses I done it outer jealousy.
An’ so, we parts fer ever —’er an’ me.
Me jealous! Jealous of that cross-eyed
cow!
I set ’im ’cos I couldn’t sight ’is face.
’ls yappin’ fair got on my nerves, some-ow.
I couldn’t stand ’im ’angin’ round ’er
place.
A coot like that! But it don’t matter
much.
She’s welkim to ’im if she fancies such.
I swear I’ll never track wif ’er no more;
I’ll never look on ’er side o’ the street —
Unless she comes an’ begs me pardin’ for
Them things she said to me in angry ’eat.
She can’t ixpeck fer me to smooge an’
crawl.
I ain’t at any woman’s beck an’ call.
Wimmin! I’ve took a tumble to their game.
I’ve got the ’ole bang tribe o’ diners set!
The ’ole world over they are all the same:
Crook to the core the bunch of ’em —an’
yet
We could ’a’ been that ’appy, ’er an’ me ... •
But wot’s it matter? Ain t I glad I m free.
A bloke wif commin-sense ’as got to own
There’s little ’appiness in married life.
The smoogin’ game is better left alone,
Fer tarts is few that make the ideel wife.
An’ them’s the sort that loves wifout dis-
guise, . ■ , , ~
An’ thinks the sun shines in their usband s
eyes.
But when the birds is matin’ in the spring,
An’ when the tender leaves begin to bud,
A feelin’ comes—a dilly sorter thing—
That seems to sorter swamp 1m like a
flood. ,
An’ when the fever ’ere inside 1m burns, ,
Then freedom ain’t the thing fer wot e
yearns.
But I ’ave chucked it all. An’ yet--I own
I dreams me dreams when soft Spiing
breezes stirs;
An’ often, when I’m moonin ere alone,
A lispin’ maid, wif ’air an’ eyes like ers,
’Oo calls me “dad,” she climbs upon me
An’ yaps ’er pretty baby torli to me.
I sorter see a little ’ouse, it seems,
Wif Someone waitin’ for me at tne
Ar, where’s "the sense in dreamin’ barmy
dreams ?
I’ve dreamed before, an’ nearly woke too
late. , .
Sich ’appiness could never last fer j ol i g ’ ,
We’re strangers —’less she owns that she
was wrong.
To call ’er back I’ll never lift a ’and ;
She’ll never ’ear frum me by word or sign.
P’raps, some day. she’ll come to understand
The mess she’s made o’ this ere life o

Dinnigan’s Dice-box.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THIS tale with the alliterative title has
to do with a certain fleshy publican
named Dinnigan; a good-natured
sinner named Scobie; a ruby-red mare
known as Flash Jenny, the property of
Dinnigan ; and a well-used dice-box with a
rough exterior, also portion of the publican s
worldly goods. . , , , ~0
This ungodly conglomeration had tor its
headquarters the Here-At-Last Inn, a cer-
tain payable shanty situated upon the
Murrumbidgee at a point where four roads,
reaching far away into the very farthest
back, met and discharged a continuous cargo
of heat-warped, thirsting souls. At the
ever-open door they would meet the readily
outstretched hand of Dinnigan, the landlord
and owner, and later on his just as readily
outstretched boot if they outlasted their
cash. All kinds of men came off the hot,
red road to breast the deal bar of the Here-
At-Last. There were decent men, perma-
nent hands at the stations, who shouted a
few times and rode away; hard cases, who
had been kicked out by Dinnigan, after he
had gathered in all but the things they stood
in, on an average once a year for the last
15 years; travellers of all kinds from beyond
the wide mirage-covered plains; restless
souls who tramped everlastingly through the
hazy heat towards an eternally indefinite
destination; sharps and spielers, who came
to prey upon the unwary; and lastly Scobie,
that modern Robin Hood of Farthest Back,
who came to prey in turn upon the spieler
push as the tiger preys upon the jackal,
with a contemptuous chuckle for his victim
but a bit of keen advice and a pound or so
for the bitten lamb. Scobie spieled only
upon his brother spieler.
Dinnigan’s mare was an extremely pre-
possessing piece of goods, and knew it. She
came of a famous trotting breed, and her
pace and style suggested great possibilities
to the active mind of Scobie. Ihe animal
had proved a gold mine till she became so
well known that she could no longer be
exploited locally, and as Dinnigan was a
fixture the animal was therefore now ot no
real value to him. Scobie wanted her, and
Dinnigan wanted to sell her; but the pub-
lican’s price was high, and Scobie s was
practically nothing. The fact that Scobie
wanted the steed was a source ot no little
anxiety to the owner. Scobie had a way ot
getting things he wanted at the price he
wanted to give, and Dinnigan found the pio-
cess of keeping one eye on the mare and the
other on Scobie very wearying. To end the
suspense he lowered the price of the mare
and offered her to the genial scamp; but the
latter winked with the reassuring remark
that he intended to get _ her for miles less
than that figure. Dinnigan put an extra
lock on the stable door and made the stable-
hand sleep in the harness-room, which ad-
joined the mare’s loose-box, and lost torn
hours’ sleep a night worrying over the mat-
Tliere was but one other building at the
Cross-Roads besides the shanty, and it was
occupied by a harness-maker _ named Nick-
son, who made a very fair living by making
and repairing harness and saddlery tor the
stations and travelling teams. Nickson was
a quiet sort of a fellow, with a keen regard
for Scobie, who had in the dim past rescued
him from the clutches of the fraternity and
set him up in the rough iron liut he called
his shop. In fact, Scobie was sleeping pait
ner in the business. When things were
quiet at the Here-At-Last he was wont to
stroll across to the harness-mender s tor a
yarn, and the saddler would retail the latest
gossip from the stations —which often con-
tained information of great professional in-
terest to the bush freebooter. # It was on the
occasion of just such a visit that fccohie,
dreamily taking his pipe from his mouth,
made a proposal to the man of leather and
wax-end.

►.cobie, nonchalantly chewing a straw, and
with face peacefully immobile, stepped for-
ward and threw down his tickets. The crowd
pressed around, Nickson, standing on a box
outside, glared anxiously through the top
iail of the window. Scobie rattled the
dice, shoved his left hand in his pocket,
flipped the straw to the opposite corner of
nis mouth with his tongue and threw—
three. The crowd gasped with relief, while
Nickson skipped off the box with a vocifer-
ous curse, spitefully punched his leg, swore
again, and remounted the box to glare at
the next throw. Scobie imperturbably re-
placed the dice and threw again—-another
three. The gang began to smile its relief,
and the saddler jumped off the box in a
frenzy of rage, landing on a boundary-rider’s
uog, and thereby immediately becoming im-
broiled in a controversy that nearly pre-
vented him remounting his box in time for
the next throw. Sco.bie threw seven, and
never turned a hair. His fourth throw
showed a total of 12, and Wolfie and co.
simmered good humor what time Nicky’s
face at the window grew white. And then
all craned forward for the last throw. Im-
perceptibly, Scobie turned the box in his
hand till the seamy side was nearest him.
There was perfect silence in the room,
through which.the impatient pawing of the
ruby-red mare sounded like the beating of a
drum. The dice rattled in the box; the
crowd held its breath; slowly he tilted the
box, and then
“Stand back, boys,” murmured Scobie.
1 am t hardly got room to throw.”
The overwrought Dinnigan turned like a
bull on the audience. “For th’ sake ov God,
get back ahnd give th’ malm his chance,” he
bellowed, and the gang moved with scarce
a sound. Scobie again rattled the box,
flipped the straw to the other side of his
mouth, shoved his left hand into his trousers-
pocket, and threw.
There was a yell of blasphemy from the
watching gang, and Nickson fell off his box.
Hitter, hawkish faces hurled recrimination
at , the „ careless figure that smiled good-
naturedly at them through the execration,
bellowing his imprecations, the landlord
hurled the box and bones into the locker, and
slammed and locked the door; whilst out-
side the travellers, with whom Scobie was
a general favorite, applauded, assisted bv a
swarm of dogs.
“Boys,” said Scobie, genially, “I’m leaving
straight away for a little drive, and I won’t
be back for a month or so f but before I go
have one with me.”
He had thrown 18.

huge bulk darkened his doorway, and he
looked up and beheld the landlord of the
Here-At-Last glowering grimly upon him.
